📖 Derek's Pronunciation Bank

Verified against dictionary sources | Continuously updated as new words are added
💡 How to use: For each word — say the stress pattern aloud 5 times, then read both example sentences aloud, emphasizing the underlined syllable. Send Claude the next batch of words anytime to keep growing this bank.

🆕 Latest Batch — Set 18 (sequential / ambient)

📌 Newest words always appear here first. Scroll down to review older entries.
WORD
SEQUENTIAL
si-KWEN-shul 3 syllables
British (RP): /sɪˈkwenʃəl/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low — peak on "KWEN" (short /e/). "Si-" reduces to schwa. "-tial" = "-shul" (always reduces — never say each letter separately).
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "KWEN". The "-tial" ending always reduces to "-shul" — never "shee-ul" or "tee-al". Following a particular order or sequence — one step logically following from the previous. Used in clinical protocols treatment plans and OET writing to describe structured, ordered care.
Example 1 — clinical protocol
The rehabilitation programme follows a sequential structure — strength first, then mobility, then return to function.
Example 2 — OET writing
A sequential approach was taken: the patient was first stabilised on oral antibiotics before IV therapy was commenced.
📌 Same "-tial" = "-shul" ending:
sequential → si-KWEN-shul  |  initial → i-NISH-ul  |  essential → i-SEN-shul  |  confidential → kon-fi-DEN-shul
WORD
AMBIENT
AM-bee-unt 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈæmbiənt/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low-low — peak on "AM" (short /æ/, same as "lamp"). Falls through "-bee-unt". The "-ient" ending = "-ee-unt" (two light syllables, not one).
⚠️ Stress on 1st syllable "AM". Three syllables — don't collapse to "AM-bunt". Relating to the immediate surrounding environment — especially temperature, light, or sound. Clinically relevant for medication storage (ambient temperature), patient comfort (ambient noise), and infection control (ambient air pressure in isolation rooms).
Example 1 — medication storage
The medication must be stored at ambient temperature — between 15 and 25 degrees Celsius — away from direct sunlight.
Example 2 — clinical environment
Negative pressure isolation rooms maintain lower ambient air pressure than the corridor to prevent airborne pathogens from escaping.
📌 Common clinical "ambient" phrases:
ambient temperature = room temperature / surrounding temp  |  ambient noise = background sound level  |  ambient light = surrounding lighting conditions

📚 Previous Batch — Set 17 (similar / neonatal / pathogen / profuse / diaphoresis / aztreonam / bluntly)

📌 Newest words always appear here first. Scroll down to review older entries.
WORD
SIMILAR
SIM-i-luh 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈsɪmɪlə/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low-low — peak on "SIM" (short /ɪ/). British RP non-rhotic: "-lar" = "-luh" (no r sound). Three syllables, not two — don't collapse it to "SIM-luh".
⚠️ British RP "-lar" = "-luh" (schwa, no r). Resembling something else but not identical — a crucial clinical qualifier. "Similar to" signals a differential is still open; "consistent with" is stronger and more diagnostic. Don't use them interchangeably.
Example 1
The rash appeared similar to contact dermatitis, but the distribution was unusual.
Example 2
"The symptoms are similar to what we saw last month, but I want to rule out something more serious before we assume it's the same cause."
📌 Clinical strength ladder — choose precisely:
similar to (possible) → consistent with (likely fits) → diagnostic of (confirms)
WORD
NEONATAL
nee-oh-NAY-tul 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌniːəʊˈneɪtəl/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-HIGH-low — peak on "NAY" (diphthong /eɪ/). "Nee-oh" builds lightly. "-tal" reduces to "-tul" (schwa).
⚠️ Stress on 3rd syllable "NAY". Relating to newborn infants — specifically the first 28 days of life. Distinct from "perinatal" (around the time of birth) and "postnatal" (after birth, generally). The neonatal period is the highest-risk window for infection and complications.
Example 1
The neonatal unit provides specialist care for premature or seriously ill newborns.
Example 2
Neonatal sepsis is a medical emergency — early antibiotic treatment is critical within the first hours of suspected infection.
📌 Related time-period terms — distinct meanings:
neonatal = first 28 days  |  perinatal = around time of birth  |  postnatal = after birth  |  antenatal = before birth
WORD
PATHOGEN
PATH-uh-jen 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈpæθədʒən/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low-low — peak on "PATH" (short /æ/, same as "cat"). The "th" is voiceless /θ/ (tongue between teeth, no vibration). "-gen" ends with soft /dʒ/.
⚠️ "PATH" uses short /æ/ (not "PAYTH"). The voiceless "th" is essential — "PAYTH" or "PAT" without th both sound wrong. A microorganism — bacterium, virus, fungus, or parasite — that causes disease. "Pathogenic" = path-uh-JEN-ik (stress shifts to the syllable before -ic).
Example 1
Identifying the specific pathogen is essential before selecting the appropriate antibiotic.
Example 2
Group B Streptococcus is a common pathogen in neonatal sepsis — maternal screening is routine in many countries.
📌 Same voiceless "th" /θ/ family:
pathogen → PATH-uh-jen  |  pathology → puh-THOL-uh-jee  |  pathological → path-uh-LOJ-i-kul
WORD
PROFUSE
pruh-FYOOS 2 syllables
British (RP): /prəˈfjuːs/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH — peak on "FYOOS" (long /uː/ with /j/ glide, same as "fuse", "use"). "Pruh-" is a quick schwa.
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "FYOOS". British RP /j/ glide — "FYOOS" not "FOOS". Present in large or excessive amounts — used clinically for sweating, bleeding, or vomiting that is far above normal levels. Always signals clinical significance — "profuse bleeding" is never trivial.
Example 1
She presented with profuse sweating and chest pain — a cardiac cause was immediately considered.
Example 2
The wound was producing profuse bleeding that required direct pressure and urgent surgical review.
📌 Same /j/ glide before long /uː/:
profuse → pruh-FYOOS  |  fuse → FYOOZ  |  refuse → ri-FYOOZ  |  confuse → kun-FYOOZ
WORD
DIAPHORESIS
dy-uh-fuh-REE-sis 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌdaɪəfəˈriːsɪs/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-low-HIGH-low — peak on "REE" (long /iː/). Long build-up through four light syllables. "Dy-" starts with diphthong /aɪ/ — same as "die".
⚠️ Primary stress on 4th syllable "REE". The medical term for profuse sweating — especially when caused by illness, fever, autonomic dysfunction, or medication side effects. Used in formal clinical documentation and OET writing. Casual equivalent: "profuse sweating" or "sweating buckets" (patient language).
Example 1
Diaphoresis and pallor on examination were consistent with haemodynamic compromise.
Example 2
The patient developed diaphoresis shortly after the infusion was commenced, prompting the nurse to stop the drip and call for review.
📌 Clinical vs patient language for the same symptom:
diaphoresis (clinical/formal) = profuse sweating (plain clinical) = sweating buckets (patient language)
WORD
AZTREONAM
AZ-tree-oh-nam 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈæztriːəʊnæm/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low-low-low — peak on "AZ" (short /æ/, "cat" vowel). Falls through "-tree-oh-nam". "Tree" uses long /iː/.
⚠️ Stress on 1st syllable "AZ". A monobactam antibiotic — the only drug in its class — effective against gram-negative bacteria including Pseudomonas. Critically, it has no cross-reactivity with penicillin in most patients, making it a key option when a severe penicillin allergy is documented. Distinct from carbapenems and cephalosporins in both structure and allergy profile.
Example 1
Aztreonam was selected because the patient had a documented severe penicillin allergy and required gram-negative cover.
Example 2
"Unlike most beta-lactams, aztreonam can generally be used safely in patients with a penicillin allergy — but always confirm with the prescriber."
📌 Key clinical point — penicillin allergy and antibiotics:
aztreonam → safe in penicillin allergy  |  cephalosporins → low cross-reactivity risk  |  carbapenems → very low but possible cross-reactivity
WORD
BLUNTLY
BLUNT-lee 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈblʌntli/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low — peak on "BLUNT" (/ʌ/ vowel, same as "cup"). The consonant cluster "bl-" + the ending "-ntly" with its four consonants requires careful articulation.
⚠️ "BLUNT" uses /ʌ/ — same as "cup", "cut". The ending "-ntly" has four consonants: n-t-l-y. In fast speech "-ntly" can reduce to "-nly" but the t is still implied. Means in a direct and sometimes stark manner without softening — used to signal that what follows is frank and unfiltered. In clinical communication, bluntness can be appropriate when clarity matters more than diplomacy — particularly in urgent or high-stakes conversations. Often preceded by a framing phrase: "I'm going to speak bluntly..." or "Bluntly, the situation is serious."
Example 1
"Bluntly, the treatment has not been working and we need to consider a different approach entirely."
Example 2
She appreciated that the consultant spoke to her bluntly — she had been getting vague answers for weeks and needed clarity.
📌 Framing phrases before blunt clinical statements:
"Bluntly, ..."  |  "To be direct, ..."  |  "I want to be honest with you, ..."  |  "I'm going to speak plainly, ..."

📚 Previous Batch — Set 15 (Remaining 202604 Medical Terms)

📌 Newest words always appear here first. Scroll down to review older entries.
WORD
PSYCHOTIC
sy-KOT-ik 3 syllables
British (RP): /saɪˈkɒtɪk/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low-low — peak on "KOT" with the diphthong /aɪ/ in "SY-" leading into it. The "p" at the start is completely silent — say it as if it begins with "S": SY-KOT-ik.
⚠️ The initial "p" is SILENT — pronounced exactly as if spelled "sychotic". Same silent-p pattern as "psychology", "psychiatrist". Stress on 2nd syllable "KOT" (short /ɒ/, "hot" vowel). Refers to a mental state involving loss of contact with reality — hallucinations, delusions, or severely disorganised thinking. In OET contexts, frequently appears as a drug side effect warning (e.g. mefloquine, steroids, some Parkinson's medications).
Example 1
She developed psychotic symptoms during her course of mefloquine — the drug was immediately discontinued.
Example 2
"This medication carries a small risk of psychotic side effects — hallucinations or unusual thoughts — please report anything like this immediately."
📌 Same silent "p" pattern:
psychotic → sy-KOT-ik  |  psychology → sy-KOL-uh-jee  |  psychiatrist → sy-KY-uh-trist
WORD
JAUNDICE
JAWN-dis 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈdʒɔːndɪs/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low — single sharp peak on "JAWN" (long /ɔː/, the "aw" sound, same as "lawn", "dawn"). "-dis" is short and falls away quickly.
⚠️ "JAWN-dis" — the "au" spelling here produces a long /ɔː/ sound (NOT a short "ow" or "ah"). Yellow discolouration of the skin and sclera (whites of the eyes) caused by elevated bilirubin — indicates liver dysfunction, bile duct obstruction, or excessive red blood cell breakdown (haemolysis). A key red-flag sign that always warrants investigation of liver function.
Example 1
She developed jaundice three days after completing the antibiotic course — liver function tests were ordered.
Example 2
"Report any yellowing of your skin or eyes — that's what we call jaundice — straight away, as it can mean the medication is affecting your liver."
WORD
LUMPECTOMY
lum-PEK-tuh-mee 4 syllables
British (RP): /lʌmˈpektəmi/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low-low — peak on "PEK", then falls through "-tuh-mee". "Lum-" uses the /ʌ/ vowel (same as "come", "cup"). Same "-ectomy" suffix pattern as "hysterectomy", "appendectomy" — always low and unstressed.
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "PEK". Same "-ectomy" suffix family as hysterectomy and gastrectomy (covered in earlier sets) — surgical removal. A lumpectomy is breast-conserving surgery that removes only the tumour and surrounding tissue, as opposed to a mastectomy (full breast removal). Used in early-stage breast cancer treatment, often combined with radiotherapy.
Example 1
She underwent a lumpectomy followed by a course of radiotherapy to reduce the risk of local recurrence.
Example 2
"A lumpectomy removes just the tumour and a small margin of healthy tissue, rather than the whole breast."
📌 Same "-ectomy" family (surgical removal):
lumpectomy → lum-PEK-tuh-mee  |  mastectomy → mas-TEK-tuh-mee  |  hysterectomy → his-tuh-REK-tuh-mee
WORD
ROTATOR CUFF
roh-TAY-tuh KUF 4 syllables total
British (RP): /rəʊˈteɪtə kʌf/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low | HIGH — two peaks. "Rotator" peaks on "TAY" (diphthong /eɪ/), British RP non-rhotic "-tor" = "-tuh" (no r sound). "Cuff" gets its own strong final peak — short /ʌ/ vowel, same as "cup".
⚠️ "Rotator" = roh-TAY-tuh (stress on 2nd syllable, "-tor" ending = schwa, no r sound in British RP). "Cuff" = KUF (short /ʌ/). The rotator cuff is a group of four muscles and tendons stabilising the shoulder joint. A "torn rotator cuff" is one of the most common shoulder injuries presented in OET orthopaedic/physiotherapy scenarios — causes pain, weakness, and restricted overhead movement.
Example 1
He was diagnosed with a torn rotator cuff following an MRI scan of his shoulder.
Example 2
"The pain when you raise your arm overhead suggests a possible rotator cuff problem — we'll need to investigate further."
WORD
IMPINGEMENT SYNDROME
im-PINJ-munt SIN-drohm 6 syllables total
British (RP): /ɪmˈpɪndʒmənt ˈsɪndrəʊm/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low | HIGH-low — two peaks. "Impingement" peaks on "PINJ" (soft "g" = /dʒ/, like "hinge"). "Syndrome" peaks on "SIN" (same pattern as "spasmodic torticollis" from earlier sets).
⚠️ "Impingement" = im-PINJ-munt — the "g" is soft /dʒ/ (like "ginger", "hinge"), not hard /g/. Stress on 2nd syllable "PINJ". A shoulder condition where tendons become compressed during arm movement (especially overhead), causing pain and restricted function. Closely related to rotator cuff problems — often appears alongside it in orthopaedic OET scenarios.
Example 1
She presented with shoulder impingement syndrome — she had been unable to reach above her head for three months.
Example 2
"The pinching sensation when you lift your arm sideways is typical of impingement syndrome — we'll start with physiotherapy."
PHRASE
BONE DENSITY SCAN (DEXA)
BOHN DEN-si-tee SKAN 5 syllables total
British (RP): /bəʊn ˈdensɪti skæn/
🎵 Pitch contour: three peaks — BOHN / DEN-si-tee / SKAN. Each content word gets its own stress. "Bone" uses the long /əʊ/ diphthong. "Density" peaks on the first syllable. "Scan" is short and sharp, short /æ/ vowel.
⚠️ Also called a DEXA scan (dual-energy X-ray absorptiometry) — the formal clinical name patients rarely use, but worth recognising. Measures bone mineral density to diagnose osteoporosis, typically expressed as a T-score. A T-score of -2.5 or below confirms osteoporosis. This is the standard confirmatory test referenced whenever discussing osteoporosis diagnosis or bisphosphonate treatment (like alendronate).
Example 1
The diagnosis of osteoporosis was confirmed by a bone density scan showing a T-score of minus 2.8.
Example 2
"We'd like to arrange a bone density scan — it's a quick, painless test that tells us how strong your bones are."
PHRASE
A LOADING DOSE
uh LOH-ding DOHS 4 syllables total
British (RP): /ə ˈləʊdɪŋ dəʊs/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low | HIGH — two peaks. "Loading" peaks on "LOH" (long /əʊ/). "Dose" gets its own sharp final peak — also /əʊ/, rhymes with "loading" — both words share the same vowel sound, making them easy to pair phonetically.
⚠️ Both "loading" and "dose" share the same /əʊ/ diphthong — practise them together. A loading dose is a higher-than-maintenance initial dose given to quickly reach therapeutic drug levels in the body (used for amiodarone, digoxin, and certain antibiotics). Patients need this explained clearly — they may worry a higher-than-usual dose is a mistake unless the rationale is given.
Example 1
The patient is starting a loading dose to build up levels of the drug in their body before switching to a lower maintenance dose.
Example 2
"You're starting on a loading dose — it's higher than what you'll eventually take, but it's needed to get the medication working quickly."
📌 Same /əʊ/ diphthong pair:
loading → LOH-ding  |  dose → DOHS  |  both → BOHTH  |  most → MOHST

📚 Previous Batch — Set 13 (Consciousness Spectrum — 8 Levels)

📌 These 8 phrases form a single descending spectrum — from fully alert to unconscious. Learn them as one connected scale, not isolated words.
SPECTRUM 1/8
ALERT AND RESPONSIVE
uh-LURT and ri-SPON-siv 6 syllables total
British (RP): /əˈlɜːt ənd rɪˈspɒnsɪv/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low | low-HIGH-low — two peaks. "Alert" = uh-LURT (British RP /ɜː/, no r sound — same as "fur"). "Responsive" peaks on "SPON". Both words carry equal weight — say them as a confident, clear pair when documenting baseline status.
⚠️ Baseline normal — full consciousness, fully able to interact appropriately. GCS 15. The standard opening phrase for any consciousness assessment documentation. Used as the comparison point for everything below on this spectrum.
Example 1
On assessment the patient was alert and responsive with a GCS of 15.
Example 2
"He was alert and responsive when I arrived, but his condition has changed significantly since then."
SPECTRUM 2/8
LESS RESPONSIVE
les ri-SPON-siv 3 syllables
British (RP): /les rɪˈspɒnsɪv/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low — peak on "SPON". "Less" is short and slightly falling — signals a comparative decline from baseline. The whole phrase should sound like a careful, watchful observation, not alarming yet but noted.
⚠️ The first sign of mild deterioration — the patient still responds but takes longer or responds less fully than their established baseline. A comparative term — always relative to how the patient was previously, not an absolute description. This is the trigger phrase for increasing monitoring frequency.
Example 1
Over the following hour the patient became less responsive and required immediate medical review.
Example 2
"She seems less responsive than she was an hour ago — I think we should increase her observations."
SPECTRUM 3/8
DAZED
DAYZD 1 syllable
British (RP): /deɪzd/
🎵 Pitch contour: single syllable, sharp peak. The vowel is the diphthong /eɪ/ — same as "days", "face". Ends with a voiced /zd/ cluster — both consonants must be audible: "dayz" + "d", not just "dayz".
⚠️ Mild confusion or disorientation — the patient is awake but appears stunned, vacant, or not fully processing their surroundings. Often used after a fall, head injury, or seizure. Don't confuse with "drowsy" (sleepy) — "dazed" specifically implies a disconnect between consciousness and clear cognitive processing.
Example 1
He appeared dazed after the fall — he was able to speak but could not recall the event.
Example 2
"She looked quite dazed when she came round — it took several minutes before she seemed fully present."
SPECTRUM 4/8
DROWSY BUT AROUSABLE
DROW-zee but uh-ROWZ-uh-bul 7 syllables total
British (RP): /ˈdraʊzi bʌt əˈraʊzəbl/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low | low-HIGH-low-low — two peaks. "Drowsy" peaks on "DROW" (diphthong /aʊ/, same as "now"). "Arousable" peaks on "ROWZ" (same diphthong). Both words share the /aʊ/ sound — practise them together to lock in the vowel.
⚠️ A reassuring middle-spectrum phrase — the patient is sleepy and may need stimulation to engage, but DOES respond when roused (to voice or light touch). The word "arousable" is the key clinical qualifier — it confirms the patient can still be brought to wakefulness, distinguishing this from more serious unresponsiveness. Both "drowsy" and "arousable" share the same /aʊ/ diphthong, making them easy to pair phonetically.
Example 1
She was drowsy but arousable — she opened her eyes and responded to her name when called.
Example 2
"He's drowsy but arousable at the moment — he wakes when I speak to him but drifts off again quickly."
SPECTRUM 5/8
LETHARGIC
li-THAR-jik 3 syllables
British (RP): /lɪˈθɑːdʒɪk/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low — peak on "THAR" (British RP non-rhotic /ɑː/, same as "car", "far"). The "th" is voiceless /θ/ (tongue between teeth, no vibration — like "think", "thin"). "-jik" ends with soft /dʒ/.
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "THAR". The "th" sound is voiceless — a common error is replacing it with a hard "t" or "s" sound. British RP "ar" = /ɑː/, no r sound. Describes unusual sluggishness and lack of normal energy — slower than baseline in both movement and mental responses, but still able to engage when prompted. Sits between "drowsy but arousable" and "disoriented" on the spectrum.
Example 1
He appeared lethargic and was slow to answer questions — consistent with early encephalopathy.
Example 2
"She's been unusually lethargic all morning — not herself at all, even after her coffee."
📌 Same voiceless "th" /θ/ — tongue between teeth, no vibration:
lethargic → li-THAR-jik  |  thirst → THURST  |  throat → THROHT
SPECTRUM 6/8
DISORIENTED TO TIME AND PLACE
dis-OR-ee-en-tid 5 syllables
British (RP): /dɪsˈɔːrientɪd/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low-low-low — peak on "OR" (British RP /ɔː/, the "aw" sound, non-rhotic — no r). "Dis-" is short and light. The full phrase has a falling, descriptive quality — clinical and matter-of-fact.
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "OR" — non-rhotic /ɔː/, sounds like "AW" not "or". A specific, structured way of describing confusion — confirms the patient cannot correctly state the current time/date or recognise their location. This is one of the three standard orientation checks (person, place, time) used in cognitive assessment — always specify WHICH domains are affected, as in this phrase.
Example 1
She was disoriented to time and place — she believed she was at home and could not recall the year.
Example 2
"He seems disoriented to time and place — he keeps asking what year it is and doesn't recognise the ward."
📌 The three orientation domains (always check all three):
orientation to person (who they are/who you are)  |  orientation to place (where they are)  |  orientation to time (date/time of day)
SPECTRUM 7/8
PROGRESSIVELY UNRESPONSIVE
pruh-GRES-iv-lee un-ri-SPON-siv 8 syllables total
British (RP): /prəˈɡresɪvli ʌnrɪˈspɒnsɪv/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low-low | low-low-HIGH-low — two peaks. "Progressively" peaks on "GRES". "Unresponsive" peaks on "SPON". Say with a sense of urgency — this phrase signals an active emergency in progress, not a stable description.
⚠️ Critical escalation phrase — describes a patient actively deteriorating in real time, not a static state. This is a "call for help NOW" phrase in OET listening and speaking scenarios — when this phrase appears, expect an urgent action (calling a code, senior review, emergency intervention) to follow immediately. The word "progressively" signals an ongoing trend, not a single observation.
Example 1
He became progressively unresponsive over the afternoon — a code was called at 15:30.
Example 2
"She's progressively unresponsive — I need senior help in here right now."
SPECTRUM 8/8
UNCONSCIOUS
un-KON-shus 3 syllables
British (RP): /ʌnˈkɒnʃəs/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low — peak on "KON" (short /ɒ/ as in "hot"). "Un-" is light. "-scious" = "-shus" (always reduces to /ʃəs/ — never pronounce the "c" and "s" separately).
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "KON". "-scious" always reduces to "-shus" /ʃəs/. The end of the consciousness spectrum — complete absence of awareness, no response to verbal or painful stimuli. A medical emergency requiring immediate assessment of airway, breathing, and circulation (ABC). This is the most serious and final point on the 8-level spectrum you've just learned — from fully alert down to this.
Example 1
By the time the ambulance arrived the patient was unconscious and not breathing normally.
Example 2
"He's completely unconscious — no response to voice or pain. Call an ambulance immediately."
📌 Full consciousness spectrum — review all 8 levels in order:
alert and responsive → less responsive → dazed → drowsy but arousable → lethargic → disoriented to time and place → progressively unresponsive → unconscious

📚 Previous Batch — Set 12 (practise / strenuous / exertion / detail)

📌 Newest words always appear here first. Scroll down to review older entries.
WORD
PRACTISE (verb) vs PRACTICE (noun)
PRAK-tis 2 syllables — same pronunciation for both
British (RP): /ˈpræktɪs/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low — peak on "PRAK" (short /æ/, as in "cat"), falls on "-tis". Both spellings sound IDENTICAL — the distinction is entirely in writing, not speech. Don't worry about pronunciation here — focus on the spelling rule for OET Writing.
⚠️ This is a SPELLING distinction unique to British English (American English uses "practice" for both). practise = verb (an action): "to practise medicine", "she practises injections." practice = noun (a thing): "his pharmacy practice", "good practice". Same rule applies to licence (noun) vs license (verb), and advice (noun) vs advise (verb). Mnemonic: "ice" is a noun (like "ice cream") → -ice = noun; "s" sounds like a verb action. Critical for OET Writing — examiners notice this error.
Example 1 — verb
Pharmacists must practise within their professional scope at all times.
Example 2 — noun
She has worked in community pharmacy practice for over ten years.
📌 Same noun/verb spelling pattern (British English only):
licence (noun) / license (verb)  |  advice (noun) / advise (verb)  |  practice (noun) / practise (verb)
WORD
STRENUOUS
STREN-yoo-us 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈstrenjuəs/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low-low — peak hard on "STREN" (short /e/ as in "bed"), then falls through "-yoo-us". The consonant cluster "str-" = three consonants together — practise "s-t-r" as one smooth unit. "-yoo-" has a /j/ glide.
⚠️ Stress on 1st syllable "STREN". The cluster "str-" is challenging — say "street", "strong" first to warm up the same cluster, then move to "strenuous". Clinically: describes physical activity requiring significant exertion — used constantly in post-operative and cardiac rehabilitation counselling. "Avoid strenuous activity" is one of the most common discharge instructions in OET.
Example 1
He was advised to avoid strenuous activity for six weeks following the surgery.
Example 2
"You can resume light exercise straight away, but anything strenuous — like running or heavy lifting — should wait until your follow-up appointment."
📌 Same "str-" consonant cluster:
strenuous → STREN-yoo-us  |  strain → STRAYN  |  stress → STRES  |  stretch → STRECH
WORD
EXERTION
ig-ZUR-shun 3 syllables
British (RP): /ɪɡˈzɜːʃən/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low — peak on "ZUR" (British RP /ɜː/, same vowel as "fur", "her" — non-rhotic, no r sound), falls on "-shun". "Ex-" reduces to "ig-" with voiced /g/ — common pattern when "ex-" precedes a stressed vowel.
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "ZUR". The "ex-" prefix here is voiced: "ig-ZUR-shun" NOT "eks-ZUR-shun" — this voicing shift happens because the stress falls on the syllable right after the prefix (compare: "exhale" = ig-ZAYL, same pattern). Clinically essential: "shortness of breath on exertion" or "chest pain on exertion" are classic phrases for assessing cardiac and respiratory symptom triggers — always ask whether symptoms occur at rest or only with exertion.
Example 1
She reported shortness of breath on exertion — even walking up one flight of stairs left her breathless.
Example 2
"Does the chest pain happen at rest, or only on exertion — for example when climbing stairs or exercising?"
📌 Same "ex-" → voiced "ig-" pattern before stressed syllable:
exertion → ig-ZUR-shun  |  exhale → ig-ZAYL  |  exist → ig-ZIST  |  exam → ig-ZAM
PHRASE
IN DETAIL / DETAILED
in di-TAYL | DEE-tayld 2 + 2 syllables
British (RP): /ɪn dɪˈteɪl/ — adjective: /ˈdiːteɪld/
🎵 "In detail" — peak on "TAYL" (2nd syllable, /eɪ/ diphthong) — stress is on the 2nd syllable when it's the noun phrase. "Detailed" (adjective, before a noun) — stress shifts to the 1st syllable: DEE-tayld. This noun/adjective stress shift is subtle but real in British RP.
⚠️ "In detail" (after a verb, e.g. "explain in detail") = stress on 2nd syllable "TAYL" — di-TAYL. "Detailed" (adjective before a noun, e.g. "a detailed history") = stress often shifts to 1st syllable — DEE-tayld, though di-TAYLD is also heard. Both are used constantly in OET — "explain in detail" is a common instruction in speaking role-plays, and "a detailed history/explanation" appears regularly in OET writing tasks.
Example 1 — "in detail"
The pharmacist explained the medication regimen in detail, covering timing, side effects, and what to do if a dose was missed.
Example 2 — "detailed"
A detailed medication history was taken before the new prescription was dispensed.
📌 OET Speaking instruction phrases using "in detail":
"Explain the procedure in detail"  |  "Describe your symptoms in detail"  |  "Go through the dosing instructions in detail"

📚 Previous Batch — 202604 Data (Pain / Gout / Neurology / Pharmacy Letters)

📌 Newest words always appear here first. Scroll down to review older entries.
WORD
EXCRUCIATING
ik-SKROO-shee-ay-ting 5 syllables
British (RP): /ɪkˈskruːʃieɪtɪŋ/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low-low-low — peak on "SKROO", then a long fall through three light syllables. "SKROO" uses long /uː/ (same as "true", "blue"). The word is often delivered with extra emphasis/length on the peak when describing severe pain.
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "SKROO". The consonant cluster "scr-" = /skr/ — practise as one unit. "-ciating" = "-shee-ay-ting" (4 light syllables). Common error: stressing "EX" at the start. Clinical use: describes pain at the absolute top of severity (8–10 on VAS) — equivalent to kidney stone or gout pain. Patients rarely use this word casually — when they do, take it seriously.
Example 1
The patient described the pain as excruciating — he rated it 9 out of 10 and was unable to walk.
Example 2
Renal colic is classically described as one of the most excruciating pains a person can experience.
📌 Other extreme-pain adjectives with similar weight:
unbearable → un-BAIR-uh-bul  |  agonising → AG-uh-ny-zing  |  severe → suh-VEER
WORD
GOUT
GOWT 1 syllable
British (RP): /ɡaʊt/
🎵 Pitch contour: single syllable, sharp peak. The vowel is a diphthong /aʊ/ — same sound as "out", "house", "now". Rhymes exactly with "out" with a /g/ added. Don't say "goot" (long oo) — that's a different word entirely.
⚠️ "Gout" rhymes with "out", "shout", "doubt" — diphthong /aʊ/. Common Korean-speaker error: pronouncing it as "goot" (rhyming with "boot") — completely changes the sound. Gout = inflammatory arthritis caused by uric acid crystal deposition, classically affecting the big toe (podagra). Excruciating pain is the hallmark presenting symptom.
Example 1
He was diagnosed with gout after presenting with acute excruciating pain in his right big toe.
Example 2
Colchicine and allopurinol are both commonly used in the management of gout, though for different purposes.
📌 Same /aʊ/ diphthong words:
gout → GOWT  |  bout → BOWT (e.g. "a bout of nausea")  |  about → uh-BOWT
WORD
COLCHICINE
KOL-chi-seen 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈkɒlkɪsiːn/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low-low — peak hard on "KOL", then falls through "-chi-seen". "KOL" = short /ɒ/ as in "hot", "lot". "-seen" ends with a long /iː/. Note: some British speakers say "KOL-chi-sin" (short final vowel) — both are acceptable in OET.
⚠️ Stress on 1st syllable "KOL". Used for acute gout flares — its most common dose-limiting side effect is diarrhoea, often severe enough to require dose reduction or discontinuation. Always counsel patients about this GI side effect proactively — it's one of the most clinically relevant counselling points for this drug.
Example 1
He was started on colchicine for the acute gout flare but developed significant diarrhoea after the second dose.
Example 2
"I need to warn you that colchicine commonly causes diarrhoea — if it becomes severe, stop the medication and contact us."
WORD
ALLOPURINOL
al-oh-PYOOR-i-nol 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌæləʊˈpjʊərɪnɒl/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-HIGH-low-low — peak on "PYOOR". "Al-oh" builds quietly. "PYOOR" has the /j/ glide — same sound as the word "pure". "-i-nol" falls and ends with short /ɒ/.
⚠️ Stress on 3rd syllable "PYOOR" — contains the /j/ glide as in "pure", "cure". Allopurinol = urate-lowering therapy for long-term gout prevention (not for acute flares). Key safety point: can cause a serious skin rash, including rare but life-threatening Stevens-Johnson syndrome — any rash must prompt immediate discontinuation and medical review.
Example 1
The patient developed a skin rash three weeks after starting allopurinol — the medication was immediately withheld.
Example 2
"Allopurinol is for long-term prevention, not for treating the pain you're having right now — we'll add something else for that."
WORD
SEPTICAEMIA (SEPTICEMIA)
sep-ti-SEE-mee-uh 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌseptɪˈsiːmiə/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-HIGH-low-low — peak hard on "SEE" (long /iː/), then falls through "-mee-uh". "Sep-ti" builds quickly and lightly before the peak.
⚠️ Stress on 3rd syllable "SEE" — long vowel, hold it slightly. British spelling: "septicaemia" (US: "septicemia") — same pronunciation. A medical emergency: bacterial infection in the bloodstream causing fever, hypotension, and potentially organ failure. Often used interchangeably with "sepsis" in casual clinical speech, though sepsis is the broader syndrome.
Example 1
He deteriorated rapidly overnight and developed septicaemia — he was transferred to the ICU immediately.
Example 2
"We're concerned this could be early septicaemia — we need to start broad-spectrum antibiotics without delay."
WORD
MEFLOQUINE
MEF-loh-kween 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈmefləʊkwiːn/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low-low — peak on "MEF" (short /e/, as in "bed"), then falls through "-loh-kween". "Kween" ends with long /iː/, same sound as "queen".
⚠️ Stress on 1st syllable "MEF". Brand name: Lariam. Antimalarial drug with a well-known and serious side-effect profile: vivid or odd dreams, anxiety, and — in some cases — psychotic symptoms (hallucinations, severe confusion). Patients should be specifically warned about neuropsychiatric effects before starting, especially those with a history of mental illness.
Example 1
He had been prescribed mefloquine for malaria prophylaxis and was warned about the risk of odd dreams and psychotic symptoms.
Example 2
"Mefloquine isn't suitable for everyone — if you have a history of depression or anxiety, we should consider an alternative."
WORD
ARTESUNATE
ar-tuh-SOO-nayt 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌɑːtɪˈsuːneɪt/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-HIGH-low — peak on "SOO" (long /uː/), then falls on "-nayt" (diphthong /eɪ/). "Ar-tuh" — British RP non-rhotic "ar" = /ɑː/, no r sound.
⚠️ Stress on 3rd syllable "SOO". British RP "ar-" = /ɑː/ (no audible r). First-line treatment for severe (complicated) malaria, given intravenously. Distinguish from mefloquine, which is used for prophylaxis and uncomplicated cases — artesunate is reserved for severe disease requiring hospital treatment.
Example 1
Artesunate was commenced intravenously as the patient had severe falciparum malaria.
Example 2
"Because your malaria is severe, we need to start artesunate through a drip rather than tablets."
WORD
AMIODARONE
uh-MY-oh-duh-ROHN 5 syllables
British (RP): /əˌmaɪəˈdærəʊn/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-low-low-HIGH — long build through four light syllables before a sharp final peak on "ROHN". "MY" uses diphthong /aɪ/. Don't peak early on "MY" — the real peak is at the end.
⚠️ Primary stress on the FINAL syllable "ROHN" — unusual for a 5-syllable word, so it's easy to mis-stress. Complex antiarrhythmic drug for atrial fibrillation with an extensive side-effect profile: photosensitivity (needs daily high-factor sunscreen), blue-grey skin discolouration (permanent), corneal deposits causing visual halos at night, and hepatotoxicity (causes jaundice). Requires baseline and periodic monitoring of thyroid, liver, and lung function.
Example 1
Amiodarone is prescribed to manage atrial fibrillation — it is a very effective but complex medication with several important side effects.
Example 2
"While taking amiodarone, you'll need regular blood tests to check your thyroid and liver function."
WORD
ALENDRONATE
uh-LEN-druh-nayt 4 syllables
British (RP): /əˈlendrəneɪt/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low-low — peak on "LEN", then falls through "-druh-nayt". "Uh-" is a quick schwa. "-nayt" ends with diphthong /eɪ/.
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "LEN". A bisphosphonate used for osteoporosis. Critical counselling point: must be taken first thing in the morning on an empty stomach with a full glass of plain water, then the patient must remain upright (sitting or standing) for at least 30 minutes — lying down too soon can cause oesophageal irritation or painful ulcers. Any swallowing pain or new heartburn must be reported immediately.
Example 1
Regarding specific precautions, Mrs Rossi must remain upright for at least 30 minutes following the administration of alendronate to prevent oesophageal irritation.
Example 2
"Take your alendronate with a full glass of water, then stay sitting up — don't lie back down for half an hour."
WORD
ASYMPTOMATIC
ay-simp-tuh-MAT-ik 6 syllables
British (RP): /ˌeɪsɪmptəˈmætɪk/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-low-low-HIGH-low — single peak on "MAT", long build before it. "Ay-" (negative prefix "a-") = diphthong /eɪ/, not a short "uh". Don't confuse with "symptomatic" alone, which has stress on "MAT" as well but no leading "ay-".
⚠️ Primary stress on 5th syllable "MAT". The "a-" prefix (meaning "without") = /eɪ/ — like saying "A" the letter. Clinically: a condition is present but produces no detectable symptoms — important in screening contexts (e.g. asymptomatic bacteriuria, asymptomatic hypertension). Often paired conceptually with "subclinical" — both describe disease without overt symptoms.
Example 1
She was asymptomatic at the time of diagnosis — the condition was detected incidentally on a blood test.
Example 2
Many patients with early-stage kidney disease remain asymptomatic until renal function has significantly declined.
📌 Severity scale — full clinical ladder:
asymptomatic → mild → moderate → moderately severe → severe → critical/acute
WORD
DYSPHAGIA
dis-FAY-jee-uh 4 syllables
British (RP): /dɪsˈfeɪdʒiə/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low-low — peak on "FAY" (diphthong /eɪ/), then falls through "-jee-uh". "Dis-" is short and light. The "g" in "-phagia" is soft /dʒ/ — same as "jelly".
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "FAY". "-phagia" = "-jee-uh" with soft /dʒ/ (not hard /g/). Means difficulty swallowing — always a red-flag symptom requiring investigation, as it can indicate oesophageal stricture, malignancy, or neurological disease. Don't confuse with "dysphasia" (difficulty with language/speech) — similar spelling, completely different meaning.
Example 1
He reported progressive dysphagia with solids over two months — an urgent endoscopy was arranged.
Example 2
"Any difficulty swallowing — what we call dysphagia — needs to be checked promptly, as it can have a number of underlying causes."
📌 Don't confuse — similar spelling, different meaning:
dysphagia → difficulty swallowing  |  dysphasia → difficulty with speech/language  |  dysarthria → slurred/unclear speech (motor)

📚 Previous Batch — Set 11 (Alcohol Abstinence)

📌 Newest words always appear here first. Scroll down to review older entries.
WORD
ALCOHOL ABSTINENCE
AL-kuh-hol AB-sti-nunts 6 syllables total
British (RP): /ˈælkəhɒl ˈæbstɪnəns/
🎵 Pitch: AL-kuh-hol (peak on 1st) | AB-sti-nunts (peak on 1st of "abstinence"). Two equal peaks — one per word. "Alcohol" falls through "-kuh-hol"; "abstinence" falls through "-sti-nunts". British RP: "alcohol" = AL-kuh-hol (the "-ol" = /hɒl/, short /ɒ/ as in "hot"). "Abstinence" = AB-sti-nunts ("-nence" reduces to "-nunts").
⚠️ "Alcohol" = AL-kuh-hol (3 syllables, stress on 1st; "-hol" = /hɒl/ with short /ɒ/). "Abstinence" = AB-sti-nunts (4 syllables, stress on 1st; "-nence" = "-nunts" with schwa). Common error: "ab-STI-nunts" (wrong stress) or "ab-sti-NENCE" (4 equal beats). "Abstinence" = complete avoidance — stronger than "moderation" or "reduction". OET pharmacy contexts: metronidazole (disulfiram-like reaction with alcohol), disulfiram itself, some cephalosporins. Distinguish: "abstinence" (complete avoidance) vs "moderation" (reduced intake) vs "reduction" (cutting down).
Example 1 — metronidazole counselling
Complete alcohol abstinence is essential during metronidazole treatment — even small amounts of alcohol can cause flushing, nausea, and palpitations.
Example 2 — general counselling
"While you are taking this medication, I must stress the importance of alcohol abstinence — this is not simply a recommendation to drink less, but to avoid alcohol completely."
📌 Abstinence vs moderation vs reduction — clinical distinction:
abstinence = zero alcohol (e.g. metronidazole, disulfiram)  |  moderation = limited amounts  |  reduction = cutting down from current level

📚 Previous Batch — Set 10 (consolidate / combine)

WORD
VITAL STATS
VY-tul STATS 3 syllables total
British (RP): /ˈvaɪtəl stæts/
🎵 Pitch: VY-tul (peak on 1st) | STATS (strong final peak). "Stats" carries the most emphasis as the key noun — short, punchy, falls immediately. "Vital" = VY-tul — "-tal" reduces to "-tul" (schwa). "Stats" = STATS — short /æ/ vowel as in "cat", "back".
⚠️ "Vital" = VY-tul (stress on 1st; "-tal" = schwa). "Stats" = short form of "statistics" — always plural in clinical use. Vowel = /æ/ as in "cat" (NOT "storts" or "stahts"). Clinical meaning: "vital stats" = informal shorthand for vital signs (= obs / observations): blood pressure, heart rate, respiratory rate, temperature, oxygen saturation (SpO₂). Used by nurses and doctors in handovers, ward rounds, and rapid assessments. OET Part B context: nurse briefing a doctor — "Her vital stats are stable" = all observations within normal limits. More formal equivalents: "vital signs are within normal limits" / "observations are stable" / "her obs are fine".
Example 1 — ward handover
"Mrs Tate's vital stats are stable this morning — BP 118 over 76, heart rate 74, sats 98% on room air."
Example 2 — clinical note equivalent
On examination, vital signs were within normal limits — the informal term "vital stats" is commonly used in verbal handovers.
📌 "Vital stats" → formal clinical equivalents:
"vital stats" (informal) = "vital signs" = "obs / observations" = "parameters"  |  Components: BP / HR / RR / Temp / SpO₂
WORD
CONSOLIDATE
kun-SOL-i-dayt 4 syllables
British (RP): /kənˈsɒlɪdeɪt/
🎵 Pitch: low-HIGH-low-low — single peak on "SOL", then falls through "-i-dayt". "Con-" reduces fully to "kun-" (schwa). "SOL" = /sɒl/ — short /ɒ/ vowel as in "hot", "lot", "got". "-i-dayt" follows quietly: "-i-" (short) then "-dayt" (diphthong /eɪ/ as in "late"). Don't stress the "-date" ending — it stays low.
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "SOL". "Con-" → "kun-" (schwa). "-idate" = "-i-dayt" — the "-date" part uses diphthong /eɪ/ but stays unstressed. Two distinct clinical uses in OET: (1) medication consolidation — combining multiple medicines into one blister pack or simplified regimen to improve adherence; (2) lung consolidation — alveoli filled with fluid/pus (e.g. pneumonia on X-ray). Context always clarifies which meaning. "Consolidate your medications" = pharmacy; "consolidation in the right lower lobe" = radiology/respiratory.
Example 1 — medication adherence
The pharmacist suggested consolidating the patient's morning medications into a single dose time to simplify the regimen and improve adherence.
Example 2 — respiratory / radiology
The chest X-ray showed consolidation in the right lower lobe, consistent with a bacterial pneumonia.
📌 Same "-idate" / "-olidate" verb pattern (stress on syllable before "-idate"):
consolidate → kun-SOL-i-dayt  |  validate → VAL-i-dayt  |  elucidate → i-LOO-si-dayt
WORD
COMBINE
kum-BYNE 2 syllables (verb)
British (RP) verb: /kəmˈbaɪn/ — noun: /ˈkɒmbaɪn/
🎵 Verb pitch: low-HIGH — "kum-" is very light (schwa), peak lands cleanly on "BYNE". "BYNE" uses diphthong /aɪ/ — same as "fine", "mine", "sign". The verb always stresses the 2nd syllable. Noun form ("combine harvester"): stress shifts to 1st syllable — KOM-byne. In OET, almost always used as a verb.
⚠️ VERB: kum-BYNE (stress on 2nd) — used in OET clinical contexts. NOUN: KOM-byne (stress on 1st) — e.g. "combine harvester" (non-clinical). This noun/verb stress shift is the same pattern as "record", "permit", "refund", "produce". Clinical OET uses of "combine": (1) drug interactions — "do not combine X with Y"; (2) treatment approach — "we can combine medication with lifestyle changes"; (3) combination therapy — "combining antibiotics is necessary for TB treatment". "Combined" (adjective) = kum-BYND.
Example 1 — drug interaction warning
"It is essential that you do not combine St. John's Wort with your sertraline — this combination can cause a serious reaction called serotonin syndrome."
Example 2 — combination therapy
Tuberculosis must be treated by combining at least four antibiotics simultaneously to prevent the development of drug resistance.
📌 Noun (1st syllable) vs verb (2nd syllable) — same word, stress shifts:
COMbine (noun) → KOM-byne  |  comBINE (verb) → kum-BYNE  |  same pattern: REcord / reCORD, PERmit / perMIT, REfund / reFUND

📚 Previous Batch — Iron / Cardiac / Mental Health / Allergy Counselling (Set 9)

📌 Newest words always appear here first. Scroll down to review older entries.
WORD
FERROUS SULFATE
FER-us SUL-fayt 4 syllables total
British (RP): /ˈferəs ˈsʌlfeɪt/
🎵 Pitch: FER-us (peak on 1st) | SUL-fayt (peak on 1st of "sulfate"). Two equal peaks. "Ferrous" = FER-us — the "-ous" reduces to "-us" (schwa). "Sulfate" = SUL-fayt — "sul-" uses /ʌ/ vowel (same as "cup"), "-fayt" is a diphthong /eɪ/. British spelling: "sulfate" or "sulphate" — both acceptable in OET.
⚠️ "Ferrous" = FER-us (stress on 1st, "-ous" = schwa). "Sulfate/Sulphate" = SUL-fayt (stress on 1st, "-fate" = /feɪt/). "Ferr-" comes from Latin "ferrum" (iron) — same root as "ferritin", "ferric". Key OET counselling points for ferrous sulfate: (1) dark/black stools = normal, harmless; (2) best absorbed on empty stomach but take with food if GI upset; (3) complete the full course; (4) avoid taking with antacids, dairy, tea, or coffee — reduces absorption; (5) can cause constipation or nausea.
Example 1
Ferrous sulfate is best absorbed on an empty stomach, but if you experience nausea, it is acceptable to take it with a small amount of food.
Example 2
"Your GP has prescribed ferrous sulfate to treat your iron deficiency anaemia — there are a few important things I need to tell you about how to take it."
📌 Same "ferr-" root (iron-related terms):
ferrous → FER-us (iron²⁺)  |  ferric → FER-ik (iron³⁺)  |  ferritin → FER-i-tin (iron storage protein)
WORD
ABSORPTION
ub-SORP-shun 3 syllables
British (RP): /əbˈsɔːpʃən/
🎵 Pitch: low-HIGH-low — peak on "SORP", falls on "-shun". "Ab-" reduces to "ub-" (schwa). "SORP" = /sɔːp/ — British RP "or" = /ɔː/ (non-rhotic, "aw" sound, same as "sort", "court", "sport"). Don't say "ab-SORP-shun" with a full "ab" — it's "ub-SORP-shun".
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "SORP". British RP: "or" in "sorp-" = /ɔː/ — sounds like "AW" not "or". "-tion" = "-shun" (always). "Ab-" → "ub-" (schwa onset). Clinical meaning: the process by which a drug or nutrient passes from the gut into the bloodstream. Factors that reduce ferrous sulfate absorption: food (especially dairy, eggs, whole grains), antacids (reduce stomach acid needed for iron absorption), tea/coffee (tannins bind iron), tetracycline antibiotics. Vitamin C (ascorbic acid) INCREASES absorption.
Example 1
Taking ferrous sulfate with food may slightly reduce absorption, but it is preferable to stopping the medication if side effects are troublesome.
Example 2
Vitamin C can enhance iron absorption — taking your ferrous sulfate with a small glass of orange juice may help.
📌 British RP "or" → /ɔː/ (sounds like "aw") — non-rhotic:
absorption → ub-SAWP-shun  |  disorder → dis-AW-duh  |  performance → puh-FAW-munts
WORD
COURSE (of treatment)
KAWSS 1 syllable
British (RP): /kɔːs/
🎵 Pitch: single syllable — one clear peak. "KAWSS" — the vowel /ɔː/ is long and rounded (same as "sort", "horse", "force"). The final "s" = voiceless /s/ (NOT /z/). Don't shorten the vowel: "KAWSS" not "KOSS". In a phrase: "complete the COURSE" — "course" gets the main stress as the key noun.
⚠️ "Course" = KAWSS — long /ɔː/ vowel. British RP: "our" in "course" = /ɔː/ — same as "four", "floor", "bore". Not to be confused with "coarse" (= rough) — they are homophones in British RP: both = /kɔːs/. Clinical use: "a course of [medication]" = a prescribed duration of treatment. Key OET phrase: "complete the full course" — even if symptoms resolve early. This applies to antibiotics, iron supplements, antifungals, and many other medications. Stopping early = incomplete treatment, risk of relapse.
Example 1
It is important to complete the full course of ferrous sulfate — your iron stores need time to replenish even after your symptoms improve.
Example 2
"This is a three-month course — please do not stop taking it early, even if you start feeling better after a few weeks."
📌 "Complete the course" — OET counselling contexts:
antibiotics → prevents resistance  |  ferrous sulfate → rebuilds iron stores  |  antifungals → prevents recurrence  |  steroids → must taper, not stop suddenly
PHRASES
"Dark stools" / "It is nothing to be concerned about"
DARK STOOLZ | NUTH-ing to be kun-SURND uh-BOWT
stools /stuːlz/ | concerned /kənˈsɜːnd/ | nothing /ˈnʌθɪŋ/
🎵 "Dark stools": DARK (peak) + STOOLZ (peak) — equal peaks; "stools" is the key noun. "Nothing to be concerned about": peaks on NUTH-ing and SURND; "about" gets a slight final fall. The whole reassurance phrase has a falling, calming intonation — the falling pitch on "about" signals "this conversation is now closed, you can relax."
"Stools" = STOOLZ — clinical term for faeces/bowel motions (never say "poo" or "excrement" in OET). "Concerned" = kun-SURND — British RP "er" = /ɜː/ (non-rhotic). "Nothing to be concerned about" = standard OET reassurance phrase for harmless side effects. Structure: state the side effect → explain why it happens → reassure. "Your stools may appear darker than usual — this is simply the iron passing through your digestive system and is nothing to be concerned about."
OET counselling — full reassurance sequence
"You may notice that your stools become darker while you are taking ferrous sulfate — this is nothing to be concerned about. It simply means the iron is passing through your system normally."
"Even if you notice dark stools, please don't stop the medication — it is nothing to be concerned about and will resolve once you finish the course."
📌 OET reassurance phrase pattern:
"This is nothing to worry about"  |  "This is completely normal"  |  "This is a common and harmless side effect"  |  "There is no cause for concern"
PHRASE
"Even if you start feeling better"
EE-vun if you start FEEL-ing BET-ter
Key: "even" /ˈiːvən/ | "better" /ˈbetə/
🎵 Pitch: "EE-vun" gets a slight rise (the conditional opener), then peaks on "FEEL-ing" and "BET-ter". "If you start" sits low between the peaks. "Better" = BET-tuh in British RP — the final "r" is silent, "-er" = /ə/. This phrase always functions as a subordinate clause — what follows ("please continue / do not stop") is the main instruction.
"Even if" = concessive conditional — acknowledges the patient's likely action while overriding it. The stress pattern signals: I know you'll be tempted to stop, but don't. OET counselling function: used to pre-empt premature discontinuation. Always follow with the positive instruction: "...please continue taking it as prescribed." Common for: antibiotics (resistance risk), iron (stores not yet replenished), antidepressants (relapse risk), corticosteroids (rebound risk). "Better" in British RP = BET-tuh — the "tt" is a clear stop, the final "er" = schwa (no "r" sound).
OET pharmacy counselling
"Even if you start feeling better after a few weeks, please continue taking the full three-month course — your iron stores need time to fully recover."
Antibiotic context
"Even if you start feeling better after two or three days, it is essential to finish all the antibiotic tablets — stopping early can allow the infection to return."
📌 Same "pre-empt discontinuation" OET phrases:
"Even if symptoms improve..."  |  "Do not stop taking it just because you feel better..."  |  "Continue the full course regardless of..."
PHRASE
"This may slightly reduce absorption, but it's better than stopping altogether"
may SLIGHT-ly re-DYOOS ab-SORP-shun | BET-ter than STOP-ping al-tuh-GETH-er
Key: slightly /ˈslaɪtli/ | altogether /ˌɔːltəˈɡeðə/
🎵 Two-clause structure — each clause has its own peak. Clause 1 peaks: SLIGHT-ly / DYOOS / SORP. Clause 2 peaks: BET-ter / STOP-ping / GETH-er. "But" signals the contrast and gets a slight rise before the second clause. "Altogether" = awl-tuh-GETH-uh (stress on 3rd syllable; British RP final "r" silent).
OET BALANCING PHRASE — acknowledges a downside while presenting the better alternative. Structure: "[Compromise] may [minor downside], but it is better than [worse outcome]." This is nuanced clinical communication: you are not saying the food option is ideal, but you are validating the patient's need for it. OET speaking principle: always offer a practical solution even when it is imperfect. "Altogether" = /ɔːltəˈɡeðə/ — 4 syllables: awl-tuh-GETH-uh. Stress on "GETH".
OET context
"If the stomach side effects are really troublesome, you can take it with a small meal — this may slightly reduce absorption, but it is better than stopping altogether."
"I know the timing isn't ideal, but this may slightly reduce absorption, though it's still better than stopping altogether — continuing the treatment matters more."
📌 Same OET "imperfect but acceptable" balancing pattern:
"It may not be ideal, but it is safer than..."  |  "This is not the first choice, but given your situation..."  |  "It may take a little longer, but it avoids the risk of..."
WORD
DIGOXIN
di-JOK-sin 3 syllables
British (RP): /dɪˈdʒɒksɪn/
🎵 Pitch: low-HIGH-low — peak on "JOK", falls on "-sin". "Di-" = /dɪ/ (short "i", like "did"). "JOK" = /dʒɒk/ — the "g" is soft, producing a "j" sound /dʒ/ (like "jump", "gym"). "-sin" = /sɪn/ (short "i"). Don't say "di-GOK-sin" — the "g" is /dʒ/, not /g/.
⚠️ The "g" in "digoxin" = /dʒ/ — soft "j" sound (like "gym", "giraffe"). di-JOK-sin NOT di-GOK-sin. This is one of the most commonly mispronounced drug names. Digoxin = cardiac glycoside used for heart failure and atrial fibrillation. Narrow therapeutic index = small dose changes cause toxicity. Signs of toxicity: nausea, vomiting, visual disturbances (yellow/green halos), bradycardia. Key interactions: many drugs affect digoxin levels, including ferrous sulfate (iron reduces digoxin absorption if taken together — separate by 2 hours).
Example 1
Digoxin has a narrow therapeutic index — even a small increase in plasma levels can cause toxicity, so dose adjustments must be made carefully.
Example 2
"If you are also taking digoxin, please make sure to take your ferrous sulfate at least two hours apart, as iron can reduce digoxin absorption."
📌 Soft "g" = /dʒ/ in drug names (like "j" not "g"):
digoxin → di-JOK-sin  |  gentamicin → jen-tuh-MY-sin  |  gel → JEL  |  gingivitis → jin-ji-VY-tis
PHRASES
"From now on" / "even if it is herbal or from a health food shop"
from NOW on | EE-vun if it is HUR-bul or from a HELTH food SHOP
herbal: British RP /ˈhɜːbəl/ — the "h" IS pronounced in British RP (unlike American where "herb" = "URB")
🎵 "From now on": peak on NOW — the time marker. "From" and "on" are both very light. "Even if it is herbal or from a health food shop": peaks on HUR-bul and HELTH / SHOP. The repeated "or from" creates a list structure — each item gets a slight rise except the last, which falls.
CRITICAL OET PRONUNCIATION NOTE — "herbal": British RP = HUR-bul (the "h" IS pronounced). American English = UR-bul (the "h" is silent). For OET, always say HUR-bul. "Health food shop" = British term for a natural health/supplement store (American: "health food store"). "From now on" = a forward-looking time marker signalling a new instruction or behaviour change — common in OET counselling when explaining ongoing precautions. OET clinical point: patients often don't mention herbal products because they don't consider them "medication" — always ask explicitly.
OET counselling
"From now on, please tell your pharmacist or doctor about everything you take — even if it is herbal or from a health food shop — as these products can interact with your prescribed medications."
"From now on, make sure to mention any supplements too — even if it is herbal or from a health food shop, it can still affect how your medication works."
📌 British RP vs American "herbal":
British RP → HUR-bul (h is pronounced)  |  American → UR-bul (h is silent)  |  OET = British → always say HUR-bul
WORD
SERTRALINE
SUR-truh-leen 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈsɜːtrəliːn/
🎵 Pitch: HIGH-low-low — peak on "SUR", falls through "truh-leen". "SUR" = /sɜː/ — British RP non-rhotic; "er" = /ɜː/ (same as "fur", "her", "word"). "-truh-" = quick schwa. "-leen" = long /iːn/. Three syllables: SUR-truh-leen.
⚠️ Stress on 1st syllable "SUR". British RP: "ser-" = SUR /sɜː/ — same vowel as "fur", "her" (no "r" sound at end). "-traline" = "-truh-leen" (the "a" reduces to schwa, "-line" = "-leen" with long /iː/). Brand name: Lustral (UK), Zoloft (US). Sertraline = SSRI (selective serotonin reuptake inhibitor) — first-line antidepressant. Key interaction: combined with St. John's Wort → serotonin syndrome risk. Key OET counselling points: takes 2–4 weeks to show effect; do not stop suddenly; report any unusual thoughts; may cause initial nausea.
Example 1
Sertraline may take two to four weeks to have its full effect — it is important not to stop taking it suddenly without speaking to your doctor first.
Example 2
"I can see you have been prescribed sertraline — before you start taking it, I need to ask whether you are taking any other supplements or herbal products."
📌 Same "-line" ending = "-leen" (long /iː/) in drug names:
sertraline → SUR-truh-leen  |  fluoxetine → floo-OK-suh-teen  |  amitriptyline → am-i-TRIP-tuh-leen
PHRASE
SEROTONIN SYNDROME
ser-uh-TOH-nin SIN-drohm 6 syllables total
British (RP): /ˌserəˈtəʊnɪn ˈsɪndrəʊm/
🎵 Pitch: low-low-HIGH-low | HIGH-low — peak on "TOH" in serotonin, then peak on "SIN" in syndrome. Two peaks. "Ser-uh" builds very quietly before "TOH". "Syndrome" = SIN-drohm (stress on 1st, "-drohm" falls).
⚠️ "Serotonin" = ser-uh-TOH-nin (stress on 3rd syllable "TOH"). "Ser-" = /ser/ — short "e" /e/ (as in "bed"), followed by "uh" schwa. "Syndrome" = SIN-drohm (stress on 1st). Serotonin syndrome = a potentially life-threatening drug reaction caused by excess serotonergic activity. Symptoms: agitation, confusion, rapid heart rate, high temperature, muscle rigidity, tremor. Caused by: combining two or more serotonergic drugs (e.g. sertraline + St. John's Wort, or two SSRIs, or SSRI + tramadol). OET critical counselling: "Do not take St. John's Wort with sertraline — this combination can cause a serious and potentially dangerous reaction called serotonin syndrome."
Example 1
Combining sertraline with St. John's Wort significantly increases the risk of serotonin syndrome — a serious condition that requires immediate medical attention.
Example 2
"I need to warn you about a condition called serotonin syndrome — it can occur if you take St. John's Wort alongside your sertraline, which is why this combination must be avoided."
📌 Drug combinations that cause serotonin syndrome:
SSRI + St. John's Wort  |  SSRI + tramadol  |  SSRI + another SSRI  |  SSRI + MAOI (most dangerous)
WORD
ST. JOHN'S WORT
sint JONZ WURT 3 syllables
British (RP): /sənt ˈdʒɒnz wɜːt/
🎵 Pitch: "sint" is very light (nearly inaudible), then peaks on JONZ and WURT. "St." = "sint" or "snt" in fast speech — almost drops out. "Wort" = WURT /wɜːt/ — British RP non-rhotic: "or" = /ɜː/ (same as "fur", "word", "first"). "John's" = JONZ — the "J" = /dʒ/ (soft j sound).
⚠️ "Wort" = WURT /wɜːt/ — an old English word meaning "plant". British RP: "wor-" = /wɜː/ (non-rhotic, same as "word", "work", "worse"). "St." in fast speech = "sint" or even "snt" — the vowel almost disappears. "John's" = JONZ. Herbal name: Hypericum perforatum. St. John's Wort = herbal antidepressant available OTC. Critical interactions: (1) enzyme inducer (like rifampicin) → reduces effectiveness of warfarin, contraceptives, digoxin, ciclosporin; (2) serotonin activity → risk of serotonin syndrome with SSRIs. Patients must be asked about it explicitly because they often don't consider it a "medicine".
Example 1
St. John's Wort is widely available in health food shops, but it can cause serious interactions with prescription medications — including antidepressants, warfarin, and the contraceptive pill.
Example 2
"Are you taking any herbal products at the moment — including St. John's Wort? It may look natural and harmless, but it can significantly affect how your other medicines work."
📌 St. John's Wort interactions — OET counselling must-knows:
+ SSRIs → serotonin syndrome  |  + warfarin → reduced anticoagulation  |  + OCP → reduced contraception  |  + digoxin → reduced levels
PHRASE
"I can see you're struggling with your mood"
I can SEE you're STRUG-ling with your MOOD
struggling /ˈstrʌɡlɪŋ/ | mood /muːd/
🎵 Pitch: rises to SEE, dips, rises to STRUG-ling, then final peak on MOOD. Three peaks create a gentle, rhythmic empathetic pattern — not too fast, not too flat. "Struggling" = STRUG-ling (2 syllables in fast speech; the middle "-gl-" cluster is smooth). "Mood" = long /uː/ — give it full length.
OET EMPATHY OPENER — signals emotional acknowledgement before clinical questions. This phrase: (1) validates the patient's emotional state; (2) opens the door to mental health discussion without labelling or diagnosing; (3) uses "struggling" (not "suffering" or "depressed") — softer, less clinical. Key OET speaking skill: reflecting the patient's language back to them. "Struggling with your mood" is more neutral than "you seem depressed" — it doesn't assume a diagnosis. Follow with: "Would you be comfortable telling me a little more about how things have been?"
OET consultation opening
"I can see you're struggling with your mood at the moment — I want you to know that what you're going through is valid, and there are things we can do to help."
Transition to medication counselling
"I can see you're struggling with your mood, and your doctor has prescribed sertraline to help with that — before you start, I'd like to go through a few important points."
📌 OET empathy opener pattern ("I can see..." / "I understand..."):
"I can see this has been difficult for you."  |  "I understand this must be worrying."  |  "It sounds like you've been going through a lot."
PHRASES
"Anaphylaxis can happen unexpectedly, so being prepared is life-saving" / "When eating out, always tell the restaurant or chef about your severe peanut allergy"
an-uh-fi-LAK-sis | un-ik-SPEK-tid-lee | LYFE-say-ving | PEE-nut AL-ur-jee
anaphylaxis /ˌænəfɪˈlæksɪs/ | unexpectedly /ˌʌnɪkˈspektɪdli/ | life-saving /ˈlaɪfseɪvɪŋ/
🎵 "Anaphylaxis": an-uh-fi-LAK-sis — single peak on "LAK" (4th syllable). "Unexpectedly": un-ik-SPEK-tid-lee — peak on "SPEK". "Life-saving": LYFE-say-ving — peak on "LYFE". "Peanut allergy": PEE-nut AL-ur-jee — two peaks. "Allergy" = AL-ur-jee (3 syllables, stress on 1st; British RP: "er" = /ə/, no "r" sound).
⚠️ "Anaphylaxis" = an-uh-fi-LAK-sis (5 syllables, stress on 4th "LAK"). Common error: "ana-phi-LAX-is" (wrong vowel in last syllable — it's "-sis" not "-xis"). "Unexpectedly" = un-ik-SPEK-tid-lee (5 syllables). "Life-saving" = compound adjective — stress on first element "LYFE". "Allergy" = AL-ur-jee (British RP: "er" = /ə/ schwa, no "r"). OET anaphylaxis counselling essentials: always carry adrenaline auto-injector (EpiPen); know how to use it; inform people around you; wear medical alert bracelet; call 999 after use; check food labels; inform restaurants.
OET counselling — anaphylaxis preparedness
"Anaphylaxis can happen unexpectedly, so being prepared is life-saving — please make sure you always carry your adrenaline auto-injector and that the people around you know how to use it."
OET counselling — eating out
"When eating out, always tell the restaurant or chef about your severe peanut allergy — do not assume they will remember, and do not be embarrassed to ask about ingredients."
📌 Anaphylaxis action plan — OET counselling checklist:
carry EpiPen always  |  know how to use it / train others  |  call 999 after use  |  wear medical alert ID  |  check food labels  |  inform restaurants  |  avoid trigger foods

PHRASE
"X-ray machine at airport security may alarm if carrying insulin supplies"
EX-ray muh-SHEEN | AIR-port SEE-kyuh-ri-tee | AL-arm | IN-syuh-lin suh-PLYz
Key words: X-ray /ˈeksreɪ/ | alarm /əˈlɑːm/ | insulin /ˈɪnsjʊlɪn/ | supplies /səˈplaɪz/
🎵 Pitch: 4 key peaks — EX-ray / AL-arm / IN-syuh-lin / suh-PLY-z. Each content word gets its own peak. "At airport security" runs low between peaks. "May" is unstressed (modal of possibility). "Alarm" = uh-LARM — stress on 2nd syllable; British RP "ar" = /ɑː/ (no "r" sound).
⚠️ "X-ray" = EX-ray (stress on EX). "Alarm" = uh-LARM (stress on 2nd syllable, British RP "ar" = /ɑː/ — same as "arm", "farm", "calm"). "Insulin" = IN-syuh-lin (British RP has /j/ glide: "syuh" not "soo"). "Supplies" = suh-PLYZE (stress on 2nd syllable). OET pharmacy context: patients with diabetes travelling internationally need counselling on: carrying medical letter, keeping insulin in hand luggage (not hold — temperature), airport security screening, time zone adjustments for dose timing. The X-ray machine itself does NOT damage insulin, but metal in pens/devices may trigger alarms.
Example 1 — patient counselling
"You should be aware that the X-ray machine at airport security may alarm when you carry your insulin supplies — I'd recommend getting a letter from your GP explaining your condition."
Example 2
Always carry insulin supplies in hand luggage — the hold can reach temperatures that damage insulin, and you'll need access during the flight.
📌 British RP "ar" = /ɑː/ — always long, never rhotic:
alarm → uh-LARM  |  pharmacy → FAR-muh-see  |  heart → HART (no "r" sound)
WORD
RESISTANCE
ri-ZIS-tunts 3 syllables
British (RP): /rɪˈzɪstəns/
🎵 Pitch: low-HIGH-low — peak on "ZIS", falls on "-tunts". "Ri-" is a very short, unstressed schwa-like syllable. "-tunts" ends with a clear "-nts" consonant cluster — don't drop the "n": it's "-tunts" not "-tuts". The "s" in "resistance" is voiced /z/ (like "zebra") — "ri-ZIS-tunts".
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "ZIS". The initial "re-/ri-" reduces to a schwa /rɪ/. The "s" = voiced /z/ — "ri-ZIS" not "ri-SIS". Clinical meanings: (1) antibiotic/drug resistance — bacteria or the body no longer responding to medication; (2) resistance in physics — opposition to electrical current (relevant for defibrillators, ECG); (3) resistance training — exercise. In OET: most commonly refers to drug resistance (e.g. rifampicin resistance in TB) or the pharmacological concept of enzyme induction reducing drug effectiveness.
Example 1 — drug resistance
Incomplete courses of antibiotics can contribute to the development of resistance, making future infections harder to treat.
Example 2 — enzyme induction context
Rifampicin does not cause resistance to contraceptives directly — rather, it speeds up their metabolism so the effective dose in the body is reduced.
📌 Same "-ance/-ence" suffix pattern (stress on syllable before the suffix):
resistance → ri-ZIS-tunts  |  compliance → kum-PLY-unts  |  adherence → ad-HEER-unts
WORD
CONTRACEPTIVE
kon-truh-SEP-tiv 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌkɒntrəˈseptɪv/
🎵 Pitch: low-low-HIGH-low — peak on "SEP", falls on "-tiv". "Kon-truh" builds quietly; "-tiv" is very short. "Contra-" prefix = KON-truh (stress on 1st of the prefix, but this is secondary — main stress is still "SEP"). Don't say "kon-TRA-sep-tiv".
⚠️ Stress on 3rd syllable "SEP". "Contra-" = KON-truh (the "a" reduces to schwa). "-ceptive" = -SEP-tiv. Common error: stressing "CONTRA-" or making it 5 syllables. It's 4 syllables: KON-truh-SEP-tiv. OET pharmacy: key counselling point — rifampicin is a potent enzyme inducer that significantly reduces plasma levels of combined oral contraceptives (COC). Patients must be counselled to use additional contraception (e.g. condoms) for the duration of rifampicin treatment AND for 4 weeks after stopping.
Example 1
Rifampicin can reduce the effectiveness of the oral contraceptive pill — you should use an additional method of contraception while taking it and for four weeks afterwards.
Example 2
"Your contraceptive pill may not work as effectively during this course of treatment — this is a well-known interaction that we need to discuss."
📌 Same "contra-" prefix (= against) in medical terms:
contraceptive → kon-truh-SEP-tiv  |  contraindication → kon-truh-in-di-KAY-shun  |  contralateral → kon-truh-LAT-uh-rul
WORD
RIFAMPICIN
ri-FAM-pi-sin 4 syllables
British (RP): /rɪˈfæmpɪsɪn/
🎵 Pitch: low-HIGH-low-low — peak on "FAM", then falls through "-pi-sin". "FAM" = short /æ/ vowel as in "cat", "bad". "-pi-sin" = two very light syllables, almost murmured. The whole word should flow as one phrase: ri-FAM-pi-sin.
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "FAM". The vowel /æ/ in "FAM" = same as "cat", "back", "lamp" — short, open, front vowel. "-pi-sin" = /pɪsɪn/ — both "i"s are short /ɪ/. Note: American English uses "rifampin" (ri-FAM-pin, 3 syllables) — OET uses British "rifampicin" (4 syllables). Critical OET pharmacy knowledge: rifampicin is a potent enzyme inducer → reduces plasma levels of warfarin, oral contraceptives, HIV antiretrovirals, corticosteroids, and many others. First-line TB drug — always in combination (RIPE regimen).
Example 1
Rifampicin is a potent inducer of hepatic enzymes and can significantly reduce the plasma concentrations of many co-administered drugs.
Example 2
"Before I dispense this, I need to check your other medications — rifampicin is known to interact with a large number of drugs, including the contraceptive pill and warfarin."
📌 TB drug regimen — pronunciation of the "RIPE" drugs:
Rifampicin → ri-FAM-pi-sin  |  Isoniazid → eye-soh-NY-uh-zid  |  Pyrazinamide → peer-uh-ZIN-uh-myd  |  Ethambutol → eth-AM-byoo-tol
WORD
TUBERCULOSIS
tyoo-bur-kyoo-LOH-sis 5 syllables
British (RP): /tjuːˌbɜːkjʊˈləʊsɪs/
🎵 Pitch: low-low-low-HIGH-low — single peak on "LOH", then falls on "-sis". Long build-up through "tyoo-bur-kyoo" before the peak. British RP: "tyoo-" with /j/ glide (not "too-"). "Bur-" = /bɜː/ — non-rhotic, same vowel as "fur", "her". Don't stress any of the first three syllables.
⚠️ Stress on 4th syllable "LOH". British RP: "tu-" = TYOO (with /j/ glide). "Bur-" = BUR /bɜː/ — same as "fur" (non-rhotic, no "r" sound). "Kyoo" = /kjuː/. "-LOH-sis" = same "-osis" pattern. Abbreviated as TB. Often referred to as "TB" in conversation — both forms appear in OET. Key clinical facts for OET: caused by Mycobacterium tuberculosis; treated with combination therapy (6 months minimum); rifampicin is a cornerstone drug; drug-resistant TB (MDR-TB) is a growing concern; notifiable disease in the UK.
Example 1
Tuberculosis is treated with a combination of antibiotics over at least six months — stopping the course early is a major cause of treatment failure and drug resistance.
Example 2
"The medication you've been prescribed for your tuberculosis includes rifampicin, which is known to interact with several other drugs — are you taking anything else regularly?"
📌 Same "-osis" + 5-syllable pattern (stress on 4th syllable):

PHRASE
REDUCES EFFECTIVENESS
ri-DYOO-siz i-FEK-tiv-nis 6 syllables total
British (RP): /rɪˈdjuːsɪz ɪˈfektɪvnɪs/
🎵 Pitch: low-HIGH-low | low-HIGH-low-low — two peaks: "DYOO" and "FEK". "Reduces" peaks on 2nd syllable; "effectiveness" peaks on 2nd syllable. The two peaks create a clear two-beat rhythm. British RP: "reduces" = ri-DYOO-siz (with /j/ glide — "dyoo" not "doo").
⚠️ "Reduces" = ri-DYOO-siz (British RP /j/ glide in "-duce"). "Effectiveness" = i-FEK-tiv-nis (4 syllables, stress on 2nd). Common error: "ef-FEC-tive-ness" — wrong syllable count or stress. This phrase is essential OET pharmacy language for explaining drug interactions. Structure: "[Drug A] reduces the effectiveness of [Drug B]." This is more precise than "makes it less effective" — use in OET writing and speaking. Mechanism here: enzyme induction → faster metabolism → lower plasma drug levels → reduced therapeutic effect.
Example 1
Rifampicin reduces the effectiveness of the oral contraceptive pill by inducing liver enzymes that break it down more rapidly.
Example 2
"I need to let you know that this antibiotic reduces the effectiveness of your contraceptive — you'll need to use an additional method for the duration of treatment."
📌 British RP /j/ glide in "-duce" words:
reduces → ri-DYOO-siz  |  induces → in-DYOO-siz  |  produces → pruh-DYOO-siz  |  (American: "ri-DOOZ-iz" — no /j/)

INSULIN ADMINISTRATION

🎵 Pitch: IN-syuh-lin (peak on 1st) | ad-min-i-STRAY-shun (peak on 4th syllable "STRAY"). Two word-peaks. "Insulin" peaks early; "administration" builds a long approach before its peak. "Administration" = 6 syllables — the longest word in this pair.
⚠️ "Insulin" = IN-syuh-lin (British RP — /j/ glide: "syuh" not "soo"). 3 syllables. "Administration" = ad-min-i-STRAY-shun (6 syllables, stress on 4th). The "-tion" = "-shun" as always. OET pharmacy insulin counselling topics: injection technique (90° angle, correct site rotation), timing relative to meals (rapid-acting vs basal), storage (fridge vs room temp), disposal of sharps, hypoglycaemia recognition and management.
Example 1
Correct insulin administration technique — including site rotation and proper needle length — helps prevent lipodystrophy and ensures consistent absorption.
Example 2
"Can you walk me through your insulin administration routine? I'd like to check that the technique and timing are working well for you."

INSULIN SUPPLIES
IN-syuh-lin suh-PLYz 5 syllables total

⚠️ "Supplies" = suh-PLYZE (stress on 2nd syllable, voiced /z/ at the end). The "su-" is a schwa — completely unstressed. "Supplies" (plural noun) = items/stock needed for ongoing use. insulin (vials/cartridges) | insulin pens | pen needles | syringes | glucose monitor | test strips | lancets | glucagon kit | sharps bin | medical letter
WORD
SUPPLEMENT
SUP-li-munt 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈsʌplɪmənt/
🎵 Pitch: HIGH-low-low — peak on "SUP", then falls through two light syllables. "SUP" = /sʌ/ — same vowel as "cup", "blood", "enough". "-li-" = /lɪ/ (short i). "-munt" = schwa + "nt". Three syllables, only the first matters for stress.
⚠️ Stress on 1st syllable "SUP". Vowel = /ʌ/ ("cup" vowel). "-ment" suffix reduces to "-munt" (schwa). Critical OET vocabulary note: "supplement" has TWO distinct clinical meanings that patients often confuse — (1) dietary supplement = vitamins, minerals, herbal products (NOT prescription); (2) supplement as in "supplementary" = additional/extra (e.g. "supplementary oxygen"). When a patient says "I take supplements," always clarify: "Do you mean vitamins or over-the-counter products?" This distinction matters for drug interactions (e.g. St John's Wort is a supplement that also induces enzymes like rifampicin).
Example 1 — vitamins/dietary
She was taking a vitamin D supplement and a fish oil capsule daily — neither of which interacted with her prescribed medications.
Example 2 — clarification in counselling
"When you say you take supplements, do you mean vitamins or herbal products? Some natural products — like St John's Wort — can interact with prescription medications."
📌 Supplement ≠ prescription medication — OET clarification table:
"supplement" → vitamins, minerals, herbal (OTC)  |  "medication" → prescription drug  |  "OTC product" → over-the-counter (pharmacy without prescription)

📚 Previous Batch — Gastroscopy Consultation Language (Set 7)

📌 Newest words always appear here first. Scroll down to review older entries.
PHRASE
SCHEDULE AN ENDOSCOPY
SKED-yool an en-DOS-kuh-pee 7 syllables total
British (RP): /ˈʃedjuːl/ or /ˈskedjuːl/ + /enˈdɒskəpi/
🎵 Pitch: SKED/SHED-yool (peak on 1st syllable) then en-DOS-kuh-pee (peak on 2nd). Two separate word-peaks. "Schedule" in British RP: traditionally SHED-yool, though SKED-yool is now widely accepted. For OET, either is fine — just be consistent.
⚠️ Two pronunciations of "schedule" are both acceptable in British RP: (1) traditional: SHED-yool /ˈʃedjuːl/; (2) increasingly common: SKED-yool /ˈskedjuːl/. American English only uses SKED. "Endoscopy" = en-DOS-kuh-pee (stress on 2nd syllable "DOS"). The "-scopy" suffix always has stress on the syllable before it. Clinical use: "schedule" = to arrange/book a procedure for a specific time. In OET speaking: "I'd like to schedule an endoscopy for you" = standard clinical recommendation language.
Example 1
Given your symptoms and history, I'd like to schedule an endoscopy to examine the lining of your stomach directly.
Example 2
"We'll schedule an endoscopy for next week — the procedure itself takes about fifteen minutes and is done under mild sedation."
📌 Same "-scopy" stress pattern (stress on syllable before "-scopy"):
endoscopy → en-DOS-kuh-pee  |  colonoscopy → koh-lon-OS-kuh-pee  |  bronchoscopy → bron-KOS-kuh-pee
PHRASE
LINING OF THE STOMACH
LY-ning uv thuh STUM-uk 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˈlaɪnɪŋ əv ðə ˈstʌmək/
🎵 Pitch: LY-ning (peak on 1st) then STUM-uk (peak on 1st of "stomach"). "Of the" reduces completely to "uv-thuh" — almost inaudible. Two clear peaks bookend the phrase. "Stomach" = STUM-uk — the "ach" at the end is /ək/ (schwa + k), not "-atch".
⚠️ "Stomach" = STUM-uk /ˈstʌmək/ — the "ach" ending is /ək/ NOT "-atch". Common error: "STOM-atch" or "STOM-ik" — both wrong. The vowel in "sto-" = /ʌ/ as in "cup" (STUM). "Lining" = the mucous membrane layer. Clinical term: gastric mucosa. In OET: "lining of the stomach" is the patient-friendly version of "gastric mucosa" — use when explaining endoscopy to patients. "Of the" in fast speech = /əvðə/ — just a soft "uv-thuh".
Example 1
The endoscopy allows us to look directly at the lining of the stomach and check for any inflammation, ulcers, or abnormal tissue.
Example 2
Long-term use of NSAIDs can irritate the lining of the stomach, which is why we recommend taking them with food or switching to a gastroprotective agent.
📌 "Stomach" vowel — /ʌ/ same as:
stomach → STUM-uk  |  come → KUM  |  blood → BLUD (all /ʌ/ vowel despite different spellings)
PHRASE
TUMMY PAIN
TUM-ee PAYN 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈtʌmi peɪn/
🎵 Pitch: TUM-ee (peak, then dip) | PAYN (final peak, then fall). "Pain" carries the strongest final stress as the key noun. "Tummy" is the warm/informal opener. Together the phrase sounds soft and sympathetic — appropriate when speaking to children or anxious patients.
⚠️ "Tummy" = informal/child-friendly word for "stomach" or "abdomen". Used with children and in casual patient conversation. Clinical equivalent: "abdominal pain" or "stomach pain". In OET: when a patient says "tummy pain", the clinical note would write "abdominal pain" or "epigastric discomfort". As a pharmacist/clinician, you might mirror the patient's language ("So you've been having tummy pain?") to build rapport, but document clinically. "PAYN" = /peɪn/ — long diphthong /eɪ/ as in "rain", "vein".
Example 1 — patient language
"I've been getting this awful tummy pain right here — it comes on about twenty minutes after I eat."
Example 2 — clinician mirroring
