"So the tummy pain is worse after meals — can you describe what type of pain it is? Burning, stabbing, or more of a dull ache?"
📌 Patient language → clinical equivalent:
"tummy pain" → abdominal pain  |  "tummy" → abdomen / stomach  |  "tummy bug" → gastroenteritis
PHRASE
PINCHED NERVE
PINCHT NURV 2 syllables
British (RP): /pɪntʃt nɜːv/
🎵 Pitch: both words peak equally — PINCHT | NURV. Short, punchy two-word phrase. "Pinched" ends in a cluster: /ntʃt/ — four consonants in a row. Practise: "p-inch-t" → "PINCHT". British RP: "nerve" = NURV /nɜːv/ — non-rhotic, the "er" = /ɜː/ (same as "fur", "her", "word"), no "r" sound at the end.
⚠️ "Pinched" ends in a complex consonant cluster: /ntʃt/ — "n" + "ch" + "t". Common error: dropping the final "t" → "PINCH-d" — make sure the "t" is audible. "Nerve" = NURV — British RP non-rhotic: "er" = /ɜː/, no "r" sound. Clinical term: nerve compression / nerve entrapment / radiculopathy. Patient language: "I think I've got a pinched nerve in my back/neck/shoulder." Common causes: herniated disc, bone spur, repetitive strain.
Example 1
"I thought it might be a pinched nerve — the pain shoots down my arm whenever I turn my head to the left."
Example 2
The physiotherapist explained that what the patient described as a pinched nerve was more likely cervical nerve root compression at the C6 level.
📌 British RP non-rhotic "-er-" words (no "r" sound):
nerve → NURV  |  herb → URB (the "h" is also silent in British RP!)  |  refer → ri-FUR
PHRASE
HEATING PAD
HEET-ing PAD 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈhiːtɪŋ pæd/
🎵 Pitch: HEET-ing (peak on 1st syllable, "-ing" drops) then PAD (final peak, short vowel /æ/ as in "cat", "bad", "mad"). Both peaks are roughly equal but "PAD" gets a slight extra push as the key noun. "Heating" = HEET-ing — long /iː/ vowel in "HEET".
⚠️ "Heating" = HEET-ing (long /iː/ vowel). "Pad" = PAD — short /æ/ vowel ("cat" vowel, NOT "paid" /eɪ/). British English also says "hot water bottle" for the same purpose — the two are near-synonyms in patient conversation. "Heating pad" = electric heated pad; "hot water bottle" = traditional rubber bottle. In OET: heating pad/hot water bottle = symptomatic relief for musculoskeletal pain, period pain, or abdominal discomfort — a home remedy patients often mention before seeking professional help.
Example 1
"I've been using a heating pad on my lower back, which helps a little, but the pain always comes back once I take it off."
Example 2
She had been managing the muscle spasm with a heating pad and over-the-counter ibuprofen, but the relief was only temporary.
📌 British English near-synonyms for heat therapy:
"heating pad" = electric  |  "hot water bottle" = traditional  |  "heat pack" = disposable chemical
PHRASE
MAGNETISM (FROM THE MACHINE)
MAG-ni-tiz-um 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈmæɡnɪtɪzəm/
🎵 Pitch: MAG-ni-tiz-um — single peak on "MAG", then falls through three light syllables. "MAG" = /mæɡ/ — short /æ/ vowel as in "bad", "cat". The "-tiz-" syllable is very fast. "-um" = schwa. Don't add extra stress to "-tiz".
⚠️ Stress on 1st syllable "MAG". 4 syllables: MAG-ni-tiz-um. "-tism" ending reduces to "-tiz-um" (the "i" in "-tiz" is short /ɪ/). In OET pharmacy/clinical context: "magnetism from the machine" = the strong magnetic field generated by an MRI (magnetic resonance imaging) scanner. Key clinical point: patients with ferromagnetic metal implants (certain pacemakers, aneurysm clips, cochlear implants) cannot enter the MRI room due to the powerful magnetic field. Pharmacists may counsel patients on MRI-safe medication patches vs non-MRI-safe ones (e.g. some transdermal patches contain metal foil).
Example 1 — patient explaining concern
"Can I have an MRI? I've heard that the magnetism from the machine can be dangerous if you have metal in your body."
Example 2 — clinician explaining
"The magnetism in an MRI scanner is extremely powerful — which is why we need to check for any metal implants before you go in."
📌 Same "-ism" suffix pattern (stress on root, "-ism" always unstressed):
magnetism → MAG-ni-tiz-um  |  metabolism → muh-TAB-uh-liz-um  |  embolism → EM-buh-liz-um
PHRASE
"I've gone through your notes"
I've GONE through your NOTES
Stress: GONE + NOTES
🎵 Pitch: rises to "GONE", dips through "through your", rises to "NOTES" and falls. Two peaks — "GONE" (verb) and "NOTES" (noun). "Through your" reduces heavily: "thruh-yuh" or even "thruh". The falling pitch on "NOTES" signals this is an opening statement — what follows is the clinical discussion.
🔊 Fast speech sounds like:
"Iv-GONE-thruh-yuh-NOTES" — "through your" almost disappears
OET OPENING PHRASE — used by clinicians at the start of a consultation to signal they have reviewed the patient's records. "Gone through" = phrasal verb meaning "reviewed/read carefully". Establishes: (1) the clinician is prepared; (2) the patient doesn't need to repeat everything from scratch. OET speaking tip: always open consultations with something like this — it immediately sets a professional, caring tone. Follow with: "...and I can see you've been having [symptom] for some time."
OET consultation opener
"I've gone through your notes, and I can see you've been experiencing stomach pain for quite a while now — can you tell me more about how it's been lately?"
"I've gone through your notes from your last visit — it looks like the dosage was adjusted in March, is that right?"
📌 Same OET consultation openers:
"I've had a look at your records..."  |  "I've reviewed what your GP sent over..."  |  "I can see from your notes that..."
PHRASES
"The last sort of month, it's been more and more" / "the worst has been in the last month"
the last SORT of MONTH | MORE and MORE | the WORST has been in the last MONTH
Key: "sort of" = hedging filler | "more and more" = gradual escalation | "the worst" = superlative peak
🎵 "Sort of month" — "SORT" gets a slight peak; "MONTH" gets the main peak. "Sort of" is a hedge — it softens the time estimate. "More and more" — both "MORE"s peak equally, creating a rhythmic rising pattern that mimics escalation. "The WORST has been in the last MONTH" — "WORST" peaks hard (superlative = always stressed), "MONTH" gets final stress.
🔊 Key patterns:
"sort of" = approximate hedge (≈ "about" / "roughly") | "more and more" = progressive worsening signal | "the worst" = escalation peak
Three patterns in one card — all from the same patient narrative about symptom escalation. OET LISTENING TRIGGER: "more and more" = symptoms are getting progressively worse — likely answer for a question about symptom trajectory. "The last sort of month" = approximately one month (hedge). "The worst has been in the last month" = clearest time frame — most likely the OET answer. Listen for the SUPERLATIVE ("worst", "most", "hardest") — it usually signals the key clinical information.
OET context
"I mean, long before that, the last six months have been tough, but the worst has been in the last month — it's getting to the point where I can barely eat a meal without the pain starting."
"The last sort of month it's been more and more — the dizziness used to happen occasionally, now it's almost every morning."
📌 Symptom escalation language — OET listening triggers:
"more and more" → getting worse  |  "the worst has been" → recent peak  |  "it's got to the point where" → functional impact
PHRASE
"I've been trying bits and pieces"
trying BITS and PEE-siz
"bits and pieces" = fixed idiom, /bɪts ən ˈpiːsɪz/
🎵 Pitch: "BITS" gets a light peak, "and" is very low (reduced to "un"), "PEE-" gets the main peak. "Pieces" = PEE-siz (2 syllables, stress on 1st, "-siz" is light). The phrase has a casual, slightly resigned quality — the patient has tried various things but nothing has worked well.
🔊 Fast speech sounds like:
"Iv-bin-TRY-ing-BITS-un-PEE-siz" — "and" → "un"
"Bits and pieces" = various things / a few different things (informal). Signals: the patient has self-managed with multiple remedies but without a systematic approach or strong results. OET clinical significance: follow-up question should be "What have you tried?" to establish prior medication use (important for drug interactions and OET answer boxes). Compare: "bits and pieces" (vague, informal) vs "various measures" (clinical equivalent).
OET context
"I've been trying bits and pieces — antacids from the chemist, cutting out coffee, trying not to eat too late — but nothing really works long-term."
"I've been trying bits and pieces for the joint pain — heat packs, paracetamol, resting it — none of it has made a lasting difference."
📌 Same "various attempts" expressions:
"this and that"  |  "various things"  |  "a few different things"  |  "all sorts of remedies"
PHRASES
"I've swallowed a knife and it's stabbing inside my tummy" / "something is on fire inside my tummy"
KNIFE / STAB-bing | FIRE
Key words: knife /naɪf/ | stabbing /ˈstæbɪŋ/ | fire /faɪə/
🎵 "KNIFE" and "STAB-bing" both get strong peaks — violent imagery always gets strong stress. "FIRE" gets an elongated peak with extra breath — the /aɪ/ diphthong in "fire" naturally stretches. Both metaphors use rising pitch on the key image word, then fall on "inside my tummy" (which is lower, almost matter-of-fact).
🔊 Pronunciation note:
"knife" = NYFE (silent k!) | "stabbing" = STAB-ing (double-b, short /æ/) | "fire" = FY-uh (2 syllables in British RP)
PAIN DESCRIPTION METAPHORS — extremely important for OET listening. Patients almost never say "I have sharp epigastric pain with a burning quality." They say things like these. Your job: decode the metaphor → identify the clinical pain type. "Swallowed a knife / stabbing" = sharp, stabbing pain → clinical: "sharp epigastric pain". "Something on fire" = burning sensation → clinical: "burning/pyrosis" → likely acid-related (GORD, gastritis, peptic ulcer). "KNIFE" — silent "k"! /naɪf/ — the "k" in "kn-" is always silent in English (knife, know, knock, knee).
OET context — patient describes pain
"It's like I've swallowed a knife and it's stabbing inside my tummy — and then there's also this burning feeling, like something is on fire inside."
Clinical interpretation
Patient describes sharp stabbing epigastric pain with associated burning sensation — consistent with peptic ulcer disease or severe gastritis.
📌 Patient metaphor → clinical pain type (OET decoding table):
"stabbing / knife" → sharp pain  |  "on fire / burning" → pyrosis/burning pain  |  "dull ache" → dull pain  |  "squeezing / crushing" → pressure pain (cardiac?)
PHRASE
"I haven't pinpointed anything yet"
I haven't PIN-poynd-id AN-ee-thing yet
pinpointed: /ˈpɪnpɔɪntɪd/
🎵 Pitch: rises to "PIN-" (strong — this is the key verb), dips through "-pointd", rises to "AN-" (negative is always stressed in English), then "yet" gets a slight final rise — signalling the situation might change. "Pinpointed" = PIN-poy-ntid (3 syllables, stress on 1st).
🔊 Fast speech sounds like:
"I-AV-nt-PIN-poynd-id-EN-ee-thing-yet" — "haven't" → "AV-nt"
"Pinpoint" (verb) = to identify precisely / to locate exactly. "I haven't pinpointed anything yet" = I haven't been able to identify a specific trigger/cause. Clinically signals: the patient has been trying to find a pattern but hasn't succeeded. OET follow-up question: "Have you tried keeping a food diary?" or "Does it happen at a particular time of day?" Compare to "I haven't put my finger on it yet" — same meaning, different idiom.
OET context
"Despite tracking my triggers carefully, I haven't pinpointed anything yet that reliably brings on the headaches."
"I've been keeping a food diary for two weeks, but I haven't pinpointed anything yet — the pain seems to come on at random, not just after certain foods."
📌 Same "identify precisely" expressions:
"I can't put my finger on it"  |  "I haven't worked out what triggers it"  |  "I haven't been able to identify a pattern"
PHRASE
"It's a tube passed through your throat with a camera at the end"
it's a TUBE passed through your THROAT with a CAM-ra at the END
Key: tube /tjuːb/ | throat /θrəʊt/ | camera /ˈkæmrə/
🎵 Pitch: four peaks — TUBE / THROAT / CAM-ra / END. Each content word gets a peak; function words (it's a, passed, through, your, with a, at the) are all low. The phrase builds through the four peaks, landing with a final fall on "END" — which also signals the physical end of the scope.
🔊 Key pronunciations:
"tube" = TYOOB (British RP — with /j/ glide before "oo") | "throat" = THROHT (long /əʊ/) | "camera" = KAM-ruh (NOT "KAM-uh-ruh" — only 2 syllables in natural speech)
OET PATIENT EXPLANATION PHRASE — the plain-English explanation of an endoscopy. In OET speaking: when you recommend a procedure, you MUST explain it in simple terms. This is the model patient-friendly explanation for an upper GI endoscopy (gastroscopy). Note: "tube" in British RP = TYOOB (with /j/ glide) not "TOOB" (American). "Camera" in fast speech = KAM-ruh (2 syllables) — the middle syllable disappears. "Throat" = THROHT — long /əʊ/ diphthong.
OET context
"What we'd like to do is a procedure called an endoscopy — it's a tube passed through your throat with a camera at the end, which lets us look directly at the lining of your stomach."
"Don't worry, it's just a tube passed through your throat with a camera at the end — you'll be sedated, so you won't feel much at all."
📌 British RP "-tube-" words with /j/ glide:
tube → TYOOB  |  tune → TYOON  |  due → DYOO  |  (American English drops the /j/: "TOOB", "TOON", "DOO")
PHRASE
"We'll reassess"
we'll ree-uh-SES
British (RP): /wiːl ˌriːəˈses/
🎵 Pitch: "we'll" is very light (reduced to "wul" or "wil"), then rises to "SES" — a single clean peak at the end. The falling pitch after "SES" signals finality — this is a closing statement. Short and decisive. In full: "We'll reassess [at that point / in two weeks / after the results]" — the added time frame usually gets a secondary peak.
🔊 Fast speech sounds like:
"wil-ree-uh-SES" — "we'll" reduces to "wil"; "re-a-" merges to "ree-uh"
OET CLOSING PHRASE — used to end a clinical encounter by signalling that the decision is not final and the patient will be reviewed. "Reassess" = ree-uh-SES (stress on final syllable "SES"). The "re-" prefix = REE (long /iː/). Clinical use: always follow "we'll reassess" with a specific time or trigger: "we'll reassess in two weeks", "we'll reassess once the results are back", "we'll reassess if symptoms don't improve." In OET: this phrase signals the consultation is ending — the clinician is closing the loop professionally.
Example 1
"Start the medication as prescribed, and come back in two weeks — we'll reassess at that point and decide whether to adjust the dose."
Example 2
"We'll reassess once the endoscopy results are back — that should give us a much clearer picture of what's going on."
📌 Same OET consultation-closing phrases:
"We'll review this in..."  |  "Let's see how you get on and..."  |  "Come back and see me if..."  |  "We'll take it from there"
PHRASE
"To be precise"
to be pri-SYCE
British (RP): /tə biː prɪˈsaɪs/
🎵 Pitch: "to be" is very low (almost inaudible at natural speed), single strong peak on "SYCE" (the final syllable). The phrase functions as a parenthetical — it's inserted mid-sentence with a slight pause before and after. The peak on "SYCE" carries a slightly careful, correcting quality.
🔊 Fast speech sounds like:
"tuh-bee-prih-SYCE" — "to be" merges into one quiet "tuh-bee"
"To be precise" = a self-correction or clarification marker — signals the speaker is about to give a more exact figure or detail. "It takes about twenty minutes — thirty, to be precise." OET significance: (1) In LISTENING — when you hear "to be precise", the number/detail that follows is usually the correct answer, not the estimate before it. (2) In SPEAKING — use it to self-correct naturally: "I've had it for about a month — three weeks, to be precise." It sounds professional and accurate.
OET listening trigger
"The pain starts about twenty minutes after eating — thirty minutes, to be precise." [→ Answer: 30 minutes, NOT 20]
OET speaking use
"The course lasts about a week — seven days, to be precise — so make sure you finish all the tablets even if you feel better sooner."
📌 OET listening rule — self-correction markers override first estimate:
"to be precise" → take the 2nd figure  |  "actually" → take what follows  |  "I mean" → take the correction  |  "or rather" → take what follows

📚 Previous Batch — Neurology / Oral Health (Set 6)

📌 Newest words always appear here first. Scroll down to review older entries.
WORD
CONVERSION
kun-VUR-zhun 3 syllables
British (RP): /kənˈvɜːʃən/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low — peak on "VUR", falls on "-zhun". The "con-" prefix fully reduces to "kun-" (schwa). "VUR" = British RP /vɜː/ — same vowel as "fur", "nurse", "word". Don't add an extra syllable: 3 syllables only.
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "VUR". The "con-" reduces to a schwa ("kun-"). The "-sion" ending = "-zhun" (voiced /ʒ/ like the "s" in "measure", "vision") — NOT "-shun". This voiced vs voiceless distinction is a subtle but real difference: conver-ZHUN (voiced), not conver-SHUN (voiceless). Clinical meanings: (1) conversion disorder = neurological symptoms without organic cause (formerly "hysteria"); (2) conversion = changing a drug from IV to oral; (3) religious/belief conversion (non-clinical). In OET neurology: "conversion" most often refers to conversion disorder.
Example 1 — conversion disorder
After all organic causes were excluded, the neurologist considered a diagnosis of conversion disorder — neurological symptoms arising from psychological distress.
Example 2 — IV to oral conversion
The patient was stable enough for conversion from intravenous to oral antibiotics on day three.
📌 "-sion" voiced /ʒ/ vs "-tion" voiceless /ʃ/ — key distinction:
conversion → kun-VUR-zhun (voiced)  |  constipation → kon-sti-PAY-shun (voiceless)  |  vision → VIZH-un (voiced)
WORD
CONVULSION
kun-VUL-shun 3 syllables
British (RP): /kənˈvʌlʃən/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low — peak on "VUL", falls on "-shun". Almost identical rhythm to "conversion" — both are 3-syllable words with stress on the 2nd syllable. The key difference: "VUL" uses the /ʌ/ vowel (as in "cup", "but"), not the /ɜː/ of "VUR" in conversion.
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "VUL". Vowel = /ʌ/ — short, open sound ("but", "cup", "blood"). Don't confuse with "conversion" (VUR = /vɜː/) — different vowel entirely. "-sion" here = "-shun" (voiceless /ʃ/) — unlike "conversion" which uses voiced /ʒ/. Common errors: (1) "CON-vul-shun" (stressing 1st syllable); (2) mixing up the vowel with "conversion". Convulsion = involuntary rhythmic muscle contractions = seizure activity. "Febrile convulsion" (triggered by fever in children) = very common OET paediatric scenario.
Example 1
The child experienced a febrile convulsion during a high fever, which resolved spontaneously within two minutes.
Example 2
She was placed in the recovery position during the convulsion and monitored closely until the episode subsided.
📌 conversion vs convulsion — vowel contrast (easy to confuse!):
conVERsion → /vɜː/ = "VUR" (same as "fur")  |  conVULsion → /vʌ/ = "VUL" (same as "cull")  |  Both: stress on 2nd syllable, "con-" = schwa
WORD
HALITOSIS
hal-i-TOH-sis 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌhælɪˈtəʊsɪs/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-HIGH-low — peak on "TOH", falls on "-sis". "Hal-i" is quick and light. "TOH" = long /əʊ/ diphthong (British RP "oh"). The "-sis" ending is very short. Say it in one breath: hal-i-TOH-sis.
⚠️ Stress on 3rd syllable "TOH". Same "-osis" suffix rule as thrombosis, fibrosis, diagnosis. The "h" in "halitosis" IS pronounced (unlike "hour" or "honest"). "Hal-" = /hæl/ — same vowel as "pal", "cal". Common error: "huh-LI-toh-sis" — stressing the 2nd syllable. Halitosis = chronic bad breath. Causes: poor oral hygiene, gum disease (periodontitis), dry mouth (xerostomia), tonsil stones, GORD, systemic conditions (diabetic ketoacidosis = acetone breath; renal failure = ammonia breath). The patient term is simply "bad breath".
Example 1
The patient presented with persistent halitosis that had not improved despite regular brushing — a dental referral was arranged to assess for gum disease.
Example 2
"In some cases, halitosis can indicate a systemic condition such as acid reflux or poorly controlled diabetes — it's worth investigating further."
📌 Same "-osis" suffix (stress on syllable before "-sis"):
thrombosis → throm-BOH-sis  |  fibrosis → fy-BROH-sis  |  diagnosis → dy-ug-NOH-sis
PHRASE
UNDERLYING CAUSE
un-dur-LY-ing KAWZ 5 syllables total
British (RP): /ˌʌndəˈlaɪɪŋ kɔːz/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-HIGH-low | HIGH — peak on "LY-ing", then a final peak on "KAWZ". "Cause" often carries the most final emphasis as the key noun. "Under-" is light and quick; "-lying" rises. British RP: "cause" = /kɔːz/ — same vowel as "jaw", "law" (non-rhotic "aw" sound, NOT "korz").
⚠️ "Underlying" = un-dur-LY-ing (stress on 3rd syllable "LY"). "Cause" = KAWZ (British RP — silent r, long /ɔː/ vowel). Together: un-dur-LY-ing KAWZ. This is one of the most important phrases in clinical English — used constantly in OET speaking, writing, and listening. Meaning: the root/original cause beneath the surface symptom. Clinical principle: always treat the underlying cause, not just the symptom. Common OET counselling structure: "This medication treats the symptom, but to address the underlying cause, we need to..."
Example 1
"The mouthwash will help with the bad breath in the short term, but we really need to identify and treat the underlying cause."
Example 2
Treating symptoms without addressing the underlying cause is unlikely to result in lasting improvement.
📌 Same clinical "root cause" expressions:
"root cause"  |  "primary cause"  |  "what's driving the symptom"  |  "the source of the problem"
PHRASE
ORAL HYGIENE
AW-rul HY-jeen 4 syllables total
British (RP): /ˈɔːrəl ˈhaɪdʒiːn/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low | HIGH-low — two separate peaks, one per word. In a sentence, "hygiene" often gets the final emphasis as the key noun: "good ORAL hyGIENE" — but as a fixed phrase alone, both words peak equally. "Hygiene" = HY-jeen (2 syllables — NOT "hy-gee-en").
⚠️ "Oral" = AW-rul (British RP — the "or" = /ɔː/ non-rhotic, same as "aural"!). Note: "oral" and "aural" are near-homophones in British RP — context makes the difference. "Hygiene" = HY-jeen — only 2 syllables. Common error: "HY-jee-een" (3 syllables). The "g" is silent — it's "HY-jeen", not "HY-gee-een". Also: "hygienic" = hy-JEE-nik (3 syllables, stress shifts). Patient equivalent: "keeping your mouth clean" / "brushing and flossing". Covers: brushing technique, flossing, tongue cleaning, mouthwash use, dental check-up frequency.
Example 1
Poor oral hygiene is the most common cause of halitosis and can usually be improved with consistent brushing, flossing, and regular dental check-ups.
Example 2
"I'd recommend starting with an oral hygiene review — sometimes small changes to your brushing technique can make a significant difference."
📌 "Hygiene" family — silent "g", always /dʒiː/ not /giː/:
hygiene → HY-jeen  |  hygienic → hy-JEE-nik  |  hygienist → hy-JEE-nist
WORD
ANTIBACTERIAL
an-tee-bak-TEER-ee-ul 6 syllables
British (RP): /ˌæntɪbækˈtɪəriəl/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-low-HIGH-low-low — single peak on "TEER". "Anti-bak" builds quickly and quietly before the peak. The "-ial" ending = "-ee-ul" (2 very light syllables). Don't add extra stress to "AN" or "BAK".
⚠️ Stress on 4th syllable "TEER". "Anti-" = AN-tee (light secondary stress on "AN"). "Bacterial" alone = bak-TEER-ee-ul (stress on 2nd syllable "TEER"). Combined: the stress stays on "TEER". The "-ial" ending = /ɪəl/ = "-ee-ul" — two unstressed syllables. Common error: equal stress on all syllables (robotic pattern). Antibacterial = kills or inhibits bacterial growth. Used for: mouthwash (chlorhexidine), soap, cream, toothpaste. Distinguished from "antibiotic" (systemic drug) — antibacterial can refer to topical or mild products too.
Example 1
An antibacterial mouthwash containing chlorhexidine may help to reduce the bacterial load responsible for halitosis.
Example 2
"This antibacterial gel can be applied directly to the affected area — it won't cure the infection, but it will help keep the bacteria under control."
📌 Same "anti-" + root stress pattern (4th syllable peak in 6-syllable words):
anticoagulant → an-tee-koh-AG-yuh-lunt  |  antidepressant → an-tee-di-PRES-unt  |  antispasmodic → an-tee-spaz-MOD-ik

📚 Previous Batch — Decongestant Counselling (Set 5)

📌 Newest words always appear here first. Scroll down to review older entries.
WORD
DECONGESTANT
dee-kun-JEST-unt 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌdiːkənˈdʒestənt/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-HIGH-low — peak on "JEST", then falls. "De-con" builds low and quickly; "-ant" is very short and barely voiced. Don't stress "DEE" at the start.
⚠️ Stress on 3rd syllable "JEST". "De-" prefix = dee (light). "Con-" reduces to "kun-" (schwa). The "-ant" ending = "-unt" (schwa + n). Common error: "DEE-kun-jest-unt" — stressing the first syllable. OET pharmacy context: decongestants (e.g. pseudoephedrine, oxymetazoline) relieve nasal congestion. Key counselling point: should not be used for more than 3–5 days due to risk of rebound congestion. Also interact with warfarin and MAOIs.
Example 1
The pharmacist advised against using the decongestant for more than three days, as prolonged use can cause rebound congestion.
Example 2
"You should avoid this decongestant if you are taking warfarin, as it may affect your blood pressure and interact with your medication."
📌 Same "de-con-" prefix reduction (schwa on "con"):
decongest → dee-kun-JEST  |  deconstruct → dee-kun-STRUKT  |  decompress → dee-kum-PRES
WORD
REBOUND (CONGESTION)
REE-bownd 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈriːbaʊnd/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low — peak sharply on "REE" (long vowel), falls on "-bownd". "REE" must be clearly long /iː/ — don't shorten to "RIH-bownd". When used as a modifier ("rebound congestion"), the main stress shifts to "congestion" in the full phrase: "rebound con-JEST-ion".
⚠️ As a standalone noun/adjective: stress on 1st syllable "REE". As a verb ("the ball rebounded"): stress shifts to 2nd syllable — "ri-BOWND". In pharmacy, always used as noun/adjective = REE-bownd. "Rebound congestion" (also called rhinitis medicamentosa) = worsening congestion that occurs when a nasal decongestant is overused or stopped. This is the key counselling warning for nasal decongestants — and frequently comes up in OET pharmacy scenarios.
Example 1
Using nasal decongestant sprays for more than five days can lead to rebound congestion — the nose becomes more blocked than before.
Example 2
"This is why we recommend switching to a saline spray — it avoids the risk of rebound effects that come with prolonged decongestant use."
📌 Noun (stress 1st) vs verb (stress 2nd) — same word, different stress:
REbound (noun) → REE-bownd  |  reBOUND (verb) → ri-BOWND  |  same pattern: REfund / reFUND, REcord / reCORD
WORD
SALINE
SAY-lyn 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈseɪlaɪn/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low — peak on "SAY" (long diphthong /eɪ/), falls on "-lyn". The second syllable "-lyn" ends in a clear /n/ — don't drop it ("SAY-lee" is wrong). "SAY" rhymes with "day", "way", "say".
⚠️ Stress on 1st syllable "SAY". The vowel /eɪ/ is a diphthong — starts at "eh" and glides to "ee". Common error: "SAL-een" (two short syllables, stress on 2nd) — this is wrong in British RP. "Saline" = sodium chloride solution. Used clinically in many contexts: saline flush, saline drip, saline nasal spray, normal saline (0.9% NaCl). In OET pharmacy: saline nasal spray = safer long-term alternative to decongestants for nasal congestion.
Example 1
A saline nasal spray can help to moisturise the nasal passages and relieve congestion without the risk of rebound effects.
Example 2
The wound was cleaned thoroughly with saline solution before the dressing was applied.
📌 Same /eɪ/ first syllable (long "ay" diphthong):
saline → SAY-lyn  |  baseline → BAYS-lyn  |  vaccine → vak-SEEN (different — stress on 2nd)
PHRASE
"It may not work as quickly as the decongestant"
it may not WORK as QUICK-ly as the de-con-JEST-unt
Key stress points: WORK / QUICK / JEST
🎵 Pitch contour: rises to "WORK", dips briefly, rises again to "QUICK-ly", then rises to "JEST-unt". Three peaks across a long phrase — the listener hears a wave pattern. "may not" sits low between "it" and "WORK". In natural speech, this phrase flows without pauses.
OET COUNSELLING PHRASE — used when recommending an alternative (saline) that is safer but slower-acting than the patient's preferred option (decongestant). This is a softening / expectation-setting phrase. Structure: "It may not [match expectation]... but [benefit/reassurance]." Always follow with a positive: "but it is much safer for long-term use." The hedge "may not" is important — don't say "it won't work as quickly" (too absolute and discouraging).
OET pharmacy context
"I'd suggest trying a saline nasal spray — it may not work as quickly as the decongestant, but it is much safer for ongoing use and won't cause rebound congestion."
Pattern in other contexts
"This paracetamol may not work as quickly as ibuprofen, but it's far more appropriate given your current medication."
📌 Same OET "manage expectations" pattern (softening before recommending an alternative):
"It's not quite as strong as..."  |  "It may take a little longer to..."  |  "You might not notice the effect immediately, but..."
PHRASE
"It should help you feel better"
it should HELP you feel BET-ter
Key stress: HELP / BET-ter
🎵 Pitch contour: rises to "HELP", dips briefly on "you feel", then rises to "BET-ter" and falls. "Should" is low and unstressed — it's a modal of expectation, not certainty. "Better" carries the final fall, signalling reassurance and closure. This is a warmly falling intonation — it sounds caring, not cold.
OET REASSURANCE PHRASE — used to close a counselling recommendation with a positive, encouraging note. "Should" = expectation (not 100% guarantee). Don't say "it WILL help you" (too certain) or "it might help" (too uncertain). "Should" is the OET sweet spot — confident but not over-promising. Often used after explaining a treatment or alternative. Key OET principle: always end with reassurance or a clear action plan.
OET pharmacy context
"Use the saline spray two or three times a day — it should help you feel better within a few days, and you won't have the rebound problem."
Variation with time frame
"Keep taking the full course of antibiotics — it should help you feel better within forty-eight hours."
📌 Same OET closing reassurance pattern (modal + positive outcome):
"This should make a real difference."  |  "You should start to notice an improvement within..."  |  "That should sort it out."
WORD
WARFARIN
WAW-fuh-rin 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈwɔːfərɪn/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low-low — peak on "WAW", then falls through "-fuh-rin". Both unstressed syllables are very short and light. "WAW" = same vowel as "jaw", "law", "saw" — a long rounded /ɔː/ vowel. This is the non-rhotic British RP version: the "r" in "war" is NOT pronounced.
⚠️ British RP: "WAW-fuh-rin" — the "r" in "war" is SILENT (non-rhotic). American English: "WOR-fuh-rin" (rhotic — the "r" is pronounced). For OET, always use the British RP version: "WAW". "Fuh" = schwa + f. "Rin" = /rɪn/ — short "i". Brand name: Coumadin. Warfarin is a vitamin K antagonist anticoagulant — one of the most interaction-prone medications. Key OET pharmacy counselling points: INR monitoring, avoid NSAIDs (use paracetamol instead), consistent vitamin K intake, report any unusual bleeding.
Example 1
Patients taking warfarin should avoid ibuprofen and other NSAIDs, as these can significantly increase the risk of bleeding.
Example 2
"Before I can recommend anything, I need to check whether it's safe to take alongside your warfarin — some decongestants can interact with it."
📌 British RP non-rhotic "war" → sounds like "WAW" (silent r):
warfarin → WAW-fuh-rin  |  ward → WAWD  |  warning → WAW-ning (no "r" sound)

📚 Previous Batch — OET Listening Part A (Set 2–4 Words)

📌 Newest words always appear here first. Scroll down to review older entries.
WORD
GASTROENTEROLOGIST
gas-troh-en-tuh-ROL-uh-jist 7 syllables
British (RP): /ˌɡæstrəʊˌentəˈrɒlədʒɪst/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-low-low-HIGH-low-low — the peak is on "ROL", then falls sharply. Say it as one smooth phrase, not 7 separate beats.
⚠️ Primary stress on 5th syllable "ROL". Secondary stress on "GAS" (1st). The middle section "troh-en-tuh" passes quickly and lightly. Common error: stressing "GAS" too hard and making it sound like 3 separate words. In OET: "I was referred to a gastroenterologist after my GP suspected IBS."
Example 1
He was referred to a gastroenterologist because his GP suspected irritable bowel syndrome.
Example 2
The gastroenterologist ordered an endoscopy to examine the upper digestive tract.
📌 Same "-ologist" pattern (stress on syllable before "-ologist"):
neurologist → nyoo-ROL-uh-jist  |  cardiologist → kar-dee-OL-uh-jist  |  dermatologist → dur-muh-TOL-uh-jist
WORD
IRRITABLE BOWEL SYNDROME
IR-i-tuh-bl BOW-ul SIN-drohm 8 syllables total
British (RP): /ˈɪrɪtəbl ˈbaʊəl ˈsɪndrəʊm/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low-low-low | HIGH-low | HIGH-low — three separate peaks, one per word. Don't run them together. Slight pause between each word helps clarity.
⚠️ Three words, three separate stresses: IR-i-tuh-bl / BOW-ul / SIN-drohm. "Bowel" = BOW-ul (rhymes with "towel" — NOT "bowl"). "Syndrome" = SIN-drohm (stress on 1st). Commonly abbreviated as IBS in clinical notes. "Bowel" is the most frequently mispronounced — the "ow" is a diphthong /aʊ/ as in "cow".
Example 1
The patient was referred for investigation of possible irritable bowel syndrome, presenting with bloating and unpredictable stools.
Example 2
IBS is a functional disorder — there is no structural abnormality, but symptoms can significantly affect quality of life.
📌 "bowel" /baʊəl/ — same "ow" diphthong (/aʊ/):
towel → TOW-ul  |  vowel → VOW-ul  |  prowl → PROWL
WORD
BLOATING
BLOHT-ing 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈbləʊtɪŋ/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low — peak on "BLOHT", falls on "-ing". The "OA" vowel is a long British /əʊ/ — like "boat", "coat", "note". Don't shorten it to "blot-ing".
⚠️ The "oa" in "bloat" is a long diphthong /əʊ/ — same as "boat", "coat", "throat". In British RP, this is rounded and slightly longer than American "oh". Common error: shortening to "BLOT-ing" (as if it rhymes with "plotting"). Clinically: bloating = feeling of fullness/distension, often in IBS, food intolerances, or bowel conditions.
Example 1
The patient's main complaint was bloating after meals, which had been worsening over the past year.
Example 2
Bloating and abdominal discomfort are common features of irritable bowel syndrome.
📌 Same /əʊ/ vowel — long "oh" sound (British RP):
throat → THROHT  |  coat → KOHT  |  dose → DOHS
WORD
CONSTIPATION
kon-sti-PAY-shun 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌkɒnstɪˈpeɪʃən/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-HIGH-low — peak on "PAY", then falls. The "-tion" ending always reduces to "-shun" (never "-see-on"). Rise before "PAY", clear fall after.
⚠️ Stress on 3rd syllable "PAY". The "-tion" suffix is always /ʃən/ — "shun", never "see-on". Common error: saying "CON-sti-pay-shun" with too much weight on "CON". Keep "kon-sti" light and quick. Same "-ation" pattern as medication, vaccination, irritation.
Example 1
He had been suffering from constipation for the past month, which was unusual for him.
Example 2
Increasing dietary fibre and fluid intake are first-line measures for managing constipation.
📌 Same "-ation" suffix, stress on syllable before "-tion":
medication → med-i-KAY-shun  |  palpitation → pal-pi-TAY-shun  |  vaccination → vak-si-NAY-shun
WORD
ANTISPASMODIC
an-tee-spaz-MOD-ik 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌæntɪspæzˈmɒdɪk/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-low-HIGH-low — the peak lands squarely on "MOD". "Anti-spaz" builds up quietly before the peak. Don't peak on "SPAZ".
⚠️ Stress on 4th syllable "MOD". "Anti-" prefix = AN-tee (light). "Spasmodic" alone stresses "MOD": spaz-MOD-ik. Combined, the stress stays on "MOD". Common error: stressing "SPAZ" because it sounds like the most important part. Examples: mebeverine, hyoscine butylbromide (Buscopan). Used in IBS to relax intestinal smooth muscle.
Example 1
His GP prescribed an antispasmodic to relax the muscular contractions that move food through the gut.
Example 2
Antispasmodic drugs are commonly used in irritable bowel syndrome to relieve cramping and abdominal pain.
📌 Same "anti-" + root stress pattern (stress stays on root, not prefix):
antidepressant → an-tee-di-PRES-unt  |  antihistamine → an-tee-HIS-tuh-meen  |  anticoagulant → an-tee-koh-AG-yuh-lunt
WORD
MIGRAINE
MEE-grayn 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈmiːɡreɪn/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low — peak on "MEE" (long vowel), falls on "-grayn". The first vowel is a long /iː/ — like "me", "see", "tree". Don't rush it.
⚠️ British RP: MEE-grayn — the first vowel is a LONG /iː/ (like "me"). American English says MY-grayn — different vowel entirely. In OET (British RP standard), always say MEE-grayn. "Migraine" not "mi-graine" — it's 2 syllables, not 3. Common Korean error: "mi-guh-rayn" (3 syllables) — wrong.
Example 1
She experienced migraines more or less at the same time as her stomach discomfort, which she hadn't initially connected.
Example 2
The patient reported a migraine lasting three days, accompanied by nausea and light sensitivity.
📌 British RP long /iː/ in medical terms:
migraine → MEE-grayn (UK) vs MY-grayn (US)  |  seizure → SEE-zhur  |  treatment → TREET-munt
WORD
NEUROLOGIST
nyoo-ROL-uh-jist 4 syllables
British (RP): /njʊˈrɒlədʒɪst/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low-low — peak on "ROL". "Nyoo" is light and quick. Don't say "NOO" — British RP has the /j/ glide: "nyoo".
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "ROL". In British RP, "neuro-" = NYOO-roh (with the /j/ sound — like "new" in British English). American English says "NOO-roh". The "-ologist" ending always reduces: "-uh-jist". Common error: stressing the first syllable "NOO-ruh-loh-jist" — wrong in both dialects.
Example 1
She was referred to a neurologist after physiotherapy failed to resolve her neck symptoms.
Example 2
The neurologist confirmed a diagnosis of spasmodic torticollis and discussed treatment options.
📌 British RP "neuro-" = NYOO-roh (with /j/ glide):
neurological → nyoo-ruh-LOJ-i-kul  |  neurology → nyoo-ROL-uh-jee  |  neuroscience → NYOO-roh-sy-uns
WORD
SPASMODIC TORTICOLLIS
spaz-MOD-ik   tor-ti-KOL-is 7 syllables total
British (RP): /spæzˈmɒdɪk ˌtɔːtɪˈkɒlɪs/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low | low-low-HIGH-low — two peaks: "MOD" and "KOL". Say both words separately, each with its own peak. Don't flatten into a monotone.
⚠️ Two words — two stress points. "Spasmodic" = spaz-MOD-ik (stress on 2nd). "Torticollis" = tor-ti-KOL-is (stress on 3rd). "Torticollis" comes from Latin "tortus" (twisted) + "collum" (neck). Also called "cervical dystonia". The condition causes involuntary pulling/twisting of the head. Appeared in OET mock test — worth knowing.
Example 1
She was eventually diagnosed with spasmodic torticollis — a condition causing her head to pull involuntarily towards her right shoulder.
Example 2
Botox injections are now a recognised treatment for spasmodic torticollis, offering temporary relief from muscle spasm.
📌 Other dystonia-related terms:
dystonia → dis-TOH-nee-uh  |  cervical → SUR-vi-kul  |  spasm → SPAZ-um
WORD
HAEMOCHROMATOSIS
hee-moh-kroh-muh-TOH-sis 6 syllables
British (RP): /ˌhiːməʊkrəʊməˈtəʊsɪs/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-low-low-HIGH-low — long build-up, single peak on "TOH", then falls. The first four syllables are all light/low. Don't peak early.
⚠️ Primary stress on 5th syllable "TOH". British spelling: "haemo-" (American: "hemo-"). The "haem" = HEEM (long ee). "Chromatosis" = kroh-muh-TOH-sis. This is a hereditary condition causing excess iron absorption. Key fact: treated by venesection (regular blood removal). Appeared in OET mock test (Haley Dove case).
Example 1
Blood tests confirmed a diagnosis of haemochromatosis — the body was absorbing and storing too much iron.
Example 2
Because haemochromatosis can be genetic, the doctor asked whether any family members had been diagnosed with the condition.
📌 Same "-osis" suffix pattern (stress on syllable before "-osis"):
diagnosis → dy-ug-NOH-sis  |  thrombosis → throm-BOH-sis  |  fibrosis → fy-BROH-sis
WORD
VENESECTION
vee-ni-SEK-shun 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌviːnɪˈsekʃən/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-HIGH-low — peak on "SEK". The "-tion" always collapses to "-shun". "Vee-ni" builds low, then peaks sharply on "SEK".
⚠️ Stress on 3rd syllable "SEK". "Vene-" = from Latin "vena" (vein) = VEEN (long ee). "Section" = SEK-shun. Also called phlebotomy. The primary treatment for haemochromatosis — regular removal of blood (initially weekly, then monthly, then quarterly) to reduce iron stores. "Came as a bit of a shock" — classic OET patient response to this diagnosis.
Example 1
She was told that venesection — the regular removal of blood — was the only treatment option for her haemochromatosis.
Example 2
After years of regular venesection, her iron levels returned to a normal range and her energy levels improved significantly.
📌 Same "vene-/veni-" (vein) root words:
intravenous → in-truh-VEE-nus  |  phlebotomy → fluh-BOT-uh-mee  |  venous → VEE-nus
WORD
ENDOMETRIOSIS
en-doh-mee-tree-OH-sis 6 syllables
British (RP): /ˌendəʊˌmiːtriˈəʊsɪs/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-low-low-HIGH-low — same long build as haemochromatosis. Single peak on "OH". Don't break it into pieces — say it as one flowing phrase.
⚠️ Primary stress on 5th syllable "OH". "Endo-" = EN-doh (inner). "Metrio-" = MEE-tree-oh (from "metra" = uterus). The "-osis" ending = -OH-sis. Common error: stressing "EN" at the start ("EN-doh-mee-tree-oh-sis" — flat robot pattern). Keep "en-doh-mee-tree" light and flowing. The condition involves uterine tissue growing outside the uterus.
Example 1
She had been living with endometriosis for years before it was formally diagnosed.
Example 2
Endometriosis commonly presents with pelvic pain, bloating, and fatigue — symptoms that are often dismissed or misattributed.
📌 Same 6-syllable "-osis" pattern (stress on 5th):
haemochromatosis → hee-moh-kroh-muh-TOH-sis  |  atherosclerosis → ath-uh-roh-skluh-ROH-sis  |  osteoporosis → os-tee-oh-puh-ROH-sis
WORD
HYSTERECTOMY
his-tuh-REK-tuh-mee 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌhɪstəˈrektəmi/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-HIGH-low-low — peak on "REK", then steps down through "-tuh-mee". Don't let the pitch rise again on "-mee".
⚠️ Stress on 3rd syllable "REK". "Hyster-" = from Greek "hystera" (uterus). "-ectomy" = surgical removal (always unstressed on "-tuh-mee"). Common error: stressing "-EC-" too broadly as "REK-toh-mee" with equal weight on both — keep "-tuh-mee" low. Same suffix as "appendectomy", "mastectomy".
Example 1
She was advised to have a hysterectomy following the cervical cancer diagnosis, and the procedure went relatively smoothly.
Example 2
A hysterectomy may be recommended when other treatments for conditions like fibroids or endometriosis have not been effective.
📌 Same "-ectomy" suffix pattern (stress always on syllable before "-ectomy"):
appendectomy → ap-en-DEK-tuh-mee  |  mastectomy → mas-TEK-tuh-mee  |  gastrectomy → gas-TREK-tuh-mee
WORD
ARRHYTHMIA
uh-RITH-mee-uh 4 syllables
British (RP): /əˈrɪðmiə/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low-low — peak on "RITH", then falls through "mee-uh". The opening "uh-" is very short and low — nearly a schwa. Don't say "AH-rith-mee-uh".
⚠️ Stress on 2nd syllable "RITH". The silent "h" — "arrhythmia" has double "r" + "h" but the "rh" is pronounced simply as /r/. The "th" here is voiced /ð/ — like "this", "the" (tongue between teeth, WITH vibration). Common error: saying "ar-RITH-mee-uh" with too much weight on "ar" — keep the first syllable as a quick schwa. Appeared in OET mock test (Marvin, long COVID case).
Example 1
The heart monitor identified arrhythmia as the cause of his palpitations and chest discomfort.
Example 2
Beta blockers were prescribed to manage the arrhythmia, and the patient reported improvement almost immediately.
📌 Same voiced "th" /ð/ in medical terms (vibration — like "the", "this"):
rhythm → RITH-um (same root)  |  breathe → BREETH (voiced) vs breath → BRETH (voiceless)
WORD
PALPITATIONS
pal-pi-TAY-shunz 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌpælpɪˈteɪʃənz/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-HIGH-low — peak sharply on "TAY", then falls. The plural "-shunz" is very short. Note: patients often say "my heart was racing / pounding / fluttering" — palpitations is the clinical term for all of these.
⚠️ Stress on 3rd syllable "TAY". Same "-ation" suffix rule — "-TAY-shun". Always used in plural when describing the symptom: "I've been having palpitations" (NOT "a palpitation"). Common error: "PAL-pi-tay-shunz" — stressing the first syllable. The first two syllables "pal-pi" should be quick and quiet before the peak on "TAY".
Example 1
When he started experiencing palpitations and chest pains, he went to see his doctor, fearing a cardiac problem.
Example 2
Palpitations can be caused by anxiety, caffeine, arrhythmia, or anaemia, among other conditions.
📌 Same "-ation" family (3rd syllable stress):
constipation → kon-sti-PAY-shun  |  fibrillation → fib-ri-LAY-shun  |  irritation → ir-i-TAY-shun
WORD
VARICOSE VEINS
VAR-i-kohs VAYNZ 4 syllables total
British (RP): /ˈværɪkəʊs veɪnz/
🎵 Pitch contour: HIGH-low-low | HIGH — two peaks, "VAR" and "VAYNZ". In a sentence, "veins" often carries more final stress as it's the key noun: "varicose VEINS". The "-ose" ending reduces to "-kohs" (not "-kohss").
⚠️ "Varicose" = VAR-i-kohs (stress on 1st). "Veins" = VAYNZ — long diphthong /eɪ/ as in "rain", "pain", "main". Don't say "vayns" with a short vowel. The "-ose" in "varicose" is "-kohs" (voiced s = z sound NOT an "s" hiss). Varicose veins = dilated, tortuous superficial veins — common in pregnancy and people who stand for long periods. Appeared in Monica Patterson OET case.
Example 1
She first developed varicose veins during her pregnancy, and they had been getting progressively worse over the past four years.
Example 2
Treatment options for varicose veins include compression stockings, foam sclerotherapy, and surgical stripping.
📌 Same /eɪ/ vowel in vascular terms:
veins → VAYNZ  |  pain → PAYN  |  drainage → DRAYN-ij
WORD
SCLEROTHERAPY
skleer-oh-THER-uh-pee 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌsklɪərəʊˈθerəpi/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-HIGH-low-low — peak on "THER". The "scler-" opening is a challenging consonant cluster: /skl/ — say "skl" as one unit, don't insert a vowel ("suh-kler" is wrong).
⚠️ Stress on 3rd syllable "THER". The tricky part: initial consonant cluster "scler-" = /skl/ — three consonants together. Practise: "skl...skleer...skleer-oh". The "th" in "therapy" is voiceless /θ/ — like "think", "thin" (tongue between teeth, NO vibration). In OET context: "injections of foam in the veins" = foam sclerotherapy. The patient (Monica) describes it as "quite a new treatment".
Example 1
The GP arranged for her to undergo sclerotherapy — injections of foam into the varicose veins under local anaesthetic.
Example 2
Following sclerotherapy, she was required to wear compression bandages for one week and compression stockings for a further week.
📌 Same "-therapy" stress pattern (stress on syllable before "-therapy"):
physiotherapy → fiz-ee-oh-THER-uh-pee  |  chemotherapy → kee-moh-THER-uh-pee  |  radiotherapy → ray-dee-oh-THER-uh-pee
WORD
HELICOBACTER PYLORI
hel-i-koh-BAK-tur   py-LAW-ree 7 syllables total
British (RP): /ˌhelɪkəʊˈbæktər paɪˈlɔːri/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-low-low-HIGH-low | low-HIGH-low — two peaks: "BAK" and "LAW". Say each word with its own peak. Don't rush through "pylori" — it has its own clear stress.
⚠️ "Helicobacter" = hel-i-koh-BAK-tur (stress on "BAK"). "Pylori" = py-LAW-ree (stress on 2nd, "LAW" — British RP non-rhotic: the "or" sounds like "aw", not "or"). Common in OET gastroenterology scenarios. Causes peptic ulcers. Treatment: triple therapy (two antibiotics + PPI) = "a course of tablets" in patient language. "Cleared up in no time" — typical patient response to successful treatment.
Example 1
The endoscopy and biopsy confirmed the presence of Helicobacter pylori, which was successfully eradicated with a course of antibiotics.
Example 2
Patients with a history of Helicobacter pylori infection are at greater risk of developing gastric cancer and should be monitored accordingly.
📌 British RP non-rhotic "or" → sounds like "aw":
pylori → py-LAW-ree  |  doctor → DOK-tuh (no "r")  |  disorder → dis-AW-duh
WORD
COMPRESSION STOCKINGS
kum-PRESH-un STOK-ingz 5 syllables total
British (RP): /kəmˈpreʃən ˈstɒkɪŋz/
🎵 Pitch contour: low-HIGH-low | HIGH-low — two peaks. "Compression" peaks on "PRESH"; "stockings" peaks on "STOK". In natural speech, "stockings" often gets slightly more final stress as the key noun.
⚠️ "Compression" = kum-PRESH-un (stress on 2nd). "Stockings" = STOK-ingz (stress on 1st). The "com-" prefix reduces to "kum-" (schwa). Common error: "COM-pres-shun" with equal stress on both syllables. In OET context: prescribed after sclerotherapy, DVT, or varicose vein treatment. Also relevant to post-operative care and long-haul flights.
Example 1
Following the procedure, she was advised to wear compression stockings for two weeks to support healing and reduce swelling.
Example 2
Patients at risk of deep vein thrombosis during long-haul flights are often recommended to wear compression stockings.
📌 Same "com-" prefix reduction to schwa:
condition → kuh-DISH-un  |  complaint → kum-PLAYNT  |  consultation → kon-sul-TAY-shun

🆕 Latest Batch — Warfarin Counselling Phrases & Key Drugs

📌 Newest words always appear here first. Scroll down to review older entries.
WORD
ANTACID
ant-AS-id 3 syllables
British (RP): /ænˈtæsɪd/
⚠️ Stress on the 2nd syllable "AS". The word breaks down as "ant-" (= against) + "acid" — so the stress falls on "acid". The vowel in "AS" is a short /æ/ (as in "cat"). Common error: stressing the first syllable "AN-tuh-sid" — this is less standard. In clinical contexts, antacids (e.g., Gaviscon, Rennies) neutralise stomach acid and are relevant to warfarin counselling because some antacids affect drug absorption.
Example 1
The patient was advised to take an antacid at least two hours after her warfarin dose to avoid affecting absorption.
Example 2
"Antacids like Gaviscon can be helpful for heartburn, but timing matters when you are on other medications."
📌 Same "ant-/anti-" prefix + root stress pattern:
antidote → AN-ti-doht  |  antibody → AN-ti-bod-ee  |  antigen → AN-ti-jun
WORD
DIETARY SUPPLEMENTS
DY-uh-tree SUP-luh-munts 6 syllables total
British (RP): /ˈdaɪətri ˈsʌplɪmənts/
⚠️ Two words — two stress points. "Dietary" = DY-uh-tree (3 syllables in British RP — note: American English often says 4 syllables "DY-uh-ter-ee"). "Supplement" = SUP-luh-munt (stress on 1st). In warfarin counselling, dietary supplements are critical — vitamin K-rich supplements, fish oil, cranberry, and St John's Wort can all affect INR significantly.
Example 1
"Please let me know if you are taking any dietary supplements, as some can interact with your warfarin."
Example 2
Fish oil and vitamin E are examples of dietary supplements that can increase the blood-thinning effect of warfarin.
📌 Same first-syllable stress pattern for "-ary/-ery" adjectives (British RP — often 3 syllables, not 4):
dietary → DY-uh-tree (3)  |  secretary → SEK-ruh-tree (3)  |  temporary → TEM-pruh-ree (3)
PHRASE
"I understand where you're coming from, and that's actually a very common concern."
I underSTAND where you're COMing from | a very COMmon conCERN
Meaning: acknowledge you understand the patient's viewpoint before responding. "Where you're coming from" = your perspective/situation. Stress falls on "STAND", "COM-ing", "COM-mon", "CERN". This is a key OET empathy phrase — validates the patient before correcting or redirecting. Use it when a patient insists on something clinically unwise.
Example 1
"I understand where you're coming from, and that's actually a very common concern about taking warfarin long-term."
Example 2
"I understand where you're coming from — many patients feel the same way about needing the medicated spray."
📌 Same OET empathy opening pattern:
"That's a very valid point..."  |  "I can see why you'd feel that way..."  |  "That's completely understandable..."
PHRASE
"Let me explain that in a little more detail."
Let me ex-PLAIN that in a little more de-TAIL
Meaning: signals you are about to give a fuller explanation. "Explain" = ik-PLAIN (stress on 2nd syllable). "Detail" = dee-TAIL or DEE-tayl — British RP prefers "dee-TAIL" (stress on 2nd). Note: "let me" in natural speech often reduces to "lemme" — "LEM-ee ex-PLAIN" sounds natural; "LET ME" stressed is slightly formal but fine in OET.
Example 1
"Let me explain that in a little more detail — warfarin works by blocking vitamin K, which your body needs to form clots."
Example 2
"Let me explain that in a little more detail so you know exactly what to expect."
📌 Same OET signposting pattern (before an explanation):
"What I mean is..."  |  "To put it another way..."  |  "In other words..."
PHRASE
"That's completely understandable. However, let me reassure you..."
com-PLETE-lee | un-dur-STAND-uh-bul | re-uh-SHOOR
Three key words: (1) "completely" = com-PLETE-lee (stress on 2nd). (2) "understandable" = un-dur-STAND-uh-bul (stress on 3rd "STAND"). (3) "reassure" = ree-uh-SHOOR (stress on 3rd). "However" = how-EV-ur — note it signals contrast/pivot. "Reassure" = telling someone not to worry. The structure "Validate → pivot → reassure" is classic OET patient communication.
Example 1
"That's completely understandable. However, let me reassure you — with regular INR monitoring, warfarin is very safe to take long-term."
Example 2
"That's completely understandable. However, let me reassure you that this side effect is temporary and will resolve quickly."
📌 Same "validate → pivot → reassure" structure:
"I can see why... However, the good news is..."  |  "That makes sense. That said, it's worth knowing..."  |  "Many patients feel that way. The important thing is..."
PHRASE
"Please do not take a double dose if you miss one."
Please do NOT take a DUB-ul DOHS
Key stressed words: "NOT" (the most important word — the prohibition), "DUB-ul" (double = two syllables, stress on 1st), "DOHS" (dose = rhymes with "close" the adjective, NOT "doze"). Note: "dose" /dəʊs/ rhymes with "close" (nearby) and "gross" — NOT with "lose" or "doze".
Example 1
"If you forget to take your warfarin one day, please do not take a double dose the next morning."
Example 2
"Please do not take a double dose if you miss one — just continue with your regular schedule."
📌 "dose" rhymes with (not "doze"):
close (nearby) → KLOHS  |  gross → GROHS  |  overdose → OH-vur-dohs
PHRASE
"You should inform all your healthcare providers that you are taking warfarin."
in-FORM all your HEALTH-kair pro-VY-durz
"Inform" = in-FORM (stress on 2nd). "Healthcare" = HEALTH-kair (compound — stress on 1st element). "Providers" = pro-VY-durz (stress on 2nd syllable "VY"). This phrase is essential for warfarin counselling — the patient must tell ALL healthcare providers (dentists, surgeons, other GPs) because warfarin has significant drug interactions.
Example 1
"You should inform all your healthcare providers — including your dentist and any specialists — that you are taking warfarin."
Example 2
"It's very important to inform all your healthcare providers before any procedure, as warfarin affects how your blood clots."
📌 Same 2nd-syllable stress verbs used in counselling:
inform → in-FORM  |  confirm → kun-FIRM  |  perform → pur-FORM
PHRASE
"increase the level of warfarin in your blood"
in-CREASE the LEV-ul | WAW-fuh-rin in your BLUD
⚠️ KEY: "increase" as a VERB = in-CREASE (stress on 2nd syllable). As a NOUN = IN-crease (stress on 1st). "The level of warfarin in your blood" = "blood warfarin level" — this is clinician language; for patients, say "the amount of warfarin in your blood". "Level" = LEV-ul (stress on 1st; the second syllable reduces to schwa).
Example 1
"Ibuprofen can significantly increase the level of warfarin in your blood, which raises your risk of bleeding."
Example 2
"Methotrexate can increase the level of warfarin in your blood, so always tell your pharmacist if you are on both."
📌 Verb vs noun stress shift pairs (same word, different stress):
increase (v) → in-CREASE / (n) → IN-crease  |  record (v) → ri-CORD / (n) → REC-ord  |  present (v) → pri-ZENT / (n) → PREZ-unt
WORD
TOXICITY
tok-SIS-i-tee 4 syllables
British (RP): /tɒkˈsɪsɪti/
⚠️ Stress on the 2nd syllable "SIS". The first syllable "tox-" = "tok" (short "o" as in "hot"). The final syllable "-tee" is unstressed. "Toxicity" refers to the degree to which a substance can harm an organism — a critical concept in warfarin and methotrexate counselling.
Example 1
Ibuprofen can increase the risk of warfarin toxicity by displacing it from plasma proteins.
Example 2
Signs of methotrexate toxicity include mouth sores, nausea, and abnormal liver function tests.
📌 Same "-icity" stress pattern (stress always on syllable before "-ity"):
electricity → ee-lek-TRIS-i-tee  |  elasticity → ee-las-TIS-i-tee  |  authenticity → aw-then-TIS-i-tee
WORD
PARACETAMOL
par-uh-SEE-tuh-mol 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌpærəˈsiːtəˌmɒl/
⚠️ Stress on the 3rd syllable "SEE" — a long "ee" sound. Secondary stress on the final syllable "-mol". British English word (American equivalent: acetaminophen). Common errors: (1) equal stress on all syllables; (2) shortening "SEE" to a short "i". Remember: par-uh-SEE-tuh-mol — "SEE" must be clear and long.
Example 1
Paracetamol is the preferred pain reliever for patients taking warfarin, as it has fewer drug interactions than ibuprofen.
Example 2
Do not exceed 4 grams of paracetamol per day, as higher doses can cause liver damage.
📌 Same 5-syllable drug name, 3rd-syllable stress:
antihistamine → an-tee-HIS-tuh-meen  |  acetaminophen → uh-see-tuh-MIN-oh-fun (US equivalent)  |  carbamazepine → kar-buh-MAZ-uh-peen
WORD
METHOTREXATE
meth-oh-TREK-sayt 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌmeθəˈtreksɪt/ or /ˌmeθoʊˈtrekseɪt/
⚠️ Stress on the 3rd syllable "TREK". The "meth-oh" prefix is quick and light. The ending varies slightly: "-site" /sɪt/ or "-sayt" /seɪt/ — both are acceptable in British clinical use. "Methotrexate" is a DMARD (disease-modifying antirheumatic drug) and also used in cancer treatment and ectopic pregnancy management. Significant interaction with warfarin and NSAIDs.
Example 1
Methotrexate must be taken once weekly, not daily — daily dosing can lead to serious toxicity.
Example 2
Ibuprofen should be avoided in patients taking methotrexate as it can increase methotrexate toxicity significantly.
📌 Same 4-syllable drug, 3rd-syllable stress (-TREK/-PRAM/-CLOF pattern):
omeprazole → oh-MEP-ruh-zohl  |  amlodipine → am-LOH-di-peen  |  citalopram → si-TAL-oh-pram
WORD
IBUPROFEN
eye-byoo-PROH-fun 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌaɪbjuːˈprəʊfən/
⚠️ Stress on the 3rd syllable "PROH". Cambridge confirmed. ⚠️ Very common error: adding an extra "r" → "i-BRU-profen" — this is WRONG. The first two syllables are "eye" + "byoo" (no "r"). "Eye" = same as the body part. "byoo" rhymes with "few", "view". Final syllable "-fun" = schwa + n (very light).
Example 1
Patients taking warfarin should avoid ibuprofen, as it can significantly increase the risk of bleeding.
Example 2
"Instead of ibuprofen, I recommend paracetamol for pain relief — it is much safer with your current medication."
📌 Same 3rd-syllable stress NSAID/analgesic drugs:
ketoprofen → kee-toh-PROH-fun  |  naproxen → nuh-PROK-sun  |  diclofenac → dy-KLOH-fen-ak
WORD
SHORTNESS OF BREATH
SHORT-nis uv BRETH 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈʃɔːtnɪs əv breθ/
⚠️ TWO critical distinctions: (1) "breath" (NOUN) = BRETH (short "e", voiceless "th") — rhymes with "death". (2) "breathe" (VERB) = BREETH (long "ee", voiced "th") — rhymes with "seethe". Very commonly confused! In OET: "shortness of breath" = the symptom (noun). "He can't breathe" = the action (verb). The "of" reduces to a schwa: "uv".
Example 1
Sudden shortness of breath can be a sign of pulmonary embolism in patients on anticoagulant therapy.
Example 2
"If you experience any shortness of breath or chest pain while taking warfarin, go to the emergency department immediately."
📌 Noun vs verb: "breath" /breθ/ vs "breathe" /briːð/ — same pattern:
breath → BRETH (short e)  |  breathe → BREETH (long ee)  |  death → DETH / seethe → SEETH
WORD 1
GOUT
GOUT 1 syllable
/ɡaʊt/
⚠️ The vowel sound is a diphthong "ow" (like "shout", "house") — NOT a simple "oo" or "o" sound. Many Korean speakers shorten this to "got" or "goot" — both are incorrect.
Example 1
The patient was diagnosed with gout after experiencing sudden pain in his big toe.
Example 2
Allopurinol is commonly prescribed to prevent recurring gout attacks.
📌 Same stress pattern:
shout → SHOWT  |  bout → BOWT  |  drought → DROWT
WORD 2
AMOXICILLIN
uh-mok-si-SIL-in 5 syllables
/əˌmɒksɪˈsɪlɪn/
⚠️ Stress falls on the 4th syllable ("SIL"), NOT the 2nd. This is one of the most commonly mis-stressed pharmacy words. Keep syllables 1, 2, 3, and 5 light and quick.
Example 1
The doctor prescribed amoxicillin for the patient's chest infection.
Example 2
She developed a rash after taking amoxicillin for three days.
📌 Same stress pattern:
penicillin → pen-i-SIL-in  |  ampicillin → amp-i-SIL-in  |  methicillin → meth-i-SIL-in
WORD 3
PENICILLIN
pen-i-SIL-in 4 syllables
/ˌpenɪˈsɪlɪn/
⚠️ Stress falls on the 3rd syllable ("SIL") — same stressed syllable shape as "amoxicillin" above. Notice the "c" is pronounced as "s", not "k".
Example 1
Always check for a penicillin allergy before prescribing this class of antibiotics.
Example 2
The patient said he was allergic to penicillin as a child.
📌 Same stress pattern:
amoxicillin → uh-mok-si-SIL-in  |  ampicillin → amp-i-SIL-in  |  flucloxacillin → floo-klok-suh-SIL-in
WORD 4
REVEAL
ri-VEEL 2 syllables
/rɪˈviːl/
⚠️ Stress is on the SECOND syllable, not the first. The first syllable "re-" should be quick and light (schwa-like), not stressed as "REE-veal".
Example 1
The blood test results reveal a slight increase in cholesterol levels.
Example 2
Please reveal any other medications you are currently taking.
📌 Same stress pattern:
conceal → kun-SEEL  |  repeal → ri-PEEL  |  appeal → uh-PEEL
WORD 5
RAISE
RAYZ 1 syllable
/reɪz/
⚠️ The "ai" makes a long "ay" diphthong sound, and the final "s" is voiced — it sounds like "z", not "s". Don't confuse with "rice" /raɪs/, which has a different vowel and an unvoiced ending.
Example 1
This medication may raise your blood pressure, so we'll monitor it closely.
Example 2
Please raise any concerns you have about the new prescription.
📌 Same stress pattern:
phrase → FRAYZ  |  gaze → GAYZ  |  phase → FAYZ
WORD 6
COUNSEL
KOWN-suhl 2 syllables
/ˈkaʊnsəl/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. Note: this is a homophone of "council" (a governing body) — same pronunciation, different spelling and meaning. As a verb in pharmacy contexts, it means "to give professional advice."
Example 1
The pharmacist will counsel the patient on how to use the inhaler correctly.
Example 2
It's important to counsel elderly patients about the risk of drug interactions.
📌 Same stress pattern:
council → KOWN-sul  |  cancel → KAN-sul  |  marvel → MAR-vul
WORD 7
SPREAD
SPRED 1 syllable
/spred/
⚠️ This word has a three-consonant cluster at the start (s-p-r) which can be difficult — don't insert a vowel sound between them (avoid "suh-PRED"). The vowel is a short "e" as in "bed", NOT a long "ee" sound.
Example 1
Without treatment, the infection could spread to other parts of the body.
Example 2
The rash began to spread from her arm to her chest.
📌 Same stress pattern:
thread → THRED  |  bread → BRED  |  dread → DRED
WORD 8
BIG TOE
BIG TOE 2 words, both stressed
/bɪɡ toʊ/
⚠️ Both words carry roughly equal stress in this compound phrase (unlike single compound words like "toothache"). "Toe" rhymes with "go", "so" — not "too".
Example 1
The patient reported sudden, sharp pain in his big toe overnight.
Example 2
Gout most commonly affects the joint at the base of the big toe.
📌 Same stress pattern:
chest pain → CHEST PAYN  |  right knee → RYGHT NEE  |  left arm → LEFT ARM
WORD 9
PINKY TOE / LITTLE TOE
PIN-kee toe / LIT-tl toe main stress on first word
/ˈpɪŋki toʊ/ — /ˈlɪtl toʊ/
⚠️ "Pinky" is the informal/common term; "little toe" is the more clinical/formal term — both acceptable in OET speaking, but "little toe" sounds slightly more professional in a clinical note.
Example 1
She injured her little toe after stubbing it against the bed frame.
Example 2
Swelling around the pinky toe can also indicate a fracture.
📌 Same stress pattern:
middle toe → MID-ul TOH  |  ring toe → RING TOH  |  broken toe → BROH-kun TOH
WORD 10
INSECT BITE
IN-sekt bite 3 syllables total
/ˈɪnsekt baɪt/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable of "insect" ("IN"), not the second. "Bite" rhymes with "light", "white" — long "i" sound.
Example 1
The redness and swelling are consistent with an insect bite rather than an infection.
Example 2
Apply a cold compress to reduce itching from the insect bite.
📌 Same stress pattern:
insect → IN-sekt  |  impact → IM-pakt  |  infant → IN-funt
WORD 11
EXCRUCIATING
ik-SKROO-shee-ay-ting 5 syllables
/ɪkˈskruːʃieɪtɪŋ/
⚠️ Stress falls on the SECOND syllable ("SKROO" — sounds like "crew"). Common error: stressing the third syllable instead. Keep the "i" in "-ating" short, like "bit" — not a long "ee" sound.
Example 1
The patient described the pain as excruciating, rating it a ten out of ten.
Example 2
She experienced excruciating pain when trying to put weight on her foot.
📌 Same stress pattern:
exasperating → ig-ZAS-puh-ray-ting  |  exhilarating → ig-ZIL-uh-ray-ting  |  excoriating → ik-SKOR-ee-ay-ting
WORD 12
VISUAL ANALOGUE SCALE (VAS)
VIZH-oo-uhl AN-uh-log SKAYL 7 syllables
/ˈvɪʒuəl ˈænəlɒɡ skeɪl/
⚠️ All three words carry their own primary stress as this is a fixed clinical term. As an abbreviation, "VAS" is normally spelled out letter-by-letter: "V-A-S" (vee-ay-es), not pronounced as one word "vass".
Example 1
Please rate your pain using the Visual Analogue Scale, from zero to ten.
Example 2
His VAS score improved from eight to three after the medication.
📌 Same stress pattern:
verbal rating scale → VUR-bul RAY-ting SKAYL  |  numeric pain scale → nyoo-MER-ik PAYN SKAYL  |  pain assessment tool → PAYN uh-SES-munt TOOL
WORD 13
UNBEARABLE PAIN
un-BEAR-uh-bl pain 4 syllables + 1
/ʌnˈbeərəbl peɪn/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable ("BEAR"). Prefix "un-" is unstressed and quick. Don't confuse with "unbearable" stressed on the first syllable, which sounds unnatural.
Example 1
He described the headache as unbearable pain that prevented him from sleeping.
Example 2
The dressing change caused unbearable pain despite the local anaesthetic.
📌 Same stress pattern:
uncomfortable → un-KUM-fur-tuh-bl  |  unacceptable → un-ak-SEP-tuh-bl  |  unforgettable → un-fur-GET-uh-bl
WORD 14
SEVERE PAIN
si-VEER pain 2 syllables + 1
/sɪˈvɪər peɪn/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable of "severe" ("VEER"). A very common error is stressing the first syllable ("SEH-veer") — this is incorrect.
Example 1
The patient is experiencing severe pain in the lower right abdomen.
Example 2
Without treatment, severe pain can significantly affect quality of life.
📌 Same stress pattern:
sincere → sin-SEER  |  career → kuh-REER  |  appear → uh-PEER
WORD 15
THROBBING
THROB-ing 2 syllables
/ˈθrɒbɪŋ/
⚠️ The "th" sound is voiceless (like "think", not "this") — tongue lightly between teeth, no vibration. Don't drop the "th" and say "frobbing" or "trobbing".
Example 1
She described the headache as a constant throbbing sensation behind her eyes.
Example 2
The infected wound has a throbbing pain that worsens at night.
📌 Same stress pattern:
stabbing → STAB-ing  |  pounding → POWND-ing  |  burning → BUR-ning
WORD 16
SEPTICEMIA
sep-ti-SEE-mee-uh 5 syllables
/ˌseptɪˈsiːmiə/
⚠️ Stress falls on the 4th syllable ("SEE"). This is the American spelling; British English uses "septicaemia" — same pronunciation and stress pattern. Also known as "blood poisoning" in everyday language.
Example 1
Untreated septicemia can rapidly progress to septic shock.
Example 2
The patient was admitted with suspected septicemia following the infected wound.
📌 Same stress pattern:
leukaemia → loo-KEE-mee-uh  |  anaemia → uh-NEE-mee-uh  |  ischaemia → is-KEE-mee-uh
WORD 17
COLCHICINE
KOL-chi-seen 3 syllables
/ˈkɒltʃɪsiːn/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. The "ch" is pronounced "ch" as in "chip" (not "k"). Used to treat gout flare-ups; common side effect is diarrhoea.
Example 1
Colchicine is often prescribed to relieve acute gout flare-ups.
Example 2
He stopped taking the colchicine after experiencing severe diarrhoea.
📌 Same stress pattern:
caffeine → KAF-een  |  codeine → KOH-deen  |  quinine → KWIN-een
WORD 18
DIARRHEA / DIARRHOEA
dy-uh-REE-uh 4 syllables
/ˌdaɪəˈriːə/
⚠️ Stress on the THIRD syllable ("REE"). Note the spelling: American English "diarrhoea", British English "diarrhoea" — pronunciation is identical.
Example 1
Persistent diarrhoea is a common side effect of this medication.
Example 2
If the diarrhoea doesn't resolve within 48 hours, contact your doctor.
📌 Same stress pattern:
gonorrhoea → gon-uh-REE-uh  |  Korea → kuh-REE-uh  |  idea → eye-DEE-uh
WORD 19
ALLOPURINOL
al-oh-PYOOR-i-nol 5 syllables
/ˌæləˈpjʊərɪnɒl/
⚠️ Stress on the THIRD syllable ("PYOOR" — rhymes with "pure"). Used for long-term gout prevention; can cause a skin rash in some patients.
Example 1
Allopurinol is used long-term to lower uric acid levels and prevent gout.
Example 2
She developed a mild skin rash shortly after starting allopurinol.
📌 Same stress pattern:
metoprolol → muh-TOP-ruh-lol  |  atenolol → uh-TEN-uh-lol  |  propranolol → pro-PRAN-uh-lol
WORD 20
MENINGITIS
men-in-JY-tis 4 syllables
/ˌmenɪnˈdʒaɪtɪs/
⚠️ Stress on the THIRD syllable ("JY" — long "i" sound, like "eye"). The "g" is soft, pronounced like "j".
Example 1
A severe, sudden headache combined with a stiff neck may indicate meningitis.
Example 2
The vaccine significantly reduces the risk of bacterial meningitis.
📌 Same stress pattern:
appendicitis → uh-pen-di-SY-tis  |  laryngitis → lar-in-JY-tis  |  dermatitis → dur-muh-TY-tis
WORD 21
MEFLOQUINE (Lariam)
MEF-loh-kween 3 syllables
/ˈmefləkwiːn/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. Brand name is "Lariam" (LAR-ee-am, stress 1st syllable). Known for potential psychiatric side effects, including vivid or odd dreams.
Example 1
He reported unusually vivid dreams after starting mefloquine for malaria prevention.
Example 2
Mefloquine is not recommended for travellers with a history of psychiatric illness.
📌 Same stress pattern:
chloroquine → KLOR-uh-kwin  |  quarantine → KWOR-un-teen  |  tramadol → TRAM-uh-dol
WORD 22
ARTESUNATE
ar-TES-yoo-nayt 4 syllables
/ɑːˈtesjuːneɪt/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable. Note: this is a less common pharmaceutical term — pronunciation guidance is moderately confident; if possible, cross-check with Greg or a pharmacology colleague.
Example 1
Artesunate is used to treat severe cases of malaria, including drug-resistant strains.
Example 2
Intravenous artesunate is the first-line treatment for severe malaria.
📌 Same stress pattern:
articulate → ar-TIK-yuh-lut  |  participate → par-TIS-i-payt  |  appreciate → uh-PREE-shee-ayt
WORD 23
JAUNDICE
JAWN-dis 2 syllables
/ˈdʒɔːndɪs/
⚠️ The "au" makes a long "aw" sound (like "lawn"). Only 2 syllables — don't add an extra one.
Example 1
Yellowing of the skin and eyes is a classic sign of jaundice.
Example 2
The newborn was monitored closely for signs of jaundice.
📌 Same stress pattern:
notice → NOH-tis  |  practice → PRAK-tis  |  office → OF-is
WORD 24
LUMPECTOMY
lum-PEK-tuh-mee 4 syllables
/lʌmˈpektəmi/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable. A surgical procedure to remove a lump (often breast tissue) while preserving the surrounding tissue.
Example 1
She underwent a lumpectomy followed by a course of radiotherapy.
Example 2
A lumpectomy is a less invasive alternative to a full mastectomy.
📌 Same stress pattern:
mastectomy → mas-TEK-tuh-mee  |  appendectomy → uh-pen-DEK-tuh-mee  |  gastrectomy → gas-TREK-tuh-mee
WORD 25
INTEGRITY
in-TEG-ri-tee 4 syllables
/ɪnˈtegrəti/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable. In clinical notes, often used to mean the soundness or condition of a structure (e.g., "skin integrity", "the integrity of dental work").
Example 1
The dentist checked the integrity of the previous filling before proceeding.
Example 2
Maintaining skin integrity is important in bedridden patients.
📌 Same stress pattern:
sincerity → sin-SER-i-tee  |  security → si-KYOOR-i-tee  |  maturity → muh-TYOOR-i-tee
WORD 26
ROTATOR CUFF
roh-TAY-tor kuf 3 syllables + 1
/roʊˈteɪtər kʌf/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable of "rotator" ("TAY"). "Cuff" rhymes with "tough", "rough" — short "u" sound.
Example 1
An MRI confirmed a partial tear of the rotator cuff.
Example 2
Rotator cuff injuries are common among overhead athletes.
📌 Same stress pattern:
creator → kree-AY-tor  |  narrator → nuh-RAY-tor  |  translator → tranz-LAY-tor
WORD 27
IMPINGEMENT SYNDROME
im-PINJ-ment SIN-drohm 3 + 2 syllables
/ɪmˈpɪndʒmənt ˈsɪndroʊm/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable of "impingement" ("PINJ") and the FIRST syllable of "syndrome" ("SIN"). Refers to shoulder tendons being pinched during movement.
Example 1
The physiotherapist diagnosed shoulder impingement syndrome from repetitive overhead movement.
Example 2
Impingement syndrome often improves with targeted strengthening exercises.
📌 Same stress pattern:
infringement → in-FRINJ-ment  |  arrangement → uh-RAYNJ-ment  |  engagement → in-GAYJ-ment
WORD 28
ASSIMILATE
uh-SIM-i-layt 4 syllables
/əˈsɪməleɪt/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable. Means "to take in and fully understand or absorb information."
Example 1
It took her a moment to assimilate the news about her diagnosis.
Example 2
New graduates often need time to assimilate clinical protocols into daily practice.
📌 Same stress pattern:
associate → uh-SOH-shee-ayt  |  articulate → ar-TIK-yuh-layt  |  accelerate → ak-SEL-uh-rayt
WORD 29
PSYCHOTIC
sy-KOT-ik 3 syllables
/saɪˈkɒtɪk/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable. The "psy-" is pronounced "sy" (the "p" is silent), like "sigh".
Example 1
Mefloquine has been associated with rare psychotic symptoms in some patients.
Example 2
The patient was monitored closely for any psychotic episodes during treatment.
📌 Same stress pattern:
narcotic → nar-KOT-ik  |  hypnotic → hip-NOT-ik  |  neurotic → nyoo-ROT-ik
WORD 30
GROGGY
GROG-ee 2 syllables
/ˈɡrɒɡi/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. Informal word meaning "dazed, weak, unsteady" — often used after anaesthesia or lack of sleep.
Example 1
She felt groggy for a few hours after the general anaesthetic wore off.
Example 2
He was still a bit groggy when the porter came to take him to his room.
📌 Same stress pattern:
foggy → FOG-ee  |  soggy → SOG-ee  |  groggy → GROG-ee
WORD 31
DOUBTLESS
DOWT-lis 2 syllables
/ˈdaʊtləs/
⚠️ The "b" in "doubt" is silent. Stress on the FIRST syllable, which rhymes with "shout".
Example 1
The new system will doubtless save time once staff become familiar with it.
Example 2
He was doubtless the busiest pharmacist on the ward that morning.
📌 Same stress pattern:
restless → REST-lus  |  careless → KAIR-lus  |  helpless → HELP-lus
WORD 32
PORTER
POR-ter 2 syllables
/ˈpɔːrtər/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. In a hospital context, refers to staff who transport patients or equipment between wards.
Example 1
A porter arrived to take the patient down to radiology.
Example 2
Please call a porter to help transfer him to the surgical ward.
📌 Same stress pattern:
doctor → DOK-tor  |  factor → FAK-tor  |  sector → SEK-tor
WORD 33
NIGGLE
NIG-uhl 2 syllables
/ˈnɪɡl/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. Informal word for "a minor, persistent ache or worry" — common in casual conversations about pain.
Example 1
I've had a bit of a niggle in my knee since the marathon.
Example 2
It's just a small niggle — nothing serious enough to stop training.
📌 Same stress pattern:
giggle → GIG-ul  |  wiggle → WIG-ul  |  tickle → TIK-ul
WORD 34
WEARY
WEER-ee 2 syllables
/ˈwɪəri/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. Means "extremely tired", often used to describe how patients feel after surgery or illness.
Example 1
You're bound to feel weary for the first few days after the operation.
Example 2
He looked weary after the long flight and lack of sleep.
📌 Same stress pattern:
clearly → KLEER-lee  |  nearly → NEER-lee  |  yearly → YEER-lee
WORD 35
MIND-BOGGLING
MYND-bog-ling 3 syllables
/ˈmaɪndˌbɒɡəlɪŋ/
⚠️ Primary stress on "MYND", secondary stress on "BOG". Informal expression meaning "extremely surprising or hard to understand/believe".
Example 1
It's mind-boggling how many interruptions the pharmacy team handles in a single shift.
Example 2
The variety of drug interactions to track can feel mind-boggling at first.
📌 Same stress pattern:
mind-blowing → MYND-bloh-ing  |  eye-opening → EYE-oh-puh-ning  |  breathtaking → BRETH-tay-king
WORD 17
COLCHICINE
KOL-chi-seen 3 syllables
/ˈkɒltʃɪsiːn/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. The "ch" makes a "tʃ" sound (like "church"), and the final syllable is a long "seen" — not "sin" or "sen".
Example 1
Colchicine is commonly prescribed to relieve acute gout flares.
Example 2
A frequent side effect of colchicine is diarrhoea.
WORD 18
DIARRHEA
dy-uh-REE-uh 4 syllables
/ˌdaɪəˈriːə/
⚠️ Stress on the THIRD syllable ("REE"). The spelling is tricky (double "r", "h" silent-ish) but doesn't affect pronunciation much — just remember "dy-uh-REE-uh".
Example 1
He reported mild diarrhoea since starting the new antibiotic.
Example 2
Persistent diarrhoea can lead to dehydration in elderly patients.
WORD 19
ALLOPURINOL
al-oh-PYUR-i-nol 5 syllables
/ˌæloʊˈpjʊərɪnɒl/
⚠️ Stress on the 3rd syllable ("PYUR" — like "pure"). Common error: stressing the 2nd syllable instead, or using a short "u" sound instead of "yoo".
Example 1
The patient developed a skin rash shortly after starting allopurinol.
Example 2
Allopurinol is used long-term to prevent future gout attacks.
WORD 20
MENINGITIS
men-in-JY-tis 4 syllables
/ˌmenɪnˈdʒaɪtɪs/
⚠️ Stress on the 3rd syllable ("JY" — rhymes with "eye"). The "g" makes a soft "j" sound (like "giant"), not a hard "g" (like "go").
Example 1
A stiff neck and severe headache can be early signs of meningitis.
Example 2
The vaccine significantly reduces the risk of bacterial meningitis.
WORD 21
MEFLOQUINE (brand name: LARIAM)
MEF-luh-kwin 3 syllables | LAIR-ee-um
/ˈmɛfləkwɪn/ — /ˈlæriəm/
⚠️ Mefloquine: stress on the FIRST syllable. Ends in "kwin" (like "win"), not "kween". Lariam (the brand name) also stresses the first syllable.
Example 1
He was prescribed mefloquine for malaria prevention before his trip.
Example 2
Lariam can cause vivid or unusual dreams in some patients.
📌 Same stress pattern:
chloroquine → KLOR-uh-kwin  |  quarantine → KWOR-un-teen  |  tramadol → TRAM-uh-dol
WORD 22
ARTESUNATE
ar-TES-yoo-nayt 4 syllables
/ɑːrˈtesjuːneɪt/
⚠️ Stress on the 2nd syllable ("TES"). This is a less common drug name — practice slowly first, then build speed.
Example 1
Artesunate is used to treat severe cases of drug-resistant malaria.
Example 2
The hospital administered artesunate intravenously due to the severity of the infection.
WORD 23
PSYCHOTIC (SYMPTOMS)
sy-KOT-ik 3 syllables
/saɪˈkɒtɪk/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable. The "p" at the start is SILENT — it sounds like "sy-KOT-ik", not "psy-KOT-ik".
Example 1
The medication was discontinued after the patient developed psychotic symptoms.
Example 2
Family members should be informed about the signs of psychotic episodes.
📌 Same stress pattern:
narcotic → nar-KOT-ik  |  hypnotic → hip-NOT-ik  |  neurotic → nyoo-ROT-ik
WORD 24
JAUNDICE
JAWN-dis 2 syllables
/ˈdʒɔːndɪs/
