⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. "Jaun-" rhymes with "lawn", "dawn" — not "John". The ending is "-dis", not "-dyse".
Example 1
The yellowing of his skin and eyes suggested jaundice.
Example 2
Newborn jaundice usually resolves within the first two weeks of life.
WORD 25
LUMPECTOMY
lum-PEK-tuh-mee 4 syllables
/lʌmˈpektəmi/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable ("PEK"). Built from "lump" + "-ectomy" (surgical removal) — same ending pattern as "appendectomy".
Example 1
She underwent a lumpectomy followed by a course of radiotherapy.
Example 2
A lumpectomy removes only the tumour, preserving most of the breast tissue.
WORD 26
INTEGRITY
in-TEG-ri-tee 4 syllables
/ɪnˈtɛɡrɪti/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable ("TEG"). In clinical contexts, it often refers to the soundness/wholeness of tissue, skin, or dental work — not just moral integrity.
Example 1
The dentist checked the integrity of the previous filling before proceeding.
Example 2
Maintaining skin integrity is important for patients who are bed-bound for long periods.
WORD 27
ROTATOR CUFF
roh-TAY-tor kuf 4 syllables total
/roʊˈteɪtər kʌf/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable of "rotator" ("TAY"). "Cuff" rhymes with "tough", "enough" — short "u" sound.
Example 1
An MRI confirmed a partial tear of the rotator cuff.
Example 2
Physiotherapy is often the first step in treating a rotator cuff injury.
WORD 28
IMPINGEMENT SYNDROME
im-PINJ-muhnt 3 syllables + syndrome
/ɪmˈpɪndʒmənt ˈsɪndroʊm/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable of "impingement" ("PINJ" — soft "g" sound, like "fringe"). "Syndrome" stresses the first syllable: SIN-drohm.
Example 1
Shoulder impingement syndrome often causes pain when raising the arm overhead.
Example 2
Repetitive overhead movements can contribute to impingement syndrome over time.

💬 Section B: Natural Expressions & Idioms

These are common idiomatic expressions used in clinical conversation. For each, focus on the naturally stressed word (shown in CAPS) rather than individual syllables — this is what makes spoken English sound fluent rather than robotic.
PHRASE 1
"We got really frightened"
we got really FRIGHT-uhnd
Meaning: expressing fear/alarm about a situation. Main stress falls on "frightened" — the emotional content word.
Original example
"When his temperature spiked overnight, we got really frightened and called the on-call doctor."
"His breathing sounded different in the night — we got really frightened and brought him straight to A&E."
PHRASE 2
"Apart from medications" / "Other than that"
a-PART from med-i-CAY-shuns
Meaning: introducing an exception — "excluding/besides X". Used to narrow down a question (e.g., asking about non-drug treatments).
Original example
"Apart from medications, have you tried any physiotherapy for the pain? Other than that, is there anything else troubling you?"
"Apart from medications, I haven't really changed anything else in my routine."
PHRASE 3
"Away with the fairies"
a-WAY with the FAIR-eez
Meaning: an informal British idiom describing someone who is confused, not fully alert, or mentally "elsewhere" — often used for delirium or disorientation in elderly patients. Common in colloquial speech, not formal clinical notes.
Original example
"By evening, the patient seemed a bit away with the fairies — confused and not quite making sense."
"The sedative made him feel completely away with the fairies — he could not hold a normal conversation for the first few hours."
PHRASE 4
Confused, disconnected, lightheaded
kuhn-FYOOZD / dis-kuh-NEK-tid / LYT-hed-id
Meaning: a cluster of words describing altered mental state or dizziness — common symptom descriptions in patient histories.
Original example
"She described feeling lightheaded and disconnected, as though she couldn't quite focus on the conversation."
"He seemed confused and disconnected, taking longer than usual to answer simple questions."
PHRASE 5
"To feel sick"
to feel SIK
Meaning: in British English, "to feel sick" usually means to feel nauseated (about to vomit) — NOT "to feel generally unwell/ill" as in American English. This distinction matters for OET.
Original example
"He started to feel sick about twenty minutes after taking the medication on an empty stomach."
"She called the pharmacy to say she was feeling very sick after the first dose."
PHRASE 6
"...quite the opposite"
kwyt thee OP-uh-zit
Meaning: used to contrast an expectation with reality — "not improving, but actually getting worse" (or vice versa). Strong stress on "opposite" signals the contrast.
Original example
"We expected the swelling to go down, but it didn't improve — quite the opposite, in fact."
"He reported that the pain had not got any better — in fact quite the opposite — it had doubled in intensity overnight."
PHRASE 7
"I was in too much pain to drive"
I was in too much PAYN to DRYV
Meaning: pain severe enough to prevent a normal activity — a useful structure: "too much [X] to [verb]" for describing functional limitation.
Original example
"By the third day, I was in too much pain to drive myself to the appointment."
"He had been unable to attend his follow-up appointment — he was in too much pain to drive."
PHRASE 8
"What have you been taking to deal with the pain?"
what have you been TAY-king to DEEL with the pain
Meaning: a natural clinical question asking about self-managed pain relief before consultation. Good model phrase for OET Speaking history-taking.
Original example
"Before we discuss next steps — what have you been taking to deal with the pain so far?"
"What have you been taking to deal with the pain? That helps me make sure nothing interacts with what I'd like to recommend."
PHRASE 9
"I can't say they made much difference"
I can't say they made much DIF-runs
Meaning: a polite, hedged way of saying "it didn't really help" — softer than "it didn't work at all". Useful in patient-reported outcomes.
Original example
"I tried the painkillers you gave me, but honestly, I can't say they made much difference."
"She had tried three different over-the-counter pain relievers but could not say they had made much difference."
PHRASE 10
"A long-haul flight"
a long-HAWL flyt
Meaning: a long-distance international flight (typically over 6 hours) — relevant context for DVT risk, jet lag, or travel medicine history.
Original example
"After a long-haul flight to Europe, his ankle became noticeably swollen."
"She reported a dull aching pain in her calf that had developed after a long-haul flight."
PHRASE 11
"Brutal"
BROO-tl 2 syllables
/ˈbruːtl/
Meaning: informal intensifier meaning "extremely harsh/severe" — often used colloquially to describe an unpleasant experience (e.g., a tough recovery or harsh side effects).
Original example
"The first few days after the surgery were brutal — far worse than I expected."
"The fatigue from her condition was brutal — she described it as completely draining her will to function."
PHRASE 12
"I'd get over it"
I'd get OH-ver it
Meaning: "I would eventually recover/feel better" — informal way of expressing confidence in natural recovery.
Original example
"I just assumed it was a normal cold and I'd get over it in a few days."
"He kept telling himself he'd get over it, but the symptoms only worsened over the following week."
PHRASE 13
Achy muscles / muscle ache
AY-kee MUS-uhlz
Meaning: generalized muscular discomfort, often associated with flu, infection, or overexertion. "Achy" is informal; "myalgia" is the clinical term.
Original example
"Alongside the fever, she complained of achy muscles all over her body."
"He reported achy muscles across his whole body — consistent with a viral illness."
PHRASE 14
"I just felt lousy" / "under the weather" / "run down"
I just felt LOW-zee
Meaning: three informal synonyms for "generally unwell, low energy". All common in patient self-reports — none are clinical terms, so avoid using them in formal write-ups.
Original example
"I'd been feeling run down for about a week before the rash appeared — just generally under the weather, you know, lousy."
"She said she just felt lousy — no single dominant symptom but a general sense of being run down."
PHRASE 15
"I did get a few bites when we were there"
I did get a few BYTS
Meaning: referring to insect bites sustained during travel — relevant in travel medicine histories (malaria, dengue exposure).
Original example
"We were careful with the repellent, but I did get a few bites when we were there, mostly on my legs."
"Despite using repellent regularly, I did get a few bites during the evenings when we were outdoors."
PHRASE 16
"Some sorts of malaria resistant to these drugs"
re-ZIS-tunt to theez drugz
Meaning: describing antimalarial drug resistance in certain regions — relevant clinical/travel-health vocabulary. Stress falls on "resistant".
Original example
"In that region, there are some sorts of malaria resistant to these drugs, so we need a different prophylaxis."
"He was worried because he had heard that some sorts of malaria are resistant to these drugs in the region he was visiting."
PHRASE 17
"Sweating buckets"
SWET-ing BUK-its
Meaning: informal idiom for profuse/excessive sweating — vivid, colloquial alternative to "diaphoretic" (the clinical term).
Original example
"During the fever spike, he was sweating buckets and had to change his shirt twice."
"He described sweating buckets throughout the night — drenching his sheets repeatedly."
PHRASE 18
"Splitting headache"
SPLIT-ing HED-ayk
Meaning: an intense, severe headache — "splitting" is an intensifier idiom (like "splitting pain"), commonly used informally by patients.
Original example
"She woke up with a splitting headache that wouldn't respond to paracetamol."
"He described a splitting headache that had come on suddenly during exertion."
PHRASE 19
"I can't keep anything down" / "I was throwing up all day"
I can't keep EN-ee-thing down
Meaning: severe nausea/vomiting preventing food or fluid retention — common informal patient description; clinical equivalent is "unable to tolerate oral intake".
Original example
"I was throwing up all day yesterday, and now I can't keep anything down, not even water."
"She called the pharmacy to report she could not keep anything down — every dose was immediately vomited back up."
PHRASE 20
"Being overrun with queries/interruptions"
oh-vuh-RUN with KWEER-eez
Meaning: overwhelmed by a high volume of questions or disruptions — useful for describing a busy clinical workload (e.g., pharmacy counter, ward duties).
Original example
"The pharmacy was being overrun with queries that afternoon, which delayed the medication review."
"The reception desk was overrun with interruptions all morning, so the consultation ran well over time."
PHRASE 21
"A bit groggy"
a bit GROG-ee
Meaning: feeling dazed, sluggish, or not fully alert — commonly used after anaesthesia, sedation, or waking up.
Original example
"He was a bit groggy when he woke up from the anaesthetic, but otherwise stable."
"She felt a bit groggy for the rest of the afternoon after the sedation wore off."
PHRASE 22
"A porter"
a POR-ter 2 syllables
/ˈpɔːrtər/
Meaning: a hospital staff member who transports patients/equipment between wards — common role in UK hospital settings, distinct from a nurse or orderly.
Original example
"A porter will come to take you down to radiology in about ten minutes."
"A porter transferred the patient to the ward once the bed was ready."
PHRASE 23
"Tingling in the fingers"
TING-gling in the FING-gerz
Meaning: pins-and-needles sensation — common symptom description for nerve compression, circulation issues, or paraesthesia.
Original example
"She reported tingling in the fingers of her right hand, especially at night."
"He described tingling in his fingers that worsened whenever he typed for long periods."
PHRASE 24
"Rehab" / "Rehabilitation"
REE-hab / ree-uh-bil-i-TAY-shun
/ˈriːhæb/ — /ˌriːəˌbɪlɪˈteɪʃən/
⚠️ "Rehab" stresses the FIRST syllable. "Rehabilitation" has its main stress on the 5th syllable ("TAY") — six syllables total, so keep the middle ones light and quick.
Original example
"He'll need several weeks of rehab before returning to full physical activity."
"She was referred for rehabilitation following her hip replacement surgery."
PHRASE 25
"To disrupt the scar tissue"
to dis-RUPT the SKAR TISH-oo
Meaning: physiotherapy technique to break down restrictive scar tissue and improve mobility after surgery or injury.
Original example
"The physiotherapist used massage techniques to disrupt the scar tissue around the incision."
"Deep tissue massage was used to disrupt the scar tissue that had formed after the operation."
PHRASE 26
Elective surgery vs. Emergency surgery
i-LEK-tiv / i-MER-jun-see
Meaning: "elective" = planned/non-urgent surgery (patient's choice/scheduling); "emergency" = immediate, life-threatening need. Important contrast pair for OET.
Original example
"Unlike his elective knee surgery last year, this time it was emergency surgery due to internal bleeding."
"The hernia repair was scheduled as elective surgery, unlike the appendectomy, which required emergency surgery."
PHRASE 27
"The limited window available to prepare"
the LIM-i-tid WIN-doh
Meaning: a short, fixed amount of time available before something must happen — "window" here means "time opportunity", not a physical window.
Original example
"Given the limited window available to prepare, the team focused only on essential pre-op checks."
"With the limited window available to prepare, the consent discussion had to be brief but thorough."
PHRASE 28
"I have a few niggles in my knee" / "just a bit of a niggle"
a few NIG-uhlz
/ˈnɪɡl/
Meaning: a minor, nagging discomfort — not serious pain, just a small recurring annoyance. Very informal/colloquial, common in British English.
Original example
"It's nothing serious, just a bit of a niggle in my knee after long walks."
"He said he had a few niggles in his knee when descending stairs but otherwise managed well."
PHRASE 29
"Paid for it"
payd for it
Meaning: idiom meaning "suffered the consequences" (not literally about money) — e.g., overexerting and then suffering pain afterward.
Original example
"He went back to the gym too soon after surgery, and he paid for it the next day."
"She pushed through her shift despite the pain and really paid for it that evening."
PHRASE 30
"Trying to assimilate the fact with..."
uh-SIM-i-layt
/əˈsɪmɪleɪt/
Meaning: "to assimilate" = to absorb/incorporate new information into existing understanding. Stress on the SECOND syllable.
Original example
"It took her a while to assimilate the fact that the diagnosis had changed."
"He was still trying to assimilate the fact that the surgery would need to be postponed."
PHRASE 31
"We would quickly associate the answer with personality"
uh-SOH-shee-ayt
/əˈsoʊʃieɪt/
Meaning: "to associate" = to mentally connect one thing with another. Stress on the SECOND syllable. Note this differs from "assimilate" — easy to confuse the two.
Original example
"People often associate a slow response with shyness, though it isn't always accurate."
"Clinicians should be careful not to associate a quiet demeanour with disengagement."
PHRASE 32
"Immediately assign the person as shy or reserved"
uh-SYN
/əˈsaɪn/
Meaning: "to assign" here means "to label/categorise" someone. Stress on the SECOND syllable. Note the three similar verbs: assimilate / associate / assign — each has a distinct meaning and stress pattern.
Original example
"It's unfair to immediately assign a quiet patient as being uncooperative."
"Staff were reminded not to immediately assign reluctance to a single difficult interaction."
PHRASE 33
"You're bound to feel weary after an operation"
bownd to feel WEER-ee
Meaning: "bound to" = certain/very likely to; "weary" = tired/exhausted (slightly more formal/literary than "tired").
Original example
"You're bound to feel weary after an operation like that — it's completely normal."
"'You're bound to feel weary for the first few days,' the nurse reassured him after the procedure."
PHRASE 34
"Doubtless" / "Mind-boggling"
DOWT-lis / MYND-bog-ling
/ˈdaʊtlɪs/ — /ˈmaɪndˌbɒɡlɪŋ/
Meaning: "doubtless" = certainly, without doubt (formal adverb). "Mind-boggling" = extremely surprising or hard to comprehend (informal). Both stress the first syllable.
Original example
"Doubtless, the new treatment will help — though the range of possible side effects is honestly mind-boggling."
"The sheer number of drug interactions to check was mind-boggling for the new graduate pharmacist."
WORD 35
POPE
POHP 1 syllable
/poʊp/
⚠️ The vowel is a long "oh" diphthong (like "hope", "rope") — not a short "o". Don't round or shorten it.
Example 1
The Pope made a statement about end-of-life care from the Vatican.
Example 2
News of the Pope's hospitalisation spread quickly across international media.
📌 Same stress pattern:
hope → HOHP  |  cope → KOHP  |  scope → SKOHP
WORD 36
PROSTHETIC
pros-THET-ik 3 syllables
/prɒsˈθetɪk/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable. The "th" is a dental fricative (tongue between teeth, like "think"). Don't say "pros-TET-ik" — the "th" sound must be clear.
Example 1
The patient's discomfort may be due to a poorly fitting prosthetic limb.
Example 2
Advances in prosthetic technology have significantly improved mobility for amputees.
📌 Same stress pattern:
aesthetic → es-THET-ik  |  synthetic → sin-THET-ik  |  pathetic → puh-THET-ik
WORD 37
AMPUTATION
am-pyoo-TAY-shun 4 syllables
/ˌæmpjʊˈteɪʃən/
⚠️ Stress on the THIRD syllable ("TAY"). Note: the second syllable contains "yoo" (like "pure"), not "poo".
Example 1
Many patients report phantom limb pain even years after amputation.
Example 2
The surgical team performed a below-knee amputation to prevent further spread of the infection.
📌 Same stress pattern:
vaccination → vak-si-NAY-shun  |  intubation → in-tyoo-BAY-shun  |  medication → med-i-KAY-shun
WORD 38
ELECTRODE
i-LEK-trohd 3 syllables
/ɪˈlektrəʊd/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable ("LEK"). The first syllable reduces to a schwa — say "ih-LEK-trohd", not "EE-lek-trohd".
Example 1
We attached electrodes to the remaining muscles of the stump to measure activity.
Example 2
Each electrode was positioned carefully to ensure an accurate reading.
📌 Same stress pattern:
explode → ik-SPLOHD  |  erode → i-ROHD  |  corrode → kuh-ROHD
WORD 39
CONSECUTIVE
kun-SEK-yuh-tiv 4 syllables
/kənˈsekjʊtɪv/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable ("SEK"). First syllable is a schwa — quick and light. Don't stress the first "con-" as in "CON-secutive".
Example 1
The patient attended three consecutive physiotherapy sessions without any improvement.
Example 2
She had missed her medication on two consecutive days.
📌 Same stress pattern:
executive → ig-ZEK-yuh-tiv  |  perspective → per-SPEK-tiv  |  subjective → sub-JEK-tiv
WORD 40
ABOUT
uh-BOWT 2 syllables
/əˈbaʊt/
⚠️ This word is frequently MISPRONOUNCED by Korean speakers as "ah-BOUT" (using a clear "ah" vowel on the first syllable). In natural English, the first syllable is always a schwa — a very light "uh". The second syllable rhymes with "shout", "out".
Example 1
Can you tell me about any medications you are currently taking?
Example 2
I'm about to refer you to a specialist for further assessment.
📌 Same stress pattern:
above → uh-BUV  |  around → uh-ROWND  |  across → uh-KROS
WORD 41
CERTAINLY
SUR-tun-lee 3 syllables
/ˈsɜːtənli/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. In fast natural speech, the middle syllable reduces significantly — sounds almost like "SUR-tn-lee". Don't over-pronounce the middle "tain" — it becomes "tun" quickly.
Example 1
"Certainly, I can arrange a follow-up appointment for you next week."
Example 2
This medication will certainly help to reduce the inflammation.
📌 Same stress pattern:
suddenly → SUD-un-lee  |  possibly → POS-uh-blee  |  clearly → KLEER-lee
WORD 42
VASODILATION
vay-zoh-dy-LAY-shun 5 syllables
/ˌveɪzəʊdaɪˈleɪʃən/
⚠️ Main stress on the 5th syllable ("LAY") with secondary stress on the 1st ("VAY"). The "vaso-" prefix rhymes with "lazy", not "vah-so". Cambridge confirmed this stress pattern.
Example 1
Vasodilation causes blood vessels to widen, lowering blood pressure.
Example 2
Some antihypertensives work through vasodilation of peripheral arteries.
📌 Same stress pattern:
inhalation → in-huh-LAY-shun  |  radiation → ray-dee-AY-shun  |  simulation → sim-yuh-LAY-shun
WORD 43
ARTERIOLES
ar-TEER-ee-ohlz 4 syllables
/ɑːˈtɪəriəʊlz/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable ("TEER"). Cambridge confirmed: /ɑːˈtɪəriəʊl/. Common error is putting stress on the first syllable (AR-tee-oles).
Example 1
The arterioles dilate in response to increased body temperature during exercise.
Example 2
Damage to small arterioles is a common complication of uncontrolled hypertension.
📌 Same stress pattern:
arterial → ar-TEER-ee-ul  |  material → muh-TEER-ee-ul  |  bacteria → bak-TEER-ee-uh
WORD 44
SIGNIFICANTLY
sig-NIF-i-kunt-lee 5 syllables
/sɪɡˈnɪfɪkəntli/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable ("NIF"). Keep the middle syllables light — many learners over-emphasise each syllable equally which sounds robotic.
Example 1
The patient's pain levels had significantly improved after two weeks of treatment.
Example 2
This medication significantly reduces the risk of further clot formation.
📌 Same stress pattern:
specifically → spuh-SIF-ik-lee  |  terrifically → tuh-RIF-ik-lee  |  magnificently → mag-NIF-i-sunt-lee
WORD 45
BLOCKAGE
BLOK-ij 2 syllables
/ˈblɒkɪdʒ/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. The ending "-age" is reduced — sounds like "-ij", not "-ayj". Don't say "blok-AYJ".
Example 1
The scan revealed a partial blockage in the left coronary artery.
Example 2
A nasal blockage can make breathing through the nose very difficult.
📌 Same stress pattern:
dosage → DOH-sij  |  drainage → DRAYN-ij  |  shortage → SHORT-ij
WORD 46
PHARMACY
FAR-muh-see 3 syllables
/ˈfɑːrməsi/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. The second and third syllables reduce — "muh-see" (both weak). Many learners stress the wrong syllable or make all three equal.
Example 1
Please take this prescription to the pharmacy before 5pm.
Example 2
The pharmacy team reviewed all the patient's medications before discharge.
📌 Same stress pattern:
therapy → THER-uh-pee  |  surgery → SUR-juh-ree  |  urgency → UR-jun-see
WORD 47
WORK / WALK
WURK / WAWK 1 syllable each
/wɜːk/ — /wɔːk/
⚠️ These two words are frequently confused. "Work" has a "ur" sound (like "bird", "nurse"). "Walk" has a long "aw" sound (like "talk", "law") — the "l" is SILENT. Don't say "wal-k" with a clear "l".
Example 1
The medication should work within 30 minutes of taking it.
Example 2
He found it very difficult to walk after the surgery.
📌 Same stress pattern:
word → WURD → world → WURLD  |  talk → TAWK → chalk → CHAWK  |  worth → WURTH → stalk → STAWK
WORD 48
MASSIVE
MAS-iv 2 syllables
/ˈmæsɪv/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. The vowel in "MAS-" is a short "æ" (as in "cat", "bad") — not a long "ah" sound. The ending "-ive" reduces to "-iv".
Example 1
He suffered a massive stroke and was rushed to intensive care.
Example 2
There was a massive improvement in her condition after starting the new medication.
📌 Same stress pattern:
passive → PAS-iv  |  active → AK-tiv  |  native → NAY-tiv
WORD 49
PERFECT
PUR-fekt 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈpɜː.fɪkt/ | American: /ˈpɝ.fɪkt/ | NZ/AU: /ˈpɜː.fekt/
⚠️ British English (RP) uses "PUR-fikt" (second syllable rhymes with "licked") — this is the target for your OET preparation and future plans in Germany. NZ/Australian English uses "PUR-fekt" (second syllable rhymes with "wrecked"), which is why Google Translate may show a different version. As a VERB ("to perfect something"), stress shifts to the SECOND syllable: pur-FEKT.
Example 1
Your blood pressure reading is perfect today — keep up the good work.
Example 2
This is the perfect opportunity to review all your current medications.
📌 Same stress pattern:
market → MAR-kit  |  blanket → BLANG-kit  |  basket → BAS-kit
WORD 50
MUCUS / MUCOSA / MUCOUS
MYOO-kus / myoo-KOH-zuh / MYOO-kus 2 / 3 / 2 syllables
/ˈmjuːkəs/ — /mjuːˈkəʊzə/ — /ˈmjuːkəs/
⚠️ KEY DISTINCTION: "Mucus" (noun) = the secretion itself. "Mucous" (adjective) = relating to mucus — same pronunciation. "Mucosa" = the mucous membrane itself — stress shifts to 2nd syllable ("KOH"). Note: "mucous" (adj) and "mucus" (noun) are spelled differently but pronounced the same.
Example 1
The patient had excess mucus in the airways, making it difficult to breathe.
Example 2
Inflammation of the nasal mucosa is commonly associated with allergic rhinitis.
📌 Same stress pattern:
virus → VY-rus  |  focus → FOH-kus  |  stimulus → STIM-yuh-lus
WORD 51
STUFFY
STUF-ee 2 syllables
/ˈstʌfi/
⚠️ Stress on FIRST syllable. Short "u" vowel (like "cup", "stuff"). Common informal clinical expression — "stuffy nose" = nasal congestion. The clinical equivalent is "nasal congestion" or "blocked nose".
Example 1
She reported a stuffy nose and a mild sore throat for the past three days.
Example 2
A stuffy nose at night can significantly disrupt sleep quality.
📌 Same stress pattern:
runny → RUN-ee  |  itchy → ICH-ee  |  puffy → PUF-ee
WORD 52
REFER / REFERRAL
ri-FUR / ri-FUR-ul 2 / 3 syllables
/rɪˈfɜːr/ — /rɪˈfɜːrəl/
⚠️ Both words stress the SECOND syllable. The vowel in "-FUR-" is the same as "her", "nurse". Common error: "REF-er" (wrong stress) or "ref-ER-al" (wrong vowel).
Example 1
I'd like to refer you to a dermatologist for further assessment.
Example 2
The GP sent a referral to the cardiology department after reviewing the ECG.
📌 Same stress pattern:
prefer → pri-FUR  |  defer → di-FUR  |  confer → kun-FUR
WORD 53
SINUS
SY-nus 2 syllables
/ˈsaɪnəs/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. The "i" in "si-" makes a long "eye" sound (like "sign", "sine"). Don't say "SIN-us" with a short "i".
Example 1
The pain behind her eyes suggested a sinus infection rather than a tension headache.
Example 2
Chronic sinus congestion can sometimes be treated with a nasal steroid spray.
📌 Same stress pattern:
minus → MY-nus  |  virus → VY-rus  |  fibrous → FY-brus
WORD 54
"Rung up" (rang up / called)
rung UP
/rʌŋ ʌp/
Meaning: informal British expression — "to ring up" = to telephone someone. Past tense: "rang up"; past participle: "rung up". "She rung up the clinic" = "She called the clinic." Stress on the particle "up" in natural speech.
Example 1
He rung up the pharmacy to check if his prescription was ready.
Example 2
The patient's carer rang up overnight to report a worsening fever.
WORD 55
SUBTRACT
sub-TRAKT 2 syllables
/səbˈtrækt/
⚠️ Stress on the SECOND syllable ("TRAKT"). First syllable reduces to schwa — "suh-TRAKT". In clinical contexts, "subtract" may appear in scoring tools (e.g., serial 7s cognitive test) or dosage calculations.
Example 1
In the serial 7s test, the patient is asked to subtract 7 from 100 repeatedly.
Example 2
To calculate the net dose, subtract the morning dose already administered.
📌 Same stress pattern:
extract → ik-STRAKT  |  distract → dis-TRAKT  |  contract → kun-TRAKT

💬 Section C: Clinical Sentences & Key Phrases

These are full clinical sentences or contextual phrases. The focus here is on natural sentence rhythm — which words carry the stress and which reduce — rather than individual syllable stress.
PHRASE 35
"This might be due to a poorly fitting prosthetic"
this might be due to a POOR-ly FIT-ing pros-THET-ik
Key stressed words: "poorly", "fitting", "prosthetic" — these carry the information. "This", "might", "be", "due", "to", "a" are all light and fast.
In context
"The increased pain this month might be due to a poorly fitting prosthetic rather than the underlying condition."
"The clinician suspected this might be due to a poorly fitting prosthetic rather than nerve damage."
PHRASE 36
"However, I still tend to think it's large numbers"
I still tend to think it's LARGE NUM-berz
Meaning: "I believe it affects a significant proportion of people". Stress falls on "large numbers" — the content. "However", "still", "tend to think" are hedging phrases that carry moderate stress.
In context
"Studies vary on the exact prevalence, however, I still tend to think it's large numbers who experience this."
"However, I still tend to think it's large numbers based on what I've seen in clinic."
PHRASE 37
"Many patients report feeling as if the missing limb is still attached"
MIS-ing LIM / still at-TACHT
Meaning: phantom limb sensation — a key clinical concept. Key stressed words: "missing limb", "still attached", "body". "Amputation" = am-pyoo-TAY-shun (see Word 37).
In context
"Many patients report feeling as if the missing limb is still attached to their body, even years after the amputation."
"Many patients report phantom sensations even years after the amputation, which can still be distressing."
PHRASE 38
"Telescoping" — the sensation that the limb has shrunk
TEL-uh-skoh-ping
/ˈtelɪskəʊpɪŋ/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable ("TEL"). In phantom limb contexts, "telescoping" describes the perception that the missing limb has become shorter or shrunk inward — a recognized clinical phenomenon.
Example 1
"Some patients experience what we call telescoping — the sensation that the missing limb is still present but has somehow become smaller."
Example 2
Telescoping is more commonly reported in upper limb amputees than lower limb amputees.

📌 New Words — Batch 4

WORD
CONJUNCTIVITIS
con-junk-ti-VY-tis 5 syllables
British (RP): /kənˌdʒʌŋktɪˈvaɪtɪs/
⚠️ Stress on the 5th syllable ("VY" — rhymes with "eye"). The "-itis" suffix always attracts stress to the syllable immediately before it. Common error: stressing "JUNK" (2nd syllable) instead. Also note: the "c" before "t" is silent — it's con-JUNK-ti-VY-tis, not con-JUNK-tiv-IT-is.
Example 1
The patient presented with conjunctivitis, with redness and discharge in both eyes.
Example 2
Bacterial conjunctivitis is now one of the conditions pharmacists can treat under the new funded scheme.
📌 Same stress pattern — "-itis" always stresses the syllable before it:
appendicitis → uh-pen-di-SY-tis  |  hepatitis → hep-uh-TY-tis  |  bronchitis → brong-KY-tis
WORD
MISDIAGNOSIS
mis-dy-ug-NOH-sis 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌmɪsdaɪəɡˈnəʊsɪs/
⚠️ CORRECTION: Primary stress falls on the 4th syllable "NOH" — NOT on "AG" as is commonly assumed. Cambridge IPA /ˌmɪsdaɪəɡˈnəʊsɪs/ confirms the ˈ stress mark is on "nəʊ". The "mis-" prefix carries secondary stress only. Think of the root: "gnosis" (knowledge) → the stress stays on "gnoh" = NOH.
Example 1
Misdiagnosis is less likely when pharmacists carefully assess symptoms before referring patients.
Example 2
The GP raised concerns about the risk of misdiagnosis, but Sam argued the conditions are well within pharmacists' expertise.
📌 Same stress pattern — "-osis" attracts stress to the syllable before it:
psychosis → sy-KOH-sis  |  fibrosis → fy-BROH-sis  |  neurosis → nyoo-ROH-sis
WORD
PRESCRIPTION
pre-SKRIP-shun 3 syllables
British (RP): /prɪˈskrɪpʃən/
⚠️ Stress on the 2nd syllable ("SKRIP"). The "-tion" ending NEVER carries stress in English. The "pre-" prefix reduces to a schwa — say "pruh-SKRIP-shun", not "PREE-skrip-shun".
Example 1
Please hand your prescription to the pharmacist at the counter.
Example 2
Some medications previously available only by prescription are now available over the counter.
📌 Same stress pattern — "-scription" always stresses "SKRIP":
description → di-SKRIP-shun  |  subscription → sub-SKRIP-shun  |  inscription → in-SKRIP-shun
WORD
DIFFERENTIAL
dif-uh-REN-shul 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌdɪfəˈrenʃəl/
⚠️ CORRECTION: Primary stress is on the 3rd syllable "REN" — NOT on "FER" (2nd syllable) as previously noted. Cambridge IPA /ˌdɪfəˈrenʃəl/ confirms the ˈ stress mark is on "ren". The "-ential" suffix pulls stress to the syllable directly before it: "REN-shul". Common in "differential diagnosis" — a key OET term.
Example 1
A differential diagnosis is essential when symptoms could indicate more than one condition.
Example 2
The pharmacist considered the differential between a drug-induced rash and an allergic reaction.
📌 Same stress pattern — "-ential" pulls stress to the syllable before it:
potential → puh-TEN-shul  |  essential → i-SEN-shul  |  credential → kruh-DEN-shul
WORD
ESCALATE
ES-kuh-layt 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈeskəleɪt/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable ("ES"). The middle syllable reduces to schwa — "ES-kuh-layt", not "es-KUH-layt" or "es-kuh-LAYT". As a clinical verb, it means to intensify or refer to a higher level of care.
Example 1
If symptoms escalate or do not improve within 48 hours, please contact your GP immediately.
Example 2
Pharmacists must know when to escalate care and refer patients to a specialist.
📌 Same stress pattern — 3-syllable verbs stressed on 1st syllable:
speculate → SPEK-yuh-layt  |  simulate → SIM-yuh-layt  |  calculate → KAL-kyuh-layt
WORD
SCHEME
SKEEM 1 syllable
British (RP): /skiːm/
⚠️ The "sch" in "scheme" makes a "sk" sound — NOT an "sh" sound. This is a key distinction in British English: "school" and "scheme" = SK sound; BUT "schedule" in British RP = SHED-yool (SH sound!). Don't say "sheem". The vowel is a long "ee" (like "cream", "dream").
Example 1
The new funded scheme allows pharmacists to treat minor conditions without a GP referral.
Example 2
The government's pharmacy scheme has significantly reduced pressure on GP surgeries.
📌 "SCH" = SK sound (same as these words):
school → SKOOL  |  scholar → SKOL-ur  |  ⚠️ BUT: schedule (British) → SHED-yool
WORD
EXPIRE
ik-SPYR 2 syllables
British (RP): /ɪkˈspaɪə/
⚠️ Stress on the 2nd syllable ("SPYR" — rhymes with "fire"). The first syllable reduces to a very brief "ik" — NOT "ex" with a clear "e". In British RP, the final "r" is not pronounced unless followed by a vowel: "ik-SPYR-uh" (before a vowel) vs "ik-SPYR" (at end of phrase).
Example 1
Please check when your prescription expires before collecting your repeat medication.
Example 2
Medicines should not be taken after they expire, as their effectiveness cannot be guaranteed.
📌 Same stress pattern — 2nd syllable stress:
inspire → in-SPYR  |  require → ri-KWYR  |  acquire → uh-KWYR
WORD
TRIAGE
TREE-ahzh 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈtriːæʒ/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable ("TREE"). The second syllable "-age" ends with a French "zh" sound (like the "s" in "measure" or "vision") — NOT a standard English "age" as in "cage". The word is borrowed from French ("trier" = to sort), and the ending retains the French /ʒ/ sound. This is one of the few English words with this ending sound.
Example 1
The nurse performed triage to assess which patients needed urgent attention first.
Example 2
Pharmacists already triage patients informally — the new scheme simply makes this official and funded.
📌 Same "-azh" French ending sound (borrowed words):
mirage → mi-RAZH  |  camouflage → KAM-uh-flahzh  |  barrage → buh-RAZH

📌 Batch 5 — Paediatric / Dermatology / Clinical Phrases

WORD
PAEDIATRICIAN
pee-dee-uh-TRI-shun 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌpiːdiəˈtrɪʃən/
⚠️ Stress on the 4th syllable "TRI". The word is very long — break it down first: "pee" + "dee" + "uh" + "TRI" + "shun". Note: British spelling is "paediatrician" (with "ae"); American is "pediatrician". Both have the same stress pattern and pronunciation.
Example 1
The GP referred the child to a paediatrician for a full developmental assessment.
Example 2
A paediatrician specialises in the medical care of infants, children, and adolescents.
📌 Same "-ician" stress pattern (stress on "TRI/TI" before "-shun"):
electrician → ee-lek-TRI-shun  |  politician → pol-i-TI-shun  |  beautician → byoo-TI-shun
WORD
ECZEMA
EK-si-muh 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈeksɪmə/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable "EK". A very common error is saying "ec-ZEE-ma" — this is WRONG. The second syllable is a short "si" (not "zee") and the final syllable is a schwa. British RP: EK-si-muh. The "cz" in the spelling is historically Greek — the "c" is silent and "z" = "z" sound.
Example 1
The child's eczema had flared up significantly during the cold winter months.
Example 2
Aqueous cream and emollients are commonly recommended for managing eczema in children.
📌 Same first-syllable stress, 3-syllable pattern:
enema → EN-uh-muh  |  stigma → STIG-muh  |  asthma → AZ-muh (British: AZ, not ASS)
WORD
AQUEOUS CREAM
AY-kwee-us KREEM 4 syllables total
British (RP): /ˈeɪkwɪəs kriːm/
⚠️ "Aqueous" — stress on the FIRST syllable "AY" (not "AH"). The "aqu-" root comes from Latin "aqua" (water) but in English it becomes "AY-kwee". Don't say "AH-kwee-us" or "uh-KWEE-us". "Cream" = KREEM (long "ee" sound).
Example 1
Aqueous cream is a basic emollient used to moisturise dry or eczematous skin.
Example 2
The pharmacist advised applying aqueous cream after bathing to help retain moisture.
📌 Same "AY-" first syllable pattern (Latin "a" → English "ay"):
various → VAIR-ee-us  |  curious → KYOOR-ee-us  |  obvious → OB-vee-us
WORD
PETROLEUM JELLY
puh-TROH-lee-um JEL-ee 6 syllables total
British (RP): /pəˈtrəʊlɪəm ˈdʒeli/
⚠️ "Petroleum" — stress on the 2nd syllable "TROH". The first syllable reduces to schwa "puh". "Jelly" — stress on the 1st syllable "JEL". Also known as Vaseline (brand name) — used clinically as a barrier cream for dry or irritated skin.
Example 1
Petroleum jelly can be applied to dry skin around the nose during a cold to reduce soreness.
Example 2
Some parents use petroleum jelly as a barrier cream to protect against nappy rash.
📌 Same 2nd-syllable stress pattern for "-roleum" words:
linoleum → li-NOH-lee-um  |  mausoleum → maw-suh-LEE-um  |  calcium → KAL-see-um (1st syllable — different!)
WORD
GALLSTONES
GAWL-stohnz 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈɡɔːlstəʊnz/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable "GAWL" (rhymes with "call", "hall", "fall"). The "all" in "gall" = "awl" sound — NOT "gal" as in "gallon". In British RP the vowel is long: "GAWL", not short "gal".
Example 1
The ultrasound confirmed the presence of gallstones in the patient's gallbladder.
Example 2
A high-fat diet is associated with an increased risk of developing gallstones.
📌 Same compound word pattern (stress on first element):
kidney stones → KID-nee stohnz  |  milestones → MYL-stohnz  |  heartburn → HART-burn
WORD
CONSTANT
KON-stunt 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈkɒnstənt/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. The second syllable "-stant" reduces to a quick schwa: "stunt". In British RP the first vowel "o" = short /ɒ/ (like "hot", "got") — not "kahn-stunt" (American) or "kon-STANT".
Example 1
He described a constant dull ache in his lower back that had persisted for three weeks.
Example 2
The constant itching was preventing the child from sleeping through the night.
📌 Same first-syllable stress, 2-syllable adjectives:
pleasant → PLEZ-unt  |  frequent → FREE-kwunt  |  urgent → UR-junt
WORD
TANTRUMS
TAN-trumz 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈtæntrəmz/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable. The vowel in "TAN-" is a short /æ/ — like "cat", "bad". Don't say "TEN-trumz" or "TON-trumz". The plural "-trums" = "trumz" (voiced z at end).
Example 1
The parents reported frequent tantrums whenever the child's routine was disrupted.
Example 2
Extreme tantrums beyond the expected age can sometimes indicate an underlying developmental issue.
📌 Same short /æ/ vowel + 2-syllable stress-first pattern:
symptoms → SIMP-tumz  |  patterns → PAT-urnz  |  fractures → FRAK-churz
WORD
HEARTBURN
HART-burn 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈhɑːtbɜːn/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable "HART". In British RP the "ear" in "heart" = "HART" (no "r" pronounced unless followed by a vowel). "Burn" = "burn" with a short "ur" sound (like "bird", "nurse"). This is the compound word for gastric reflux — the clinical term is "gastro-oesophageal reflux" (GORD) or "acid reflux".
Example 1
She reported worsening heartburn after meals, particularly with fatty or spicy foods.
Example 2
Heartburn is a common symptom of gastro-oesophageal reflux disease.
📌 Same compound word stress on first element:
sunburn → SUN-burn  |  heartbeat → HART-beet  |  windpipe → WIND-pyp
WORD
ASPERGER'S
AS-pur-gurz 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈæspɜːɡərz/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable "AS". Named after Austrian physician Hans Asperger. The "er" in "-per" and "-ger" both use the British schwa-r sound (not a strong "r"). In current clinical use, "Asperger's syndrome" is now included within the broader diagnosis of Autism Spectrum Disorder (ASD).
Example 1
She mentioned she had heard about Asperger's and wondered if her son's behaviour matched the description.
Example 2
A formal assessment is needed before a diagnosis of Asperger's or ASD can be confirmed.
📌 Same eponymous syndrome naming pattern (stress on first syllable):
Alzheimer's → ALZ-hy-murz  |  Parkinson's → PAR-kin-sunz  |  Turner's → TUR-nurz
WORD
CALF (body part)
KAHF 1 syllable — "L" IS SILENT
British (RP): /kɑːf/
⚠️ The "L" in "calf" is completely SILENT in British RP. It is pronounced "KAHF" — NOT "kalf". This silent "L" rule applies consistently to many English words with "-alf", "-alm", "-alk", "-alve". Deep vein thrombosis (DVT) often presents as pain or swelling "in the calf".
Example 1
She presented with sudden swelling and pain in her calf following a long-haul flight.
Example 2
A warm, red, swollen calf can be a sign of deep vein thrombosis.
📌 Same silent "L" pattern in British RP:
calm → KAHM  |  palm → PAHM  |  half → HAHF
WORD
BOWEL (HABITS)
BOW-ul 2 syllables — rhymes with "OWL"
British (RP): /ˈbaʊəl/
⚠️ "Bowel" rhymes with "OWL", "FOWL", "TOWEL" — the "ow" is a diphthong "ow" sound (as in "cow"). Do NOT say "BOHL" (which would rhyme with "bowl", the dish). This is a very common mistake. "Bowel habits" = the frequency, consistency, and regularity of a patient's stools.
Example 1
The doctor asked about any changes in her bowel habits over the past few weeks.
Example 2
Altered bowel habits, such as alternating constipation and diarrhoea, can be a sign of IBS.
📌 Same "ow" = "ow/oul" sound (rhymes with "owl"):
towel → TOW-ul  |  vowel → VOW-ul  |  trowel → TROW-ul

💬 Idioms & Phrases — Batch 5

PHRASE
"Put it down to that cold snap"
put it down to that COLD SNAP
Meaning: "put it down to X" = attribute or blame something to X. A "cold snap" = a brief, sudden period of cold weather. Together: "I blamed it on that brief cold spell." Common in clinical history-taking when patients describe a trigger they noticed.
In context
"I thought the rash was just irritation, so I put it down to that cold snap we had last month."
"She had put it down to that cold snap, but the rash had persisted for several weeks."
PHRASE
"Pin down what sets it off"
pin DOWN what SETS it off
Meaning: "pin down" = identify precisely or with certainty. "Set off" = trigger. Together: "identify exactly what triggers it." Both are useful OET Speaking/Listening phrasal verbs in patient history-taking.
In context
"We've been trying to pin down what sets off his eczema, but it doesn't seem consistent."
"The doctor asked whether she had managed to pin down what sets off his allergic reaction."
PHRASE
"We've well, kind of given in"
given IN
Meaning: "given in" = surrendered, stopped resisting, accepted defeat. The "well, kind of" = hesitation/hedging language, showing the speaker is slightly embarrassed by the admission. Common in consultations when patients describe not following medical advice.
In context
"We kept trying to limit his screen time, but we've well, kind of given in — it's the only thing that calms him down."
"They had tried to restrict his diet, but eventually just given in because he refused to eat anything else."
PHRASE
"They weren't very practical"
they weren't very PRAK-ti-kul
"Practical" = PRAK-ti-kul (stress on 1st syllable). Meaning: not workable in real-life conditions. Common patient complaint about medical advice that is theoretically correct but difficult to follow daily.
In context
"The doctor suggested cotton-only clothing, but to be honest, they weren't very practical for an active 6-year-old."
"The previous treatment regimen wasn't very practical for someone working long shifts."
PHRASE
"Highly strung"
HIGH-lee STRUNG
Meaning: very tense, anxious, sensitive, and easily upset. A common British informal expression, often used to describe children or adults who are nervous or emotionally reactive. Clinical equivalent might be "emotionally dysregulated" or "anxious temperament".
In context
"His teachers have alluded to the fact that he's highly strung — that's definitely how I'd sum him up too."
"He's always been highly strung — even small changes to his routine can trigger a meltdown."
PHRASE
"Alluded to the fact that..."
uh-LYOOD-id to the fact
"Allude to" = to hint at or reference indirectly without stating clearly. /əˈluːd/ — stress on 2nd syllable "LYOOD". Often used when professionals mention a concern without being explicit: "the teacher alluded to behavioural issues without naming them directly."
In context
"His teachers have alluded to the fact that he struggles socially, but they haven't said anything more specific."
"His teachers have alluded to the fact that he finds it hard to concentrate in group settings."
PHRASE
"Sum him up" / "That's definitely how I'd sum him up"
sum him UP
Meaning: "sum up" = describe someone or something briefly and accurately. The stress falls on "up" (the particle). Useful clinical communication expression when a parent or patient summarises their view of a person or situation.
In context
"Anxious, sensitive, and very particular about routine — that's definitely how I'd sum him up."
"'That's definitely how I'd sum him up too,' the mother agreed — very anxious and easily overwhelmed."
PHRASE
"All hell broke loose"
all HELL broke LOOSE
Meaning: complete chaos suddenly erupted. A very informal British idiom expressing extreme disruption. Patients often use this to describe a child's extreme reaction to a change in routine. "Loose" = LOOS (not LOWZ) — rhymes with "goose", "juice".
In context
"All hell broke loose because he had to go to the childminder on a different day — he just couldn't handle the change."
"All hell broke loose the moment we tried to introduce a new food into his diet."
PHRASE
"He finds it hard to cope with change — is that a fair assessment?"
KOHP with change | FAIR uh-SES-munt
"Cope with" = manage or deal with (stress on "cope"). "Fair assessment" = an accurate, balanced evaluation. "Assessment" = uh-SES-munt (stress on 2nd syllable "SES"). Asking "is that a fair assessment?" is a polite way of checking if your summary is accurate — common in OET consultations.
In context
"He finds it hard to cope with change — is that a fair assessment of what you've been experiencing at home?"
"'He finds it hard to cope with change — is that a fair assessment?' the pharmacist asked gently."
PHRASE
"Cling film" / "He's convinced it'll have germs on it"
KLING film | kuh-VINST
"Cling film" = thin plastic wrap for food (British English; "plastic wrap" = American). "Convinced" = kuh-VINST (stress on 2nd syllable). "He's convinced it'll have germs on it" = he firmly believes, despite evidence to the contrary — often used to describe obsessive or rigid thinking patterns in a child.
In context
"I wrap all his food in cling film when we're out, because he's convinced it'll have germs on it otherwise."
"She now wraps all his food in cling film when they go out, otherwise he refuses to eat."
PHRASE
"My clothes have felt looser recently"
clothes have felt LOO-sur
Meaning: clothes fitting less tightly — a common indirect way patients describe unintentional weight loss without being precise about numbers. "Looser" = LOO-sur (comparative of "loose"). This is a significant symptom — unexplained weight loss is a red flag and should always be investigated.
In context
"I haven't weighed myself, but my clothes have felt looser recently — things I bought last year are now quite baggy."
"She hadn't weighed herself but noticed that her clothes have felt looser recently."
PHRASE
"Getting the food down is hard sometimes"
getting the food DOWN
Meaning: "get food down" = manage to swallow or eat food. "Down" is stressed (the particle carries the meaning here). This phrase suggests difficulty eating — could indicate dysphagia, nausea, poor appetite, or food avoidance. A subtle but clinically important symptom descriptor.
In context
"My appetite has dropped and getting the food down is hard sometimes — even things I normally enjoy."
"She said getting the food down is hard sometimes, especially with solid foods in the evening."
PHRASE
"Just feel shattered"
just feel SHAT-urd
Meaning: "shattered" = completely exhausted (British English informal). Equivalent to "knackered", "drained", "wiped out". Used when fatigue is severe — clinically relevant as persistent exhaustion can indicate anaemia, thyroid disorder, depression, or other systemic conditions. Note: "shattered" in American English usually means "broken into pieces".
In context
"I just feel shattered all the time — even after a full night's sleep, I wake up exhausted."
"She said she just feels shattered by mid-afternoon and can barely get through her shift."
PHRASE
"Hard dropping off to sleep"
dropping OFF to sleep
Meaning: "drop off to sleep" = fall asleep. "Off" is the key particle here. Common patient description for initial insomnia (difficulty falling asleep, as opposed to staying asleep). Clinically important to distinguish: "hard to drop off" = onset insomnia vs "waking in the night" = maintenance insomnia.
In context
"It's really hard dropping off to sleep some nights — my mind just won't switch off."
"Once I'm asleep I'm usually fine, but it's hard dropping off in the first place — it can take hours."
PHRASE
"The responsibility got me down"
responsibility got me DOWN
Meaning: "get someone down" = make someone feel depressed or disheartened (British English phrasal verb). "Down" is stressed (particle = meaning). "Responsibility" = ri-spon-si-BIL-i-tee (stress on 4th syllable "BIL"). Common expression when patients describe low mood linked to burden of caregiving or work.
In context
"I don't mind looking after everyone, but if I'm honest, the responsibility has really started to get me down."
"Looking after both kids and my mother has been exhausting — the responsibility is really getting me down lately."

📌 Batch 6 — Cardiology / Pharmacology

WORD
ANTIBIOTIC RESISTANCE
an-tee-by-OT-ik ri-ZIS-tuns 9 syllables total
British (RP): /ˌæntɪbaɪˈɒtɪk rɪˈzɪstəns/
⚠️ Two stress points: "antibiotic" stress on 4th syllable "OT" — /ˌæntɪbaɪˈɒtɪk/; "resistance" stress on 2nd syllable "ZIS" — /rɪˈzɪstəns/. Common error: stressing "AN" at the start instead of "OT" in the middle.
Example 1
The overuse of antibiotics has contributed significantly to the rise of antibiotic resistance globally.
Example 2
Patients should always complete their full course of antibiotics to help prevent antibiotic resistance.
📌 Same "-otic" stress pattern (4th syllable "OT"):
narcotic → nar-KOT-ik  |  neurotic → nyoo-ROT-ik  |  hypnotic → hip-NOT-ik
WORD
WARFARIN
WAW-fuh-rin 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈwɔːfərɪn/
⚠️ Stress on the FIRST syllable "WAW". In British RP the "war" is non-rhotic — it sounds like "WAW" (same vowel as "jaw", "law", "saw"), NOT "WAR" with a strong "r". The second and third syllables reduce: "fuh-rin". Brand name: Coumadin. Warfarin requires regular INR monitoring — commonly comes up in OET scenarios.
Example 1
The patient was prescribed warfarin to reduce the risk of stroke associated with atrial fibrillation.
Example 2
Patients taking warfarin must avoid foods high in vitamin K, such as spinach and kale, as these can affect INR levels.
📌 Same "-arin/-irin/-ulin" drug ending pattern (1st syllable stress):
heparin → HEP-uh-rin  |  aspirin → AS-puh-rin  |  insulin → IN-syoo-lin
WORD
ATRIAL FIBRILLATION
AY-tree-ul   fib-ri-LAY-shun 7 syllables total
British (RP): /ˈeɪtriəl ˌfɪbrɪˈleɪʃən/
⚠️ Two words — two stress points: "atrial" = AY-tree-ul (stress on 1st); "fibrillation" = fib-ri-LAY-shun (stress on 3rd syllable "LAY"). Common errors: 1) saying "AT-ri-ul" with a short "a"; 2) stressing "FIB" in fibrillation instead of "LAY". Abbreviated as AF or AFib in clinical notes.
Example 1
Warfarin is commonly prescribed to manage stroke risk in patients diagnosed with atrial fibrillation.
Example 2
The patient reported palpitations and was later confirmed to be in atrial fibrillation.
📌 Same "-LAY-shun" pattern (fibrillation family):
palpitation → pal-pi-TAY-shun  |  vaccination → vak-si-NAY-shun  |  irritation → ir-i-TAY-shun
WORD
ANTICOAGULANT
an-tee-koh-AG-yuh-lunt 6 syllables
British (RP): /ˌæntɪkəʊˈæɡjʊlənt/
⚠️ Primary stress on the 4th syllable "AG", secondary stress on "AN" (1st). This is a long word — break it down: "anti" + "coagulant". The "coagul-" part comes from "coagulate" (to clot). The "-ant" ending reduces to "-unt" (schwa). Common error: stressing the first syllable "AN" only, making it sound like "AN-ti-koh-ag-yuh-lunt" (flat, robotic).
Example 1
The patient was started on an anticoagulant following a diagnosis of deep vein thrombosis.
Example 2
Patients on anticoagulant therapy require regular blood tests to ensure their INR remains within the therapeutic range.
📌 Same "-AG-yuh-lunt / -IG-nuh-lunt" stress pattern (6-syllable words, 4th syllable stress):
anticonvulsant → an-tee-kun-VUL-sunt  |  antidepressant → an-tee-di-PRES-unt  |  antihistamine → an-tee-HIS-tuh-meen
WORD
BLOOD THINNER
blud THIN-ur 3 syllables
British (RP): /blʌd ˈθɪnər/
⚠️ Stress on "THIN" (the second element). "Blood" modifies "thinner", so the main stress falls on the noun head "thinner". The "th" is voiceless (as in "think") — tongue lightly between teeth, no vibration. Do NOT say "slood" or "blut". "Blood thinner" is the informal/lay term for anticoagulant — used when explaining to patients who may not be familiar with the clinical term.
Example 1
"Warfarin is a type of blood thinner — it helps stop your blood from clotting too easily."
Example 2
Always inform your dentist that you are taking a blood thinner before any dental procedure.
📌 Same two-word clinical descriptor pattern (stress on 2nd element):
pain killer → payn KIL-ur  |  fever reducer → fee-vur ri-DYOO-sur  |  sleep aid → sleep AYD
WORD
BRUTAL (INFORMAL)
📝 Extremely exhausting or severe — emotionally loaded term — used by patients to describe debilitating conditions
Example
The fatigue from her condition was Brutal — she described it as completely draining her will to function.
WORD
CONFUSED / DISCONNECTED / LIGHTHEADED
📝 General terms for impaired mental clarity — often used together to describe drug-induced cognitive effects
Example
She felt Confused and disconnected after starting the new medication — she described it as being lightheaded all day.
WORD
THE PAIN DIDN'T GET ANY BETTER — IN FACT QUITE THE OPPOSITE
📝 Patient expression for worsening symptoms despite treatment — signals treatment failure
Example
He reported that The pain had not got any better — in fact quite the opposite — it had doubled overnight.
WORD
BRUTAL (ADJECTIVE)
📝 Exhausting and overwhelming — used by patients to describe particularly debilitating experiences
Example
The weeks of sleeplessness had been Brutal — she felt she could not carry on without more support.
WORD
I FEEL UNDER THE WEATHER
📝 Informal expression for mild general unwellness — typically cold flu or fatigue
Example
She phoned in saying she had felt under the weather all week and was not sure if she should take her usual medication.
WORD
I FEEL RUN DOWN
📝 Feeling depleted and weakened — often from chronic illness poor sleep or inadequate nutrition
Example
She had been feeling run down for weeks — fatigue poor appetite and difficulty concentrating.
WORD
I WAS THROWING UP ALL DAY YESTERDAY
📝 Patient description of persistent vomiting — clinically signals dehydration risk and inability to take oral medications
Example
He reported throwing up all day yesterday — oral medication was not viable and IV access was considered.
WORD
PAID FOR IT (IDIOM)
📝 Experienced consequences for overexertion — the pain or fatigue returned after physical activity
Example
He had pushed through his physiotherapy session and really Paid for it — he was unable to walk the next day.
WORD
FINE TREMOR
📝 A subtle low-amplitude tremor — common with lithium or thyroid medication — usually visible in outstretched hands
Example
It is quite common to notice a Fine tremor when taking lithium — please hold out your hands so I can have a look.
WORD
NARROW THERAPEUTIC WINDOW
📝 The gap between an effective dose and a toxic dose is very small — requires close monitoring
Example
Lithium has a very Narrow therapeutic window — regular blood tests are essential to keep levels safe.
WORD
COARSE TREMOR
📝 A more obvious larger-amplitude tremor — indicates toxicity or severe neurological disturbance
Example
If the fine tremor changes to a Coarse tremor with obvious shaking please contact us immediately.
WORD
SLURRED SPEECH
📝 Dysarthria — unclear or indistinct speech — a sign of neurological impairment or drug toxicity
Example
If you notice Slurred speech or difficulty forming words seek medical attention urgently.
WORD
PROFOUNDLY DROWSY
📝 Severe drowsiness beyond normal tiredness — a red flag for drug toxicity or neurological emergency
Example
If you feel Profoundly drowsy or cannot stay awake please call 111 immediately.
WORD
STRABISMUS / SQUINT
📝 A misalignment of the eyes — one or both eyes turn inward outward or upward — also called strabismus or crossed eyes
Example
She was referred to ophthalmology for assessment of her Strabismus — it had been gradually worsening.
WORD
EPILEPTIC SEIZURE
📝 An episode of abnormal electrical activity in the brain — can cause convulsions loss of consciousness or absence
Example
She was warned that stroboscopic lights at club venues can trigger Epileptic seizures.
WORD
COMPULSIVE ACTION
📝 A behaviour performed automatically without deliberate thinking — associated with OCD or habitual use
Example
A Compulsive action is something you do automatically without thinking — the patient was unaware he was doing it.
WORD
CEREBRAL PALSY
📝 A non-progressive neurological condition affecting movement and posture — caused by brain injury before or around birth
Example
Patients with Cerebral palsy often require mobility support devices and regular physiotherapy.
WORD
BIPOLAR DISORDER
📝 A mood disorder with alternating episodes of mania and depression
Example
He had been diagnosed with Bipolar disorder and was maintained on lithium for mood stabilisation.
WORD
SCHIZOPHRENIA
📝 A severe mental illness characterised by hallucinations delusions and disorganised thinking
Example
She had a history of Schizophrenia and was taking clozapine — regular monitoring was required.
WORD
ADHD — ATTENTION DEFICIT HYPERACTIVITY DISORDER
📝 A neurodevelopmental disorder characterised by inattention hyperactivity and impulsivity
Example
ADHD stands for Attention Deficit Hyperactivity Disorder — it is typically managed with stimulant medication.
WORD
ABDOMINAL PAIN
📝 Pain in the abdomen — location character and radiation should all be assessed
Example
He presented with severe Abdominal pain in the right iliac fossa — appendicitis was the working diagnosis.
WORD
NUMBNESS
📝 Loss of sensation in a body part — can indicate nerve compression poor circulation or neuropathy
Example
He reported Numbness in his fingers that was worse in the mornings — consistent with carpal tunnel syndrome.
WORD
FAINTING / SYNCOPE
📝 A temporary loss of consciousness due to reduced blood flow to the brain — may be cardiac vasovagal or orthostatic
Example
She fainted in the shower — a tilt-table test was arranged to investigate vasovagal syncope.
WORD
HAIR LOSS / ALOPECIA
📝 Loss of hair from the scalp or body — can be a side effect of chemotherapy or certain medications
Example
She reported significant Hair loss since starting the new medication — the pharmacist documented this as an adverse event.
WORD
BRUISING EASILY
📝 A tendency to develop bruises with minimal trauma — may indicate low platelets or anticoagulant therapy
Example
She noticed she was Bruising easily — a full blood count and coagulation screen were requested.
WORD
EXCESSIVE SWEATING
📝 Sweating beyond normal thermoregulation — may be a drug side effect infection or hormonal cause
Example
She developed Excessive sweating within days of starting the new antidepressant.
WORD
CHANGE IN BOWEL HABITS
📝 Alteration in the frequency consistency or urgency of bowel motions — a red flag symptom requiring investigation
Example
He reported a Change in bowel habits over six weeks — alternating diarrhoea and constipation.
WORD
LOSS OF APPETITE
📝 Reduced desire to eat — clinically significant if unexplained or persistent — may indicate malignancy depression or drug effect
Example
She described a significant Loss of appetite — she had lost four kilograms without trying over the past month.
WORD
JOINT SWELLING
📝 Visible enlargement of a joint due to fluid or inflammation — associated with gout arthritis or infection
Example
The right knee showed marked Joint swelling with warmth and tenderness — joint aspiration was performed.
WORD
CHEST TIGHTNESS
📝 A squeezing or pressure sensation in the chest — may indicate cardiac respiratory or musculoskeletal cause
Example
He described Chest tightness on exertion — a stress ECG was arranged to rule out cardiac ischaemia.
WORD
MOOD SWINGS
📝 Rapid or unpredictable changes in emotional state — may be a drug side effect or indicate a mood disorder
Example
She reported significant Mood swings since starting the new contraceptive — an alternative was discussed.
WORD
BLURRED VISION
📝 Loss of visual clarity — may indicate ocular side effects of medication or systemic disease
Example
She reported Blurred vision that had started after the new medication was commenced — an ophthalmology referral was arranged.
WORD
FREQUENT URINATION
📝 Increased frequency of passing urine — may indicate urinary tract infection diabetes or medication effect
Example
He reported Frequent urination especially at night — a urine dipstick and blood glucose were checked.
WORD
HOT FLASHES
📝 Sudden sensations of intense body heat — common in menopause or as a side effect of some medications
Example
She described Hot flashes that woke her several times a night — HRT was discussed as an option.
WORD
THE PATIENT'S EGFR IS REDUCED WHICH MAY AFFECT THIS MEDICATION
📝 Standard pharmacist communication about renal function and drug dosing
Example
The patient's eGFR is reduced at 28 mL/min which may affect the safety of this medication at the current dose.
WORD
I AM WRITING TO EXPRESS MY PROFESSIONAL CONCERN REGARDING
📝 Formal opening for a pharmacist referral letter — clinical concern about a patient
Example
I am writing to express my professional concern regarding Mr Henderson's medication adherence and his current clinical status.
WORD
HIS BLOOD PRESSURE WAS PERSISTENTLY ELEVATED
📝 A clinical observation documented in a referral letter — signals hypertension not at target
Example
Upon taking His blood pressure I found it was persistently elevated at 165/95 mmHg on two consecutive readings.
WORD
UPON REVIEWING HIS DISPENSING HISTORY
📝 A pharmacist audit phrase — used when reviewing medication collection records to identify non-adherence
Example
Upon reviewing his dispensing history it appears that he has been collecting his antihypertensive medication inconsistently.
WORD
A FORMAL MEDICATION REVIEW MAY BE NECESSARY
📝 A professional recommendation that the prescriber reassess the patient's overall drug therapy
Example
Given the complexity of his current regimen A formal medication review may be necessary.
WORD
I WOULD RECOMMEND A REVIEW OF HIS MEDICATION REGIMEN TO ENSURE SAFE USE AND OPTIMISE HIS TREATMENT
📝 Standard OET Writing closing recommendation phrase — specific and action-oriented
Example
I would recommend a review of his medication regimen to ensure safe use and optimise his treatment given his declining renal function.
WORD
PLEASE DO NOT HESITATE TO CONTACT ME IF FURTHER INFORMATION IS REQUIRED
📝 Standard professional closing for a referral or communication letter
Example
Please do not hesitate to contact me if further information is required — I am available on the number below.
WORD
HER SYMPTOMS ARE SUGGESTIVE OF FLUID RETENTION AND POSSIBLE DETERIORATION IN RENAL FUNCTION
📝 Clinical interpretation phrase — symptoms point toward a diagnosis without confirming it
Example
Her ankle oedema and weight gain are suggestive of fluid retention and possible deterioration in renal function.
WORD
THE PATIENT REPORTED INCREASING USE OF
📝 Documents self-reported escalation of a substance or medication
Example
The patient reported increasing use of ibuprofen over the past month — up to six tablets daily.
WORD
I WOULD RECOMMEND AN URGENT REVIEW
📝 A stronger recommendation phrase — appropriate when the clinical situation is time-sensitive
Example
I would recommend an urgent review to assess for possible methotrexate toxicity given his reported symptoms.
WORD
I OBSERVED THAT HIS BLOOD PRESSURE WAS PERSISTENTLY ELEVATED AT 165 OVER 95
📝 Specific documented clinical observation — includes the numeric reading for precision
Example
I observed that his blood pressure was persistently elevated at 165 over 95 mmHg on repeat measurement.
WORD
IT APPEARS THAT HE HAS BEEN IRREGULAR IN COLLECTING HIS PRIMARY ANTIHYPERTENSIVE MEDICATION
📝 A non-accusatory documentation phrase for non-adherence identified via dispensing records
Example
It appears that he has been irregular in collecting his primary antihypertensive medication — there have been three gaps of over two weeks in the past six months.
WORD
HE ADMITTED TO INADVERTENTLY SKIPPING DOSES DUE TO FORGETFULNESS
📝 Documented patient self-report of unintentional non-adherence
Example
He admitted to inadvertently skipping doses due to forgetfulness — a once-daily formulation or dosette box was discussed.
WORD
EXPRESSED CONCERNS REGARDING THE ADVERSE EFFECTS SPECIFICALLY A PERSISTENT DRY COUGH
📝 Documents the patient's reported side effect in formal language
Example
She Expressed concerns regarding the adverse effects — specifically a persistent dry cough which she believed started after the recent dosage titration.
WORD
GIVEN HIS COMORBIDITIES AND THE INCREASED RISK OF CARDIOVASCULAR EVENTS
📝 Clinical reasoning phrase — connects the patient's background conditions to the clinical decision
Example
Given his comorbidities and the increased risk of cardiovascular events I am concerned that his current regimen may no longer be optimal.
WORD
COULD YOU PLEASE EVALUATE WHETHER A SWITCH TO AN ARB MIGHT BE MORE APPROPRIATE
📝 A pharmacist's recommendation framed as a request to the prescriber — collaborative tone
Example
Could you please evaluate whether a switch to an ARB might be more appropriate to mitigate the side effects while maintaining efficacy?
WORD
SHOULD HER EGFR FALL BELOW 30 ML/MIN A COMPLETE CESSATION OF METFORMIN MAY BE NECESSARY
📝 Conditional clinical statement — documents a threshold for medication change
Example
Should her eGFR fall below 30 mL/min/1.73m² a complete cessation of Metformin may be necessary to prevent lactic acidosis.
WORD
PATIENT MUST USE HIGH-FACTOR SUNSCREEN EVEN ON CLOUDY DAYS
📝 Amiodarone counselling point — causes photosensitivity — essential safety advice
Example
You must use high-factor sunscreen every day even on cloudy days — amiodarone makes your skin very sensitive to UV light.
WORD
DEVELOPMENT OF A BLUE-GREY SKIN DISCOLOURATION
📝 A known and permanent side effect of long-term amiodarone — must be disclosed upfront
Example
I need to let you know that long-term amiodarone can cause a blue-grey skin discolouration — this is not dangerous but is permanent.
WORD
YOU MAY NOTICE HALOS AROUND LIGHTS AT NIGHT
📝 An ocular side effect of amiodarone — common and usually mild
Example
You may notice halos around lights at night — this is a recognised effect of amiodarone and is usually not a reason to stop.
WORD
REPORT ANY YELLOWING OF THE SKIN OR EYES
📝 Instruction to report signs of jaundice — indicates hepatotoxicity — a serious amiodarone side effect
Example
Report any yellowing of the skin or eyes immediately — this could indicate that the drug is affecting your liver.
WORD
STRICTLY AVOID GRAPEFRUIT JUICE AS IT INCREASES DRUG CONCENTRATION
📝 Standard counselling for drugs metabolised by CYP3A4 — grapefruit is a CYP3A4 inhibitor
Example
Strictly avoid grapefruit juice as it increases drug concentration and can lead to toxic effects.
WORD
REQUIRES BASELINE AND PERIODIC BLOOD TESTS
📝 Monitoring requirement phrase — applies to many high-risk medications
Example
This medication Requires baseline and periodic blood tests — we will check your thyroid liver and lung function regularly.
WORD
STAYING UPRIGHT IS VITAL TO PREVENT PAINFUL ULCERS
📝 Alendronate counselling — must remain upright for 30 minutes after taking to prevent oesophageal erosion
Example
Staying upright after taking this tablet is vital to prevent painful ulcers forming in your oesophagus.
WORD
WRITING THE EXACT FREQUENCY IS VITAL
📝 Documentation principle — the exact dosing frequency must be clearly written in any clinical letter or note
Example
When documenting the prescription Writing the exact frequency is vital — once daily versus twice daily can have significant clinical implications.
WORD
THE ANTI-INFLAMMATORY MEDICATION YOU ARE TAKING MAY REDUCE BLOOD FLOW TO YOUR KIDNEYS
📝 Patient-friendly explanation of NSAID nephrotoxicity
Example
The anti-inflammatory medication you are taking may reduce blood flow to your kidneys — this is why we recommend using it only when necessary.
WORD
WHEN THIS MEDICINE IS TAKEN TOGETHER WITH YOUR BLOOD PRESSURE MEDICATION AND DIURETICS IT MAY FURTHER IMPAIR KIDNEY FUNCTION
📝 Triple whammy effect counselling — NSAIDs plus ACE inhibitor plus diuretic
Example
When this medicine is taken together with your blood pressure medication and diuretics it may further impair kidney function — this combination should generally be avoided.
WORD
REDUCED KIDNEY FUNCTION MAY ALSO AFFECT HOW YOUR DIABETES MEDICATION IS PROCESSED IN YOUR BODY
📝 Explains the clinical impact of renal impairment on metformin clearance
Example
Reduced kidney function may also affect how your diabetes medication is processed in your body — specifically the risk of metformin accumulation increases.
WORD
IF YOU BECOME UNWELL ESPECIALLY WITH VOMITING DIARRHOEA OR REDUCED FLUID INTAKE YOU SHOULD TEMPORARILY STOP CERTAIN MEDICATIONS AND SEEK MEDICAL ADVICE
📝 Sick day rules counselling — important for patients on metformin ACE inhibitors diuretics or NSAIDs
Example
If you become unwell — especially with vomiting diarrhoea or reduced fluid intake — you should temporarily stop certain medications and seek medical advice.
WORD
PLEASE CONTINUE TAKING YOUR MEDICATION AS ADVISED
📝 Standard adherence instruction — closing line in counselling
Example
Please continue taking your medication as advised — do not stop without first speaking to your pharmacist or doctor.
WORD
PLEASE ENSURE THAT THE MEDICATION IS ADMINISTERED AS PRESCRIBED
📝 Formal instruction to a carer or care home — documents the expected administration practice
Example
Please ensure that the medication is administered as prescribed — any deviation should be reported to the GP.
WORD
I WOULD RECOMMEND A REVIEW OF THE PATIENT'S MEDICATION REGIMEN
📝 A professional recommendation to a prescriber — invites clinical collaboration
Example
I would recommend a review of the patient's medication regimen in light of his recent blood test results.
WORD
IT'S QUITE COMMON TO NOTICE A FINE TREMOR
📝 Reassurance phrase — normalises a benign drug side effect
Example
It is quite common to notice a fine tremor when taking this medication — please show me your hands so I can assess it.
WORD
LITHIUM HAS A VERY NARROW THERAPEUTIC WINDOW
📝 Core lithium counselling fact — explains why monitoring is essential
Example
Lithium has a very narrow therapeutic window — the difference between a helpful dose and a toxic one is very small.
WORD
IF THAT CHANGES TO A COARSE TREMOR WHERE THE SHAKING BECOMES MUCH MORE OBVIOUS OR JERKY
📝 Lithium toxicity warning — escalation sign that requires urgent review
Example
If the fine tremor changes to a coarse tremor where the shaking becomes much more obvious or jerky please contact us immediately.
WORD
IF YOU START TO HAVE SLURRED SPEECH FEEL PROFOUNDLY DROWSY OR EXPERIENCE SEVERE VOMITING
📝 Lithium toxicity red flag symptoms — requires immediate medical attention
Example
If you start to have slurred speech feel profoundly drowsy or experience severe vomiting seek emergency medical care immediately.
WORD
DIAGNOSIS WAS CONFIRMED BY A BONE DENSITY SCAN
📝 Standard clinical documentation phrase — names the investigation that confirmed the diagnosis
Example
The Diagnosis of osteoporosis was confirmed by a bone density scan showing a T-score of minus 2.8.
WORD
NOT SUPPOSED TO LIE DOWN FOR AT LEAST 30 MINUTES AFTER ADMINISTRATION
📝 Alendronate dosing instruction — prevents oesophageal irritation
Example
You are Not supposed to lie down for at least 30 minutes after taking this tablet — it must be swallowed with a full glass of water while sitting upright.
WORD
NEGLIGIBLE
📝 So small as to be unimportant — does not require clinical attention
Example
The interaction between these two drugs is Negligible at standard doses and does not require any action.
WORD
MINIMAL / SLIGHT / MINOR
📝 A very small amount of change or pain — present but barely noticeable
Example
She reported Minimal discomfort at the injection site — no treatment was required.
WORD
MILD
📝 Present and noticeable but not significantly limiting daily activities
Example
He had Mild symptoms that did not prevent him from working — watchful waiting was recommended.
WORD
MODERATE
📝 Between mild and severe — requires management but not emergency intervention
Example
Her condition was classified as Moderate — pharmacological treatment was initiated.
WORD
MODERATELY SEVERE
📝 Beyond moderate — approaching severe — may require dose change or escalation of treatment
Example
The pain was described as Moderately severe — the current analgesia was no longer sufficient.
WORD
CRITICAL / ACUTE / EXTREME
📝 Life-threatening or immediately dangerous — requires emergency management
Example
He was in a Critical condition on arrival — he was taken directly to the resuscitation bay.
WORD
PROFOUND
📝 A complete or near-complete loss of a specific function — often used with hearing or cognition
Example
She had a Profound hearing loss in her right ear following the infection — cochlear implant was discussed.
WORD
DID YOU TRY ANY OTHER SORT OF TREATMENT APART FROM THE MEDICATIONS
📝 Open question about non-pharmacological self-management
Example
Did you try any other sort of treatment apart from the medications — such as heat packs rest or physiotherapy?
WORD
THE PHARMACIST RESPONDS WITH HEAVY MEDICAL JARGON AND THE PATIENT JUST ZONES OUT
📝 A teaching point — using complex language loses patient engagement
Example
If The pharmacist responds with heavy medical jargon the patient just zones out — always aim for plain language.
WORD
IT HAS THE EXACT SAME ACTIVE INGREDIENT AS YOUR USUAL BRAND — IT IS JUST MADE BY A DIFFERENT COMPANY AND COSTS LESS
📝 Generic substitution explanation — reassures patient about equivalence
Example
It has the exact same active ingredient as your usual brand — it is just made by a different company and costs less. The effect will be identical.
WORD
IF YOU HAVE BEEN UNDER THE WEATHER THIS MEDICATION WILL HELP YOU RECOVER
📝 Empathetic counselling opener using patient language
Example
If you have been under the weather lately this medication will help your body recover more quickly.
WORD
ACKNOWLEDGING THE PATIENT'S ANXIETY WILL INSTANTLY BUILD RAPPORT
📝 A communication principle — validated empathy improves the consultation quality
Example
Acknowledging the patient's anxiety will instantly build rapport — start by saying 'I can see this has been worrying for you.'
WORD
SOUGHT AN OVER-THE-COUNTER REMEDY FOR A COUGH
📝 Documents the patient's self-referral reason in a clinical letter
Example
She Sought an over-the-counter remedy for a cough — during the consultation I identified a potential drug interaction.
WORD
DEVELOPED A DRY TICKLY COUGH THAT WORSENS AT NIGHT AND CAUSES INSOMNIA
📝 Detailed symptom description — includes character timing and functional impact
Example

WORD
ST JOHN'S WORT
📝 Herbal supplement with antidepressant and enzyme-inducing properties — interacts with many prescription drugs
Example

WORD
I CAN SEE YOU ARE STRUGGLING WITH YOUR MOOD
📝 Empathy opener — validates emotional state without diagnosing — invites further discussion
Example

WORD
ANAPHYLAXIS CAN HAPPEN UNEXPECTEDLY SO BEING PREPARED IS LIFE-SAVING
📝 Core anaphylaxis counselling statement — emphasises unpredictability and importance of carrying auto-injector
Example
anaphylaxis can happen unexpectedly so being prepared is life-saving — always carry your adrenaline auto-injector.
WORD
MAGNETISM FROM THE MACHINE
📝 Patient-friendly explanation of the magnetic field in an MRI scanner — relevant for patients with metal implants
Example
Patients with certain implants cannot have an MRI because of the magnetism from the machine.
WORD
I HAVE GONE THROUGH YOUR NOTES
📝 Clinical consultation opener — signals the clinician has reviewed the records and is prepared
Example
I have gone through your notes and I can see you have been experiencing these symptoms for some time.
WORD
THE LAST SORT OF MONTH IT HAS BEEN MORE AND MORE
📝 Patient language for escalating symptoms over approximately one month — hedge word sort of signals approximation
Example
the last sort of month it has been more and more — I can barely get through a meal without the pain starting.
WORD
I HAVE BEEN TRYING BITS AND PIECES
📝 Patient has self-managed with various remedies without a systematic approach — signals prior self-medication
Example
I have been trying bits and pieces — antacids cutting out coffee — but nothing works long-term.
WORD
PARTICULARLY WHEN YOU HAVE FOOD I SUPPOSE
📝 Clinician checking a possible trigger — hedged question using I suppose to soften the assumption
Example

WORD
I CAN STILL GET THE SYMPTOMS
📝 Patient reports symptoms persist despite treatment or avoidance — signals incomplete resolution
Example
Even when I follow all the dietary advice I can still get the symptoms — usually late at night.
WORD
I HAVE SWALLOWED A KNIFE AND IT IS STABBING INSIDE MY TUMMY
📝 Patient metaphor for sharp stabbing epigastric pain — decode as: sharp pain
Example
It feels like I have swallowed a knife and it is stabbing inside my tummy — that is the only way I can describe it.
WORD
IT IS A TUBE PASSED THROUGH YOUR THROAT WITH A CAMERA AT THE END
📝 Plain English explanation of an endoscopy — essential OET patient-explanation language
Example

WORD
JUST PAIN RELATED TO LIFTING
📝 Musculoskeletal pain with a clear mechanical trigger — lifting as causative activity
Example
He described it as just pain related to lifting — it only comes on when he carries heavy objects.
WORD
WE WILL REASSESS
📝 Clinical closing phrase — signals the decision is not final and the patient will be reviewed
Example

WORD
REBOUND CONGESTION
📝 Worsening of nasal congestion after overusing or stopping a nasal decongestant
Example
Using a decongestant for more than five days can cause rebound congestion — the nose becomes more blocked than before.
WORD
INSOMNIA
📝 Persistent difficulty falling or staying asleep — can be onset (can't get to sleep) or maintenance (waking in the night)
Example
He went to bed exhausted every night but suffered from insomnia — much more than he had ever experienced before.
WORD
NUTRITIONIST
📝 A professional who advises on diet and nutrition — not the same as a dietitian in all contexts
Example

WORD
FIBRE (DIETARY)
📝 Indigestible plant material that supports bowel health — found in vegetables wholegrains and legumes
Example

WORD
DAIRY PRODUCTS
📝 Foods made from animal milk — butter cheese yoghurt milk — can worsen IBS in some patients
Example
