WORD
MUSCULAR CONTRACTIONS
📝 The rhythmic squeezing movements of the gut wall that move food through the digestive tract
Example

WORD
ANTIDEPRESSANTS
📝 Medications that target nerve signals — in IBS context used for their effect on the gut-brain axis not for depression per se
Example
He had been given antidepressants and was giving them a go — though he was not entirely sure why.
WORD
OSTEOPATH
📝 A healthcare practitioner who treats musculoskeletal problems through manipulation
Example
She visited an osteopath hoping for relief from her neck stiffness but the treatment made things worse.
WORD
CHIROPRACTIC TREATMENT
📝 Manual therapy focused on the spine and musculoskeletal system — provided by a chiropractor
Example
She tried chiropractic treatment for six months at great expense but it provided no lasting benefit.
WORD
SUPPORT GROUP
📝 A group of people with a shared condition who meet to provide mutual understanding and information
Example
Joining a support group for spasmodic torticollis patients gave her a sense of community.
WORD
ORTHOPAEDIC CHAIR
📝 A chair designed to support correct posture and reduce musculoskeletal strain
Example
She purchased an orthopaedic chair at home to provide postural support and reduce her pain levels.
WORD
BOTOX INJECTION (THERAPEUTIC)
📝 Botulinum toxin injected into muscles to treat dystonia spasm or chronic migraine — not cosmetic in this context
Example
She read about Botox injections being used at a university hospital to treat spasmodic torticollis.
WORD
UP THE DOSAGE
📝 To increase the dose — informal phrasing — clinical equivalent: uptitrate or increase the dose
Example

WORD
MORPHINE PUMP (PCA)
📝 A patient-controlled analgesia device allowing self-administration of morphine for pain relief
Example

WORD
OCCASIONAL BOUTS OF NAUSEA
📝 Intermittent episodes of nausea — not constant but recurring
Example
She experienced occasional bouts of nausea that came on without warning after meals.
WORD
CERVICAL CANCER
📝 Cancer of the cervix — the lower part of the uterus
Example
She was eventually diagnosed with cervical cancer which fortunately had not advanced too far.
WORD
IRON DEFICIENCY ANAEMIA
📝 Low red blood cell count due to insufficient iron — causes fatigue pallor and breathlessness
Example

WORD
IRON OVERLOAD
📝 Excess iron accumulation in the body — as in haemochromatosis
Example
The blood test results were unexpected — she had iron overload rather than iron deficiency.
WORD
STIFFNESS IN JOINTS
📝 Reduced ease of movement in joints — particularly noticeable in the morning or after rest
Example
She had started to notice stiffness in her joints — particularly in her fingers and knees.
WORD
SHORT OF BREATH
📝 Difficulty breathing or breathlessness — even at rest or during mild activity
Example
She occasionally felt short of breath even during light activities like walking the dog.
WORD
CAUGHT COVID-19
📝 Became infected with the COVID-19 virus
Example
It all started when he caught COVID-19 — he had not been vaccinated at the time.
WORD
FLU-LIKE SYMPTOMS

Example

WORD
SAPPED MY ENERGY
📝 Gradually drained or depleted energy levels over time — more gradual than wipe out
Example

WORD
GETTING OUT OF BREATH JUST DOING ORDINARY THINGS
📝 Breathlessness with minimal exertion — a significant functional limitation

WORD
BRAIN WOULD GO KIND OF FUZZY
📝 Informal description of cognitive impairment — brain fog — difficulty thinking clearly
Example

WORD
WORDS WOULD BE ON THE TIP OF MY TONGUE
📝 Word-finding difficulty — almost remembering a word but being unable to retrieve it — a specific cognitive symptom
Example
words would be on the tip of my tongue but just would not come out — it was very frustrating.
WORD

📝 A recording of the electrical activity of the heart — used to detect arrhythmias and cardiac abnormalities
Example
He underwent several cardiac tests including an ECG and was placed on a heart monitor for five days.
WORD
BETA BLOCKERS
📝 Medications that slow the heart rate and reduce blood pressure — used for arrhythmias angina and hypertension
Example

WORD
VITAMIN D LEVELS WERE LOW
📝 Blood test finding — vitamin D deficiency — common in people with limited sun exposure
Example
Blood tests also revealed that his vitamin D levels were low and supplements were recommended.
WORD
DULL ACHE
📝 A persistent low-level pain — not sharp or stabbing — often described in joints or muscles
Example
The joint pain was best described as a dull ache rather than a sharp or stabbing sensation.
WORD
NUMBNESS IN FINGERS

Example

WORD
STOMACH ULCER

HERNIA OPERATION

WORD
OCCUPATIONAL INJURY
📝 An injury or health condition caused or worsened by work activities or environment
Example

WORD
RUNNING OUT OF ENERGY

WORD

RUN IN FAMILIES

WORD

IN MY CALF

WORD

WORD
SALT INTAKE

📝 A gradual worsening of cognitive function consciousness or behaviour over time

📝 Any change from baseline mental functioning — includes confusion agitation or reduced consciousness
Example

O2 SATS ARE DROPPING
📝 Oxygen saturation falling — a critical clinical sign requiring immediate action

WORD

SHE IS SEIZING
📝 Active seizure in progress — requires immediate medical response
Example
she is seizing — give lorazepam 2mg IV and call for senior support.
WORD
SHE IS ASPIRATING
📝 Inhaling foreign material (food secretions or vomit) into the airway — risk of aspiration pneumonia
Example

WORD
SHE IS ASHEN
📝 Very pale or grey in colour — a sign of poor perfusion or shock
Example

WORD

📝 Unable to provide effective breaths through a bag mask or tube — requires alternative airway

WORD

WORD

WORD

📝 To pass a breathing tube into the trachea immediately — used in emergency airway management

WORD

📝 The process of delivering an electrical shock to restore normal heart rhythm — Charging = loading the device — Clear = warning others to stand back
Example

WORD

📝 A diagnosis reached by systematically ruling out all other possible causes

CULTURES ARE PENDING

WORD

WORD
THERAPEUTIC TRIAL
📝 A treatment given to test a diagnostic hypothesis — if the patient improves the diagnosis is supported
Example
A therapeutic trial of high-dose steroids was initiated — improvement would support the working diagnosis.
WORD
TREATMENT DISCONTINUED DUE TO ADVERSE REACTION
📝 The medication was stopped because of a harmful or unexpected side effect
Example
treatment was discontinued due to an adverse reaction — the patient developed anaphylaxis within minutes.
WORD
CEREBRAL VASCULITIS
📝 Inflammation of blood vessels in the brain — rare — presents with headache seizures and neurological deficits
Example
cerebral vasculitis was confirmed on biopsy and treatment with high-dose steroids was commenced.
WORD
NEUROCYSTICERCOSIS
📝 A parasitic infection of the brain caused by tapeworm larvae — common in areas where pigs are raised
Example
neurocysticercosis was identified on MRI — the patient had recently travelled to an endemic region.
WORD
ANAPHYLACTIC SHOCK
📝 A severe life-threatening allergic reaction — causes airway swelling hypotension and circulatory collapse
Example
She went into anaphylactic shock within minutes of the first dose — adrenaline was given immediately.
WORD
MULTIPLE SCLEROSIS (MS)
📝 An autoimmune condition attacking the myelin sheath of nerves — causes relapsing or progressive neurological symptoms
Example
Oligoclonal bands in the CSF and white matter lesions on MRI are consistent with multiple sclerosis.
WORD
DNR — DO NOT RESUSCITATE
📝 A legal clinical document stating that cardiopulmonary resuscitation should not be attempted if the patient arrests
Example
The DNR was signed by the patient after a detailed discussion with the palliative care team.
WORD
ALBENDAZOLE
📝 An antiparasitic drug used to treat tapeworm infections including neurocysticercosis
Example
albendazole was prescribed for neurocysticercosis along with steroids to reduce the inflammatory response.
WORD
EPINEPHRINE (ADRENALINE)
📝 A catecholamine drug used in anaphylaxis cardiac arrest and severe asthma — causes bronchodilation and vasoconstriction
Example
adrenaline (epinephrine) 0.5mg was administered intramuscularly for the anaphylactic reaction.
WORD
LORAZEPAM (ATIVAN)
📝 A benzodiazepine used to terminate seizures and reduce anxiety — given IV or IM in emergencies
Example
lorazepam was given intravenously to terminate the seizure activity.
WORD
GADOLINIUM
📝 A contrast agent injected intravenously for MRI — enhances visibility of lesions and abnormal tissue
Example
gadolinium was injected into a vein — it distributes through the brain and acts as contrast material for the MRI.
WORD
PRACTISE (VERB) / PRACTICE (NOUN)
📝 British spelling distinction — practise is always the verb practice is always the noun (US English uses practice for both)
Example
She needs to practise her injection technique daily before the assessment. His pharmacy practice has grown significantly since 2020.
WORD
ALLERGEN
📝 A substance that triggers an allergic reaction — distinct from allergy (the reaction itself) — allergen is the trigger substance — stress on AL-uh-jen
Example
Dust mites pollen mold and pet dander are the most common allergens associated with asthma.
WORD
DUST MITES
📝 Microscopic insects living in household dust — one of the most common asthma allergens — stress on DUST MYTS
Example
House dust mites are found in bedding carpets and soft furnishings — regular washing in hot water helps reduce exposure.
WORD
POLLEN
📝 Fine powder released by plants — a common seasonal allergen triggering asthma and hay fever — stress on POL-un
Example
Her asthma tends to worsen in spring when the pollen count is high.
WORD
MOLD (MOULD)
📝 A type of fungus growing in damp conditions — a common indoor allergen for asthma — British spelling is mould American is mold
Example
mold in bathrooms and basements can significantly worsen asthma symptoms in susceptible patients.
WORD
WHEEZING
📝 A high-pitched whistling breathing sound caused by narrowed airways — a hallmark symptom of asthma — stress on WHEEZ-ing
Example
She presented with audible wheezing on expiration and significant chest tightness — her peak flow was reduced.
WORD
SALBUTAMOL
📝 A short-acting beta-2 agonist bronchodilator — the most common reliever inhaler — brand name Ventolin — stress on sal-BYOO-tuh-mol
Example
salbutamol should be used for immediate relief of acute asthma symptoms — not as a regular daily treatment.
WORD
PREVENTER INHALER
📝 An inhaled corticosteroid used daily to reduce airway inflammation and prevent asthma symptoms — distinct from reliever inhaler
Example
She was reminded to use her preventer inhaler every morning regardless of whether she had symptoms.
WORD
RELIEVER INHALER
📝 A bronchodilator inhaler used when asthma symptoms occur for immediate relief — salbutamol is the most common example
Example
She carries her reliever inhaler at all times in case of an acute asthma episode.
WORD
INHALED CORTICOSTEROIDS (ICS)
📝 Steroid medications delivered directly to the airways by inhaler — the mainstay of asthma preventer therapy — examples include fluticasone and budesonide
Example
inhaled corticosteroids are the most effective preventer treatment for persistent asthma and should be taken daily.
WORD
MUSCLE TIGHTENING
📝 The contraction of smooth muscle surrounding the airways in asthma — causes the airway to narrow and makes breathing difficult
Example
Asthma causes muscle tightening around the airways which reduces the diameter through which air can pass.
WORD
NON-PHARMACEUTICAL MANAGEMENT
📝 Treatment approaches that do not involve medication — for asthma includes trigger avoidance environmental control and breathing exercises
Example
non-pharmaceutical management of asthma is an important complement to medication — identifying and avoiding triggers can significantly reduce the frequency of attacks.
WORD
MEDICAL HEDGING
📝 The use of qualifying language such as may include can cause or is often associated with to express clinical uncertainty appropriately — essential in OET writing and speaking
Example
medical hedging such as may include and is often triggered by is essential when describing symptoms — it reflects clinical accuracy and avoids overstating certainty.
WORD
REBOUND CONGESTION (RHINITIS MEDICAMENTOSA)
📝 Worsening nasal congestion that occurs after stopping or overusing a nasal decongestant — caused by receptor desensitisation
Example
She had been using the decongestant for three weeks — the pharmacist explained that her ongoing congestion was likely rebound congestion.
WORD
PROLONGED USE
📝 Use of a medication for longer than the recommended duration — often associated with increased risk of side effects or tolerance
Example
prolonged use of nasal decongestants is not recommended — the maximum safe duration is three to five days.
WORD
NASAL PASSAGE / NASAL MUCOSA
📝 The internal cavity of the nose lined with mucous membrane — the surface affected by decongestants and saline spray
Example
Prolonged decongestant use can cause irritation to the mucous lining of the nasal passage.
WORD
SAFETY NETTING
📝 The clinical communication strategy of telling a patient what to do if symptoms continue worsen or change — always included in OET closing
Example
safety netting is the final step — always tell the patient to come back if the problem persists or if they have any concerns.
WORD
OCCUPATIONAL HEALTH NURSE
📝 A registered nurse who advises employers on workplace health and wellbeing — the role described in the Maria Chen reading passage
Example
Maria Chen worked as an occupational health nurse advising companies on how to support staff mental health and prevent burnout.
WORD
STIGMA (MENTAL HEALTH)
📝 The negative attitudes and social shame attached to mental health conditions that prevent people from seeking help
Example
The stigma surrounding mental health in the workplace is slowly fading as employers increasingly offer counselling and support.
WORD
SCOPE OF PRACTICE
📝 The defined range of procedures and actions that a healthcare professional is legally and professionally authorised to perform
Example
If the patient's symptoms are outside our scope of practice we would refer them to the appropriate healthcare professional.
WORD
PREVENTER INHALER / CONTROLLER INHALER
📝 Daily inhaled corticosteroid used to prevent airway inflammation — distinct from reliever inhalers used for acute symptoms
Example
She had been taking her preventer inhaler daily for six months and had not needed her salbutamol once.
WORD
COPD — CHRONIC OBSTRUCTIVE PULMONARY DISEASE
📝 A chronic progressive lung condition — always introduce abbreviation with full form on first use — OET best practice
Example
Patients with COPD, which is a chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, often require both pharmacological and non-pharmacological management.
WORD
SECOND-HAND SMOKE
📝 Smoke inhaled by non-smokers from a nearby smoker — also called passive smoking — an important counselling topic
Example
second-hand smoke affects family members who live with smokers — children are particularly vulnerable to its effects.
WORD
NICOTINE DEPENDENCE / NICOTINE ADDICTION
📝 Both acceptable in OET — dependence is slightly more clinical — key point: cessation is very difficult without structured support
Example
nicotine dependence makes cessation very difficult — pharmacological support such as nicotine replacement therapy is strongly recommended.
WORD
CESSATION
📝 The act of stopping a behaviour — clinical term for quitting smoking — more formal than quitting
Example
cessation is strongly recommended for all smokers — a range of pharmacological and behavioural support options are available through community pharmacy.
WORD
CARDIOVASCULAR DISEASE
📝 Disease affecting the heart and blood vessels — smoking raises blood pressure and leads to cardiovascular disease and stroke
Example
Smoking increases blood pressure which over time leads to cardiovascular disease and significantly raises the risk of stroke.
WORD
IMPAIR (COGNITIVE FUNCTION)
📝 To weaken or damage a specific function — impair + noun — impair cognitive function is a high-register clinical phrase
Example
Second-hand smoke can impair cognitive function in children — particularly affecting attention and language development.
WORD
DETRIMENTAL
📝 Causing harm or damage — a high-register clinical adjective that lifts writing from B2 to C1 — stress on det-ri-MEN-tul
Example
Smoking has detrimental effects on both the respiratory and cardiovascular systems.
WORD
NASAL MUCUS / NASAL PASSAGE
📝 The lining and internal channel of the nose — mucus = the fluid produced — both terms used in decongestant counselling
Example
Saline spray helps the nasal mucus to recover and keeps the nasal passage moist and clear.
WORD
IF THE PROBLEM PERSISTS PLEASE COME BACK
📝 OET essential closing phrase — always include a safety net and referral option in closing — problem persists must be memorised
Example
if the problem persists after two weeks of using the saline spray please come back — we can discuss other options or refer you to your doctor.
WORD
CAUSATIVE STRUCTURE — CAUSE / LEAD TO / RESULT IN
📝 Things cause effects using a noun structure — not to + verb. Pattern: [cause] + cause/lead to/result in + [noun effect]. Smoking damages ✅ / smoking causes to damage ❌
Example
High fever can cause significant fluid loss — prolonged fever may lead to dehydration and in severe cases can result in seizures.
WORD
STATIVE VERBS — CONSIST OF / CONTAIN / INCLUDE
📝 These verbs describe a state not an action — never use is before them. Body consists of water ✅ / body is consist of water ❌
Example
Our body consists of approximately 60 to 70 percent water — which is why adequate daily fluid intake is essential.
WORD
TRANSITIVE VERBS — ACKNOWLEDGE / CONFIRM / ACCEPT
📝 These verbs take a direct object with no preposition — acknowledge this ✅ / acknowledge about this ❌. Same rule: confirm / accept / consider / understand
Example
Parents must acknowledge the early signs of fever and respond promptly rather than waiting.
WORD
EMBEDDED QUESTION — STATEMENT WORD ORDER
📝 When a question is inside a statement the word order flips to subject + verb — what the fever is ✅ / what is the fever ❌
Example
First we need to define what the fever is — then we can explain how the body responds.
WORD
TRIGGER (ASTHMA)
📝 A substance or condition that provokes an asthma attack — Richard specifically noted this word appears constantly in OET
Example
Identifying and avoiding personal asthma triggers is a key part of long-term asthma management.
WORD
INFLAMMATION
📝 The body's immune response causing redness swelling heat and pain — a core mechanism in asthma and many OET conditions — Richard specifically flagged this word
Example
Persistent airway inflammation in asthma leads to progressive narrowing and hyperresponsiveness of the airways.
WORD
ACUTE
📝 Sudden in onset and usually short in duration — contrasted with chronic — stress on uh-KYOOT
Example
Salbutamol is used for acute symptom relief while the preventer inhaler controls the chronic underlying inflammation.
WORD
NON-PHARMACEUTICAL
📝 Relating to treatment approaches that do not involve medication — including trigger avoidance environmental control and lifestyle modification
Example
non-pharmaceutical management of asthma includes avoiding known triggers and maintaining a clean low-allergen environment.
WORD
OCCUPATIONAL HEALTH
📝 The branch of healthcare concerned with the physical and mental wellbeing of workers — seen in Greg Lesson 14 Maria Chen text
Example
Maria Chen was an occupational health nurse who advised companies on staff wellbeing and mental health programmes.
WORD
STIGMA
📝 A mark of disgrace or disapproval attached to a condition or behaviour — particularly relevant in mental health contexts
Example
The stigma around mental health in the workplace is slowly fading as employers begin to offer counselling and support.
WORD
BURNOUT
📝 A state of chronic physical and emotional exhaustion caused by prolonged workplace stress — distinct from tiredness — stress on BURN-owt
Example
Many community pharmacists report burnout within the first five years due to high dispensing volumes and understaffing.
WORD
REFER A PATIENT
📝 To send a patient to another healthcare professional for specialist assessment or treatment — a core OET clinical action
Example
If the symptoms persist or are beyond the pharmacist's scope, the patient should be referred to their GP.
WORD
THERAPEUTIC
📝 Relating to the treatment or cure of a disease or disorder — stress on ther-uh-PYOO-tik
Example
The programme was designed to focus on treatment in a therapeutic way rather than simply managing symptoms.
WORD
DEHYDRATION
📝 A dangerous condition resulting from insufficient fluid intake or excessive fluid loss — common cause: fever vomiting diarrhoea or sweating
Example
Prolonged fever may result in dehydration due to excessive fluid loss through sweating.
WORD
FLUID INTAKE
📝 The total amount of liquid consumed — always a noun phrase — intake is a NOUN not a verb
Example
Patients should be advised to maintain adequate fluid intake particularly during illness when losses are increased.
WORD
HYDRATION
📝 The process of maintaining adequate fluid levels in the body — clinical equivalent of drinking enough water
Example
Adequate hydration is essential for normal digestive function and temperature regulation.
WORD
REGULATE
📝 To control or maintain something at a stable level — used for temperature blood pressure and organ function
Example
Water plays a critical role in helping the body regulate its internal temperature.
WORD
MONITOR
📝 To observe and check something regularly over time — a core clinical instruction verb
Example
We should monitor body temperature at regular intervals to detect any changes early.
WORD
VIGILANT
📝 Watchfully alert and attentive — used when advising carers or parents to watch for warning signs
Example
Parents and carers should be advised to remain vigilant for signs of fever or deterioration in young children.
WORD
CARER
📝 A person who provides care for someone who is sick elderly or dependent — an OET key word for written tasks
Example
Parents and carers should be advised to remain vigilant for early signs of fever in children.
WORD
IMMUNE RESPONSE
📝 The body's defensive reaction to a pathogen or foreign substance — fever is one manifestation of this response
Example
Fever is the body's immune response to an infection or pathogen entering the system.
WORD
EXCEED
📝 To go beyond a defined limit or threshold — used with temperature dosage and clinical parameters
Example
Fever is defined as a core body temperature exceeding 38 degrees Celsius.
WORD
PROLONGED
📝 Lasting for longer than expected or desired — Richard used this frequently: prolonged fever / prolonged use / prolonged exposure
Example
prolonged fever may result in dehydration and requires medical review if it does not resolve within 48 hours.
WORD
INTERVAL
📝 A defined period of time between events or measurements — used in monitoring frequency instructions
Example
Body temperature should be measured at regular intervals — every four hours during an acute febrile illness.
WORD
COPD (CHRONIC OBSTRUCTIVE PULMONARY DISEASE)
📝 A serious progressive lung condition caused primarily by long-term smoking — characterised by airflow limitation — always give the full form on first use
Example
COPD, which is a chronic respiratory disease, was identified as the primary reason for his progressive breathlessness.
WORD
NICOTINE DEPENDENCE
📝 The clinical term for nicotine addiction — preferred in formal and OET writing over nicotine addiction
Example
nicotine dependence makes smoking cessation particularly challenging without appropriate pharmacological support.
WORD
IMPAIR
📝 To weaken damage or reduce the effectiveness of a function or ability — stress on im-PAIR
Example
Second-hand smoke can impair cognitive function and respiratory development in children exposed during early childhood.
WORD
VULNERABLE
📝 Susceptible to harm or at increased risk — stress on VUL-ner-uh-bul
Example
Children are more vulnerable than adults to the effects of second-hand smoke on respiratory and cognitive development.
WORD
DETERIORATE
📝 To become progressively worse — INTRANSITIVE: cannot take a direct object — the thing itself deteriorates
Example
Without treatment the patient's respiratory function deteriorated steadily over the following months.
WORD
PHARMACOLOGICAL SUPPORT
📝 Treatment using medication — used when advising patients about cessation aids such as nicotine patches or varenicline
Example
Nicotine dependence makes cessation challenging without pharmacological support such as varenicline or nicotine replacement therapy.
WORD
SPITTING UP / VOMITING MILK (INFANT)
SPIT-ing UP / VOM-it-ing MILK 5 + 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈspɪtɪŋ ʌp/ | /ˈvɒmɪtɪŋ mɪlk/
⚠️ 'Spitting up' is the colloquial patient/parent term for infant regurgitation — recognise it and ask follow-up questions. Clinically may indicate gastro-oesophageal reflux (GOR). Distinguish from projectile vomiting (possible pyloric stenosis). 'Vomit' = VOM-it (stress on VOM, NOT vom-IT). OET tip: always explore frequency, volume, and weight gain when a parent describes spitting up.
Example 1
The mother reported that the baby had started spitting up after every feed — the pharmacist asked how much milk was coming back up and whether the baby was still gaining weight.
Example 2
Occasional spitting up in infants is normal, but if the baby is vomiting forcefully or not gaining weight, a GP review is needed.
📌 Key distinction for OET:
spitting up / regurgitation → usually normal, low volume | projectile vomiting → forceful, high volume → red flag for pyloric stenosis
WORD
RESUSCITATION
reh-SUS-ih-TAY-shun 5 syllables
British (RP): /rɪˌsʌsɪˈteɪʃən/
⚠️ Stress on TAY (4th syllable). Do NOT say 'reh-SUS-ih-tay-SHUN' or 'REH-sus-ih-tay-shun'. 5 syllables: reh-SUS-ih-TAY-shun. Resuscitation = the emergency process of restoring breathing and circulation — CPR, defibrillation, airway management. OET context: pharmacists may need to refer to resuscitation plans or emergency action plans when counselling carers.
Example 1
The emergency team commenced resuscitation immediately — chest compressions were started while the defibrillator was being prepared.
Example 2
The carer was advised to call 111 immediately and begin resuscitation if the patient became unresponsive and stopped breathing.
WORD
THE PARENTS ARE DEVASTATED
the PAR-unts ar dev-uh-STAY-tid 7 syllables
British (RP): /ðə ˈpɛərənts ɑː ˈdɛvəsteɪtɪd/
⚠️ 'Devastated' — stress on STAY: dev-uh-STAY-tid (4 syllables). Do NOT say 'DEV-uh-stay-tid' (wrong stress) or 'dev-uh-stat-ID'. 'Devastated' = deeply shocked and grief-stricken — strongest emotional response to bad news. OET key communication point: when parents are devastated, acknowledge the emotion FIRST before giving any further information. Never continue talking as if they haven't reacted.
Example 1
The parents were absolutely devastated when they were told the diagnosis — the pharmacist paused, expressed empathy, and allowed them time before continuing.
Example 2
It was clear the parents were devastated — the pharmacist said: 'I'm so sorry. This must be incredibly difficult to hear. Take all the time you need.'
📌 OET empathy response framework:
Acknowledge → Pause → Validate → Offer support → Then continue information
WORD
EMOTIONAL TOLL (ON HEALTHCARE WORKERS)
ih-MOH-shun-ul TOHL 5 syllables
British (RP): /ɪˈməʊʃənəl təʊl/
⚠️ 'Emotional' — stress on MOH: ih-MOH-shun-ul (4 syllables). 'Toll' = the cumulative cost or damage — rhymes with 'hole'. The emotional toll = the psychological burden accumulated over time from difficult clinical experiences. OET context: relevant in occupational health, burnout, and peer support discussions. Compassion fatigue is a related concept.
Example 1
The emotional toll of losing a paediatric patient is significant — many healthcare workers benefit from structured debriefing and peer support.
Example 2
Pharmacists working in palliative care often describe the emotional toll as one of the hardest aspects of the role — yet also the most meaningful.
📌 Related vocabulary cluster:
emotional toll | compassion fatigue | burnout | debriefing | peer support | psychological safety
WORD
UTENSILS
yoo-TEN-silz 3 syllables
British (RP): /juːˈtɛnsɪlz/
⚠️ Stress on TEN (2nd syllable). Do NOT say 'YOO-ten-silz' or 'yoo-ten-SILZ'. The /j/ glide before the long /uː/ is standard British RP — say 'YOOT-en-silz', not 'OO-ten-silz'. In clinical/OET context: refers to feeding implements (spoons, oral syringes, measuring cups) for administering medicine or food — especially in paediatric or aged care settings.
Example 1
The pharmacist advised the mother to use the oral syringe provided rather than household utensils, as the dosing markings ensure accurate measurement.
Example 2
All feeding utensils should be sterilised regularly — this is especially important for infants under six months.
WORD
ENLARGED SPLEEN / RISK OF RUPTURE (SPLENOMEGALY)
en-LARJD SPLEEN | splee-noh-MEG-uh-lee 3 syllables (splenomegaly = 5)
British (RP): /ɪnˈlɑːdʒd spliːn/ | /ˌspliːnəʊˈmɛɡəli/
⚠️ 'Enlarged' — stress on LARJD: en-LARJD. 'Splenomegaly' — stress on MEG: splee-noh-MEG-uh-lee (5 syllables). Splenomegaly = abnormal enlargement of the spleen — caused by infections (EBV/glandular fever), liver disease, or blood disorders. A significantly enlarged spleen carries a risk of rupture — a surgical emergency, especially after trauma. OET counselling key: advise avoiding contact sports and heavy lifting.
Example 1
The pharmacist noted the patient had an enlarged spleen — she advised him to avoid contact sports and to seek urgent help if he experienced sudden severe left-sided abdominal pain.
Example 2
Rupture of an enlarged spleen is rare but serious — patients should be warned to avoid any activity that could cause abdominal trauma.
📌 Safety netting for splenomegaly:
avoid contact sports | avoid heavy lifting | seek urgent care if: sudden severe left-sided pain / dizziness / signs of shock
WORD
STIGMATA (CLINICAL CONTEXT)
STIG-muh-tuh 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈstɪɡmətə/
⚠️ Stress on STIG (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'stig-MAH-tuh'. In medicine, 'stigmata' (plural of stigma) = physical signs or marks indicating a particular disease. Most commonly: 'stigmata of chronic liver disease' — includes jaundice, spider naevi, palmar erythema, leukonychia, and Dupuytren's contracture. Do NOT confuse with the social meaning (shame) or the religious meaning (wounds of Christ).
Example 1
On examination the patient showed several stigmata of chronic liver disease — jaundice, spider naevi on the chest, and palmar erythema were all noted.
Example 2
The presence of stigmata of liver disease on physical examination prompted further investigation into her alcohol history and liver function.
📌 Stigmata of chronic liver disease — key signs:
jaundice | spider naevi | palmar erythema | leukonychia (white nails) | Dupuytren's contracture | gynaecomastia | caput medusae
WORD
CONVENT
KON-vunt 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈkɒnvənt/
⚠️ Stress on KON (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'kon-VENT'. 'Convent' = a religious community of nuns — relevant in OET or clinical contexts when a patient's lifestyle, contraception choices, or social history involves religious life. Always approach such patients with sensitivity and without assumptions about their healthcare needs.
Example 1
The patient had lived in a convent for many years — the pharmacist was sensitive to her religious beliefs when discussing treatment options.
Example 2
A patient from a convent may have different healthcare priorities — always ask open questions and respect her beliefs without making assumptions.
WORD
COPPER IUD (INTRAUTERINE DEVICE)
IN-truh-YOO-ter-in DEH-vys 6 syllables (intrauterine = 5)
British (RP): /ˈkɒpər ˌɪntrəˈjuːtərɪn dɪˈvaɪs/
⚠️ 'Intrauterine' — stress on YOO: IN-truh-YOO-ter-in (5 syllables). Do NOT say 'in-truh-OO-ter-in' (missing the /j/ glide). Copper IUD = a small T-shaped copper device inserted into the uterus — long-term reversible contraception, also used as emergency contraception. Contains NO hormones — ideal for women who cannot use hormonal contraception. OET: relevant in contraception counselling or gynaecological history-taking.
Example 1
The patient had been using a copper IUD for several years — the pharmacist confirmed she had no symptoms of infection or displacement and reminded her of the importance of regular GP follow-up.
Example 2
A copper IUD is a hormone-free option that can remain effective for up to ten years — it also provides emergency contraception if inserted within five days of unprotected intercourse.
📌 Copper IUD vs hormonal IUD (Mirena):
copper IUD → no hormones, periods may be heavier | hormonal IUD (Mirena) → releases progestogen, periods often lighter or absent
WORD
NUN
NUN 1 syllable
British (RP): /nʌn/
⚠️ 'Nun' rhymes with 'sun', 'fun', 'run' — short /ʌ/ vowel. Do NOT say 'noon' (that is a different word). A nun = a woman who has taken religious vows and lives in a convent. Relevant in OET contexts when taking a social or contraceptive history. OET sensitivity principle: always approach religious identity respectfully — do not make assumptions about healthcare preferences, and do not volunteer unnecessary personal questions.
Example 1
The patient identified herself as a nun — the pharmacist was respectful and non-judgemental when discussing her medical history, focusing on her presenting symptoms.
Example 2
When taking a contraceptive history, a patient who is a nun may decline certain options for religious reasons — always acknowledge and respect her decision without pressure.
📌 OET cultural sensitivity principle:
respect religious identity | avoid assumptions | ask open questions | never pressure | document patient's preferences clearly
WORD
HAV VS HBV (HEPATITIS A VS B)
HEP-uh-ty-tis AY vs BEE 5 syllables (hepatitis)
British (RP): /ˌhɛpəˈtaɪtɪs eɪ/ vs /ˌhɛpəˈtaɪtɪs biː/
⚠️ 'Hepatitis' — stress on TY: hep-uh-TY-tis (4 syllables). Do NOT say 'HEP-uh-titis' or 'hep-uh-TIT-iss'. HAV = Hepatitis A Virus → faecal-oral transmission (contaminated food/water) → acute illness ONLY, no chronic form, self-limiting, vaccine available. HBV = Hepatitis B Virus → blood and body fluids (unprotected sex, shared needles, mother-to-child) → CAN become chronic → risk of cirrhosis and liver cancer. OET key: know the transmission routes and chronicity distinction.
Example 1
Hepatitis A spreads through contaminated food and water — it causes acute illness only and does not become chronic.
Example 2
Hepatitis B is transmitted through blood and body fluids and can become a long-term condition — this is why vaccination and regular monitoring are so important.
📌 HAV vs HBV — critical OET distinction:
HAV → faecal-oral | acute only | self-limiting | vaccine available || HBV → blood/body fluids | may become CHRONIC | risk of cirrhosis/liver cancer | vaccine available
WORD
PAIN DESCRIBED AS CONSTANT
payn dih-SKRYBD az KON-stunt 5 syllables
British (RP): /peɪn dɪˈskraɪbd æz ˈkɒnstənt/
⚠️ 'Constant' — stress on KON: KON-stunt. 'Described' — stress on SKRYBD. In pain history-taking, 'constant' = continuous and unremitting — does NOT come and go. Contrast: intermittent (comes and goes) | colicky (waves of severe pain, then eases) | breakthrough (sudden spike over background pain). OET tip: always clarify — 'Is the pain there all the time, or does it come and go?'
Example 1
The patient described the pain as constant — it had been present continuously for three days and did not ease with rest or paracetamol.
Example 2
Constant pain that does not change with position or activity may suggest inflammation or a structural cause — further investigation was recommended.
📌 Pain character vocabulary for OET:
constant | intermittent | colicky | dull/aching | sharp/stabbing | burning | throbbing | cramping | radiating
WORD
CRYING HIS EYES OUT (IDIOM)
CRY-ing hiz EYZ owt Idiomatic phrase — no stress marking required
British (RP): /ˈkraɪɪŋ hɪz aɪz aʊt/
⚠️ This is an idiom — 'eyes out' is figurative, NOT literal. Meaning: to cry very intensely and for a long time. Patients and carers commonly use this to describe a child's or their own extreme distress. OET response: do NOT ignore the emotion — acknowledge it immediately. 'It sounds like he's been really distressed — that must be so worrying for you.' Then explore clinically.
Example 1
The mother said her baby had been crying his eyes out all night — the pharmacist acknowledged her concern and asked whether the baby had a temperature or any other symptoms.
Example 2
She said she had been crying her eyes out since the diagnosis — the pharmacist paused, expressed empathy, and gave her a moment before continuing.
📌 Related idioms for distress — recognise in OET listening:
crying his/her eyes out | beside himself/herself | at her wit's end | falling apart | completely devastated | inconsolable
WORD
RUSHING TO THE LOO (IDIOM)
RUSH-ing to thuh LOO 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈrʌʃɪŋ tə ðə luː/
⚠️ 'Loo' = British colloquial for toilet/bathroom. Very commonly used by British patients — essential for OET listening. 'Rushing to the loo' = urgency to use the toilet — describes diarrhoea, bowel urgency, or urinary urgency. OET response: recognise it, use it back naturally or rephrase clinically — 'So you're finding you need to rush to the toilet quite urgently?' Then explore: frequency, blood/mucus, pain.
Example 1
The patient said he kept having to rush to the loo — the pharmacist asked how many times a day this was happening and whether there was any blood or mucus.
Example 2
She described rushing to the loo immediately after eating — the pharmacist noted this pattern was consistent with irritable bowel syndrome and arranged a GP referral.
📌 British toilet idioms — recognise in OET listening:
the loo | the bathroom | the toilet | spending a penny | caught short | caught short | bowel urgency | urinary urgency
WORD
FEELING GASSY / MY STOMACH FEELS GASSY
FEE-ling GA-see 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈfiːlɪŋ ˈɡæsi/
⚠️ 'Gassy' = informal patient term for excessive gas, bloating, or flatulence. Clinical equivalents: flatulence, bloating, abdominal distension, meteorism. OET context: rephrase gently — 'So you're experiencing a lot of bloating or wind?' Common causes: IBS, lactose intolerance, high-fibre diet changes, or medications (e.g. metformin). Always explore: timing (after meals?), associated bowel changes, duration.
Example 1
The patient said her stomach felt really gassy — the pharmacist asked whether it was worse after eating and whether she had noticed any change in her bowel habits.

📌 Colloquial → clinical translation for OET:
gassy / full of wind → flatulence / bloating | tummy pain → abdominal pain | runny tummy → diarrhoea | being sick → vomiting | off my food → reduced appetite
WORD
ITCHY / ITCHINESS / PRURITUS
ICH-ee | ICH-ee-ness | proo-RY-tus 2 / 3 / 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈɪtʃi/ | /ˈɪtʃinəs/ | /prʊˈraɪtəs/
⚠️ 'Pruritus' — stress on RY: proo-RY-tus (3 syllables). Do NOT say 'PROO-ri-tus' or 'pru-RI-tis'. 'Itchy' is the patient term — always acceptable to use it. Pruritus = clinical term for the sensation that provokes scratching. Causes: eczema, allergic reaction, dry skin, liver disease (bile salt deposition), renal failure, drug reactions (e.g. opioids). OET: ask location, severity, triggers, associated rash.
Example 1
The patient complained of an itchy rash on both arms — the pharmacist asked how long it had been there, whether anything triggered it, and whether she had started any new medications.

📌 OET itch history — key questions:
Where is it? | How long? | Is there a rash? | Any new soaps/detergents/medications? | Any known allergies? | Does anything make it better or worse?
WORD
INTRUSIVE
in-TROO-siv 3 syllables
British (RP): /ɪnˈtruːsɪv/
⚠️ Stress on TROO (2nd syllable). Do NOT say 'IN-troo-siv' or 'in-troo-SIV'. Meaning: causing unwanted disruption by entering someone's space or privacy. Clinical uses: (1) intrusive thoughts — unwanted, distressing thoughts (OCD, PTSD); (2) intrusive questioning — asking overly personal questions without sensitivity. OET context: be aware of how your questions land — frame sensitive topics carefully to avoid seeming intrusive.
Example 1
The pharmacist was careful not to appear intrusive when asking about the patient's contraceptive history, using open and empathetic language.
Example 2

WORD
DEFICIT VS DEFICIENCY
DEF-ih-sit vs dih-FISH-un-see 3 / 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈdɛfɪsɪt/ | /dɪˈfɪʃənsi/
⚠️ 'Deficit' — stress on DEF: DEF-ih-sit (3 syllables). 'Deficiency' — stress on FISH: dih-FISH-un-see (4 syllables). KEY DISTINCTION: DEFICIT = a shortage or gap in quantity (e.g. fluid deficit, attention deficit, cognitive deficit). DEFICIENCY = a lack of an essential substance or quality (e.g. iron deficiency, vitamin D deficiency, immune deficiency). Deficit → how much is missing. Deficiency → what essential thing is absent.
Example 1
The patient had an iron deficiency — her haemoglobin showed a significant deficit compared to the normal range.
Example 2
A vitamin D deficiency is common in New Zealand's elderly population — a daily supplement can correct the deficit within a few months.
📌 Deficit vs Deficiency — quick rule:
DEFICIT → quantity shortfall ('not enough of something') | DEFICIENCY → absence of something essential ('missing a vital substance')
WORD
FACIAL HAIR (IN WOMEN) / HIRSUTISM
FAY-shul HAIR | HUR-syoo-tiz-um 3 / 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈfeɪʃəl hɛər/ | /ˈhɜːsjuːtɪzəm/
⚠️ 'Hirsutism' — stress on HUR: HUR-syoo-tiz-um (4 syllables). Do NOT say 'hir-SOO-tiz-um'. Hirsutism = excessive growth of dark, coarse hair on a woman's face, chest, or back — caused by elevated androgens. Seen in: Cushing's syndrome, PCOS, long-term steroid use, adrenal tumours. Patient term: 'facial hair' or 'hair on my chin/face'. OET: always approach with empathy — this is a sensitive and distressing symptom for many patients.
Example 1
The patient mentioned she had been developing facial hair over the past few months — the pharmacist noted this alongside her other symptoms and recommended an urgent GP review.
Example 2
Hirsutism in women can be caused by elevated cortisol levels — it is one of the features that may suggest Cushing's syndrome.
📌 Cushing's syndrome symptom cluster — OET red flags:
central obesity | buffalo hump | moon face | facial hair (hirsutism) | stretch marks (striae) | easy bruising | poor wound healing | acne | skin fragility
WORD
MY STOMACH HAS BECOME HUGE / ABDOMINAL DISTENSION
ab-DOM-in-ul dis-TEN-shun 7 syllables
British (RP): /æbˈdɒmɪnəl dɪˈstɛnʃən/
⚠️ 'Abdominal' — stress on DOM. 'Distension' — stress on TEN: dis-TEN-shun (3 syllables). Patient expressions: 'my stomach has become huge', 'my belly is really swollen', 'I look pregnant'. Clinically: abdominal distension. In Cushing's syndrome: caused by central obesity and fat redistribution. Also seen in ascites, bowel obstruction. OET rephrase: 'So you've noticed your abdomen has become significantly larger?'

SEN-trul oh-BEE-sih-tee 6 syllables
British (RP): /ˈsɛntrəl əʊˈbiːsɪti/
⚠️ 'Obesity' — stress on BEE: oh-BEE-sih-tee (4 syllables). Do NOT say 'ob-EE-sih-tee' or 'OH-bees-ih-tee'. Central obesity = fat accumulation around the abdomen/trunk rather than limbs — a hallmark of Cushing's syndrome and metabolic syndrome. Patient expressions: 'weight around my middle', 'belly fat', 'I've put on a lot around my tummy'. OET tip: contrast with peripheral obesity (limbs and buttocks).

WORD
BUFFALO HUMP / DORSOCERVICAL FAT PAD
BUF-uh-loh HUMP | dor-soh-SUR-vih-kul fat pad 4 / 7 syllables

⚠️ 'Dorsocervical' — stress on SUR: dor-soh-SUR-vih-kul (5 syllables). Patient term: 'a hump on my back' or 'a lump at the back of my neck'. Clinical term: dorsocervical fat pad or buffalo hump. The patient pointed to a noticeable hump at the back of her neck — the pharmacist recognised this as a potential sign of Cushing's syndrome and recommended an urgent GP review.
Example 2
A buffalo hump, along with central obesity and moon face, forms part of the classic triad of Cushing's syndrome features.

moon face (rounded, puffy face) | buffalo hump (fat pad at back of neck) | central obesity (truncal weight gain with slim limbs)

EXCESSIVELY
ek-SES-iv-lee 4 syllables

⚠️ Stress on SES (2nd syllable). Do NOT say 'EK-ses-iv-lee' or 'ek-ses-IV-lee'. 'Excessively' = to a degree that is more than necessary, normal, or desirable. OET tip: use this word to convey severity without being alarmist — 'Have you been sweating excessively, even when you're not particularly warm?'

WORD

poor WOOND HEE-ling 4 syllables
British (RP): /pɔː wuːnd ˈhiːlɪŋ/
⚠️ 'Wound' = WOOND (rhymes with 'mooned') — do NOT say 'WOWND' (that is 'wound' as in 'wound up'). Patient expression: 'it takes ages to heal', 'cuts don't heal properly'. Clinical term: impaired or delayed wound healing. Caused by: cortisol excess (Cushing's, long-term steroids), diabetes, poor circulation, malnutrition. skin fruh-JIL-ih-tee 5 syllables

⚠️ 'Fragility' — stress on JIL: fruh-JIL-ih-tee (4 syllables). Do NOT say 'FRAJ-ih-lih-tee'. Skin fragility = skin that bruises, tears, or breaks easily — caused by cortisol-induced collagen breakdown. Patient expression: 'if I get a cut or scratch it bleeds a lot and doesn't heal', 'my skin tears easily', 'I bruise at the slightest thing'. OET: ask 'Do you find your skin bruises or tears more easily than it used to?'

WORD
STRETCH MARKS / STRIAE
STRECH MARKS | STRY-ee 2 / 2 syllables
British (RP): /strɛtʃ mɑːks/ | /ˈstraɪiː/
⚠️ 'Striae' — pronounced STRY-ee (2 syllables, stress on STRY). Do NOT say 'STREE-ay' or 'STRAY-ee'. Striae = the clinical term for stretch marks. In Cushing's syndrome: striae are characteristically wide, purple or reddish, and appear on the abdomen, thighs, upper arms — caused by skin thinning and rapid weight gain from cortisol excess. Contrast with normal striae (white/silver, from pregnancy or puberty — narrower and less colourful).
Example 1
The patient showed the pharmacist red stretch marks on her abdomen and upper arms — the pharmacist noted these were wider and more deeply coloured than typical and recommended urgent GP assessment.
Example 2
Wide purple striae are a hallmark feature of Cushing's syndrome — they differ from normal stretch marks in their colour, width, and location.
📌 Cushing's striae vs normal striae:
Cushing's: wide, purple/red, abdomen + thighs + arms | Normal: narrow, white/silver, from pregnancy or puberty
WORD
SKIN LOOKS RED AND PUFFY / MOON FACE / PLETHORIC FACE
MOON fayss | pleh-THOR-ik fayss 2 / 4 syllables

⚠️ 'Plethoric' — stress on THOR: pleh-THOR-ik (3 syllables). Do NOT say 'PLETH-or-ik'. Moon face = a rounded, full, puffy face from fat redistribution — a classic sign of Cushing's syndrome. Plethoric face = flushed, reddish appearance from increased blood vessel fragility. Patient expressions: 'my face looks really puffy', 'my skin is red', 'I look like I've gained weight in my face'. The patient said she used to have quite pale skin but now it looked red and puffy — the pharmacist recognised this as a plethoric appearance consistent with possible Cushing's syndrome.

Moon face is caused by cortisol-driven fat redistribution to the face and is one of the most visible signs of Cushing's syndrome.

⚠️ Stress on AK (1st syllable): AK-nee. Do NOT say 'ak-NEE' or 'AK-nay'. Acne = a skin condition causing spots, pimples, and cysts. ⚠️ 'Apart' — stress on PART: uh-PART. This is a key OET listening signal — 'apart from X' means 'in addition to X'. When a patient says this, they are about to disclose MORE medical history beyond what they have already mentioned. Always listen carefully after 'apart from' and document every condition mentioned. Rephrase clinically: 'So in addition to your hypertension, are there any other medical conditions I should know about?'

WORD
BLOOD-THINNING MEDICATION / ANTICOAGULANT / ANTIPLATELET
an-tee-koh-AG-yoo-lunt | an-tee-PLAYT-let 5 / 4 syllables

⚠️ 'Anticoagulant' — stress on AG: an-tee-koh-AG-yoo-lunt (5 syllables). 'Antiplatelet' — stress on PLAYT: an-tee-PLAYT-let (4 syllables). Patient term: 'blood thinners' or 'blood-thinning tablets'. OET CRITICAL: always ask about these before any procedure or surgery — increased bleeding risk. Examples: warfarin, apixaban, rivaroxaban (anticoagulants); aspirin, clopidogrel (antiplatelets). WORD
STENTS (CORONARY / VASCULAR)
STENT 1 syllable
British (RP): /stɛnt/
⚠️ 'Stent' — 1 syllable, rhymes with 'bent' and 'went'. Do NOT say 'STEN-t' with a split or 'STEE-ent'. A stent = a small mesh tube inserted into a narrowed or blocked blood vessel to keep it open. Coronary stents are placed after a heart attack. Patients with stents are typically on dual antiplatelet therapy — aspirin + clopidogrel — and must NEVER stop without cardiology advice (risk of stent thrombosis = life-threatening). OET red flag.
Example 1
The patient mentioned he had had two stents fitted after his heart attack — the pharmacist confirmed he was still taking both aspirin and clopidogrel and stressed the importance of never stopping them without speaking to his cardiologist.
Example 2
Patients with coronary stents on antiplatelet therapy must inform all healthcare providers — stopping these medications even briefly can cause the stent to block suddenly.
📌 Stent patient counselling — OET must know:
confirm antiplatelet therapy (aspirin + clopidogrel) | NEVER stop without cardiology advice | inform all HCPs before any procedure | alert card — patient should carry one
WORD
I SPRAINED IT BADLY / SPRAIN VS STRAIN
SPRAYND | STRAYN 1 syllable each
British (RP): /spreɪnd/ | /streɪn/
⚠️ 'Sprained' — SPRAYND (1 syllable). 'Strained' — STRAYND (1 syllable). These sound similar — do not confuse them. KEY DISTINCTION: SPRAIN = injury to a LIGAMENT (connects bone to bone) — e.g. sprained ankle. STRAIN = injury to a MUSCLE or TENDON (connects muscle to bone) — e.g. pulled hamstring. OET: when a patient says 'I sprained it badly', ask: Was it X-rayed? Is it still giving trouble? Any ongoing instability?
Example 1
The patient said she had sprained her ankle badly the previous year — the pharmacist asked whether it had fully recovered and whether she had any ongoing pain or instability.
Example 2
A severe ankle sprain can sometimes cause ligament damage that leads to long-term instability — if symptoms persist, a physiotherapy referral may be appropriate.
📌 Sprain vs Strain — OET distinction:
SPRAIN → ligament injury (bone-to-bone) → e.g. ankle sprain | STRAIN → muscle/tendon injury → e.g. pulled hamstring, back strain
WORD
CENTRAL INCISORS (THE TWO FRONT TEETH)
in-SY-zorz 3 syllables
British (RP): /ɪnˈsaɪzəz/
⚠️ 'Incisors' — stress on SY: in-SY-zorz (3 syllables). Do NOT say 'IN-sih-zorz' or 'in-SIZ-orz'. Central incisors = the two front teeth in the centre of the upper or lower jaw — the most visible teeth. Upper = maxillary central incisors; lower = mandibular central incisors. Patient term: 'my two front teeth'. OET dental/trauma context: relevant in documenting tooth injuries, facial trauma, or dental history.

WORD
UM, WELL, I SUPPOSE THE MAIN THING IS... (HESITATION FILLERS)
Idiomatic — no stress marking required Patient speech pattern
British (RP): /ʌm wɛl aɪ səˈpəʊz ðə meɪn θɪŋ ɪz/
⚠️ This is a critical OET listening signal — NOT just filler noise. When a patient uses 'um', 'well', 'I suppose', 'I mean', 'the main thing is' — they are building up to their PRIMARY concern. This is the moment to listen most carefully. OET tip: do NOT jump in or interrupt during hesitation fillers. Let the patient finish. The concern that follows is almost always the most clinically important thing they want to discuss.
Example 1
The patient said, 'Um, well, I suppose the main thing is... I'm a bit worried about the anaesthetic' — the pharmacist recognised this as the patient's core concern and gave it full, unhurried attention.
Example 2
'What I really wanted to ask was...' and 'the main thing on my mind is...' are similar signals — always slow down and listen carefully when you hear these phrases.
📌 OET listening — patient concern signals:
'Um, well...' | 'I suppose the main thing is...' | 'What I really wanted to ask...' | 'The thing that worries me most...' | 'I mean, will I...' → ALL signal the patient's PRIMARY concern
WORD

pohst-OP-ur-uh-tiv 5 syllables

⚠️ 'Post-operative' — stress on OP: pohst-OP-ur-uh-tiv (5 syllables). Do NOT say 'pohst-op-UR-uh-tiv'. Patient expressions: 'after the operation', 'when I wake up', 'after the surgery'. Clinical: post-operative period. OET pre-op counselling: patients commonly ask about what to expect on waking — pain, nausea, disorientation, monitoring equipment. Address these concerns directly, empathetically, and in plain language.
Example 1
The patient asked what would happen after the operation when she woke up — the pharmacist explained she would be in a recovery room with nurses monitoring her, and that any pain or nausea would be treated promptly.
Example 2
In the post-operative period, patients are monitored for pain, nausea, blood pressure, and oxygen levels — this is a normal part of the recovery process.
WORD
WILL I BE IN A LOT OF PAIN? / POST-OPERATIVE PAIN MANAGEMENT
pohst-OP-ur-uh-tiv PAYN MAN-ij-munt 8 syllables
British (RP): /pəʊst ˈɒpərətɪv peɪn ˈmænɪdʒmənt/
⚠️ 'Management' — stress on MAN: MAN-ij-munt (3 syllables). Fear of post-operative pain is one of the most common pre-operative patient concerns. OET communication principle: (1) acknowledge the concern empathetically FIRST — 'That's a completely understandable worry'; (2) then reassure with factual information about the pain management plan; (3) do NOT dismiss or minimise. Options include: regular paracetamol, NSAIDs, opioids, patient-controlled analgesia (PCA), nerve blocks.
Example 1
The patient anxiously asked whether she would be in a lot of pain after the operation — the pharmacist acknowledged her concern and explained that the team would monitor her pain closely and act quickly if needed.
Example 2
Post-operative pain management typically involves a combination of regular paracetamol, anti-inflammatory medication, and stronger pain relief if necessary.
📌 OET empathy + information framework for pain concerns:
(1) Acknowledge: 'That's a very understandable concern' | (2) Reassure: 'The team will monitor your pain closely' | (3) Inform: explain the pain relief plan | (4) Invite questions: 'Does that help to put your mind at ease?'

⚠️ 'Come around' / 'come round' = British English idiom for regaining consciousness after anaesthesia or sedation. Recognise this immediately in OET listening. Clinical equivalent: emergence from anaesthesia / regaining consciousness. 'Consciousness' — stress on KON: KON-shus-ness (3 syllables). FIZ-ih-kul EK-sur-shun 5 syllables

⚠️ 'Exertion' — stress on ZUR: eg-ZUR-shun (3 syllables). Do NOT say 'EK-zur-shun' or 'ex-UR-shun'. Physical exertion = strenuous physical activity that increases heart rate and breathing demand. British (RP): /taɪt əˈkrɒs ðə tʃɛst/
⚠️ Patient expression for chest tightness or pressure — a RED FLAG symptom in OET. 'Tight across the chest' describes a squeezing or constricting sensation. Causes: angina (exertional, relieved by rest or GTN), asthma (with wheeze/cough), anxiety, musculoskeletal. OET: NEVER minimise chest tightness. Always explore: character, onset, duration, radiation (arm/jaw?), associated symptoms (SOB, palpitations, sweating), triggers.
Example 1
The patient said she had felt a bit tight across the chest during her morning walk — the pharmacist asked whether it had happened before, how long it lasted, and whether it spread to her arm or jaw.
Example 2
Chest tightness on exertion that resolves with rest is a classic presentation of stable angina and requires urgent GP referral.
📌 Chest tightness — OET history-taking framework (SOCRATES):
Site | Onset | Character (tight/sharp/burning) | Radiation (arm/jaw/back?) | Associated symptoms (SOB/sweating/nausea) | Timing | Exacerbating/relieving factors | Severity
WORD
THIS IS THE FIRST TIME I'VE REALLY BEEN ILL
Patient statement — no stress marking required Conversational phrase
British (RP): /ðɪs ɪz ðə fɜːst taɪm aɪv ˈrɪəli bɪn ɪl/
⚠️ This patient statement signals: (1) no significant prior medical history; (2) the current illness feels unusually severe or frightening to the patient; (3) they may have limited health literacy or high anxiety. OET communication response: acknowledge empathetically FIRST — 'I can understand this must feel quite unsettling if you're not used to being unwell.' Then take extra care to explain clearly. Do NOT rush past this statement.
Example 1
The patient said this was the first time she had really been ill — the pharmacist acknowledged how unsettling that must be and took extra care to explain everything clearly and at a slower pace.
Example 2
When a patient says it's the first time they've been seriously ill, it's a signal to slow down, use plain language, and check understanding at every step.
📌 OET response framework for anxious first-time patients:
(1) Acknowledge: 'That must feel quite frightening' | (2) Normalise: 'It's completely understandable to feel worried' | (3) Reassure: 'You're in the right place' | (4) Explain clearly: use plain language, check understanding frequently
WORD
PASSES VERY SMELLY WIND / MALODOROUS FLATUS
mal-OH-dur-us FLAY-tus 5 syllables
British (RP): /mælˈəʊdərəs ˈfleɪtəs/
⚠️ 'Malodorous' — stress on OH: mal-OH-dur-us (4 syllables). 'Flatus' — stress on FLAY: FLAY-tus (2 syllables). Patient expression: 'I pass very smelly wind', 'I have bad wind', 'I'm very gassy and it smells terrible'. Clinical: malodorous flatus. Caused by bacterial fermentation of undigested food — seen in IBS, lactose intolerance, coeliac disease, SIBO. OET rephrase: 'So you've been passing a lot of wind, and it has a particularly strong odour?'
Example 1
The patient mentioned she had been passing very smelly wind for the past few weeks — the pharmacist asked about her diet, bowel habits, and whether she had noticed any bloating.
Example 2
Malodorous flatus associated with bloating, cramping, and changed bowel habits may suggest malabsorption or an underlying bowel condition — a GP referral is warranted.
📌 IBS symptom cluster — OET GI history:
bloating | cramping (colicky pain) | altered bowel habit (diarrhoea/constipation/alternating) | smelly wind | urgency | pain relieved by opening bowels | no blood/weight loss (distinguishes from IBD)
WORD
IMODIUM (LOPERAMIDE)
ih-MOH-dee-um | loh-PER-uh-myd 4 / 4 syllables
British (RP): /ɪˈməʊdiəm/ | /ləʊˈpɛrəmaɪd/
⚠️ 'Imodium' — stress on MOH: ih-MOH-dee-um. 'Loperamide' — stress on PER: loh-PER-uh-myd (4 syllables). Loperamide = an antidiarrhoeal agent — slows bowel motility by acting on mu-opioid receptors in the gut wall. OET counselling: (1) short-term use only; (2) do NOT use if diarrhoea is bloody or infection suspected; (3) not for children under 12 without medical advice; (4) max 4 capsules (8mg)/day; (5) seek GP advice if diarrhoea persists beyond 48 hours.
Example 1
The patient asked if Imodium would help — the pharmacist asked how long the diarrhoea had been present and whether there was any blood, before advising on its appropriate use.
Example 2
Loperamide can be used for short-term relief of diarrhoea, but should not be used if you have bloody stools or if you think you may have a gut infection.
📌 Loperamide (Imodium) — OET counselling checklist:
✅ Short-term diarrhoea relief | ❌ NOT if bloody stool | ❌ NOT if infection suspected | ❌ NOT under 12 without advice | Max 8mg/day | See GP if >48 hours
WORD
BARIUM ENEMA
BAIR-ee-um EN-uh-muh 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˈbɛəriəm ˈɛnəmə/
⚠️ 'Barium' — stress on BAIR: BAIR-ee-um (3 syllables). 'Enema' — stress on EN: EN-uh-muh (3 syllables). Do NOT say 'bar-EE-um' or 'en-EE-muh'. A barium enema = barium contrast introduced rectally to coat the large bowel lining — X-rays then visualise the colon. Used to detect: colorectal cancer, polyps, diverticular disease, Crohn's. Now largely replaced by colonoscopy and CT colonography, but still appears in OET listening passages and patient records.
Example 1
The GP had arranged a barium enema to investigate the change in bowel habits — the pharmacist explained what the procedure involved and what to expect on the day.
Example 2
A barium enema can reveal structural changes in the large bowel such as polyps or narrowing — the patient will need to follow a special bowel preparation diet beforehand.
WORD
IT CAN'T BE ALL IN THE HEAD / PSYCHOSOMATIC SYMPTOMS
sy-koh-soh-MAT-ik 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌsaɪkəʊsəˈmætɪk/
⚠️ 'Psychosomatic' — stress on MAT: sy-koh-soh-MAT-ik (5 syllables). 'All in the head' = dismissive lay expression implying symptoms are imaginary. CRITICAL OET communication point: psychosomatic symptoms are REAL physical symptoms influenced by psychological factors — they are NEVER 'made up'. Responding with dismissiveness damages the therapeutic relationship and is penalised in OET. Always validate: 'Your symptoms are absolutely real — let's work out what's causing them.'
Example 1
The patient became upset and said she knew her pain was real and couldn't be all in her head — the pharmacist validated her experience and reassured her that her symptoms were being taken seriously.
Example 2
Psychosomatic symptoms such as IBS or tension headaches have a real physical component — dismissing them as 'all in the head' is both inaccurate and harmful to the therapeutic relationship.
📌 OET validation response — never dismiss symptoms:
❌ 'There's nothing physically wrong' | ❌ 'It might just be stress' (without validation) | ✅ 'Your symptoms are real and we take them seriously' | ✅ 'Let's look at what might be contributing to how you feel'
WORD
BRING BACK A FEW REASSURING THINGS / PATIENT REASSURANCE
reh-SHOOR-unss 3 syllables
British (RP): /rɪˈʃʊərəns/
⚠️ 'Reassurance' — stress on SHOOR: reh-SHOOR-unss (3 syllables). Do NOT say 'REE-uh-shoor-unss' or 'reh-shoor-ANTS'. Reassurance = a deliberate clinical communication strategy — ending a consultation by reinforcing what is NOT wrong and what IS being done. Essential in IBS, functional disorders, and anxiety-related consultations. OET structure: (1) acknowledge concern, (2) share reassuring findings, (3) outline plan, (4) safety net.
Example 1
The pharmacist tried to bring back a few reassuring things at the end of the consultation — explaining that the tests had ruled out anything serious and that the symptoms were manageable.
Example 2
Effective reassurance is not just saying 'don't worry' — it means specifically naming what has been ruled out and clearly explaining what happens next.
📌 OET reassurance framework (end of consultation):
(1) Name what's been ruled out: 'The tests show no sign of X' | (2) Validate: 'Your symptoms are real and understandable' | (3) Plan: 'Here's what we're going to do' | (4) Safety net: 'If things change, here's when to come back'
WORD
CRAMPING FEELING / ABDOMINAL CRAMPS / COLICKY PAIN
ab-DOM-in-ul KRAMPS | KOL-ih-kee payn 5 / 4 syllables
British (RP): /æbˈdɒmɪnəl kræmps/ | /ˈkɒlɪki peɪn/
⚠️ 'Colicky' — stress on KOL: KOL-ih-kee (3 syllables). Do NOT say 'kol-IK-ee'. Cramping / colicky pain = intermittent, spasmodic abdominal pain caused by bowel wall muscle contractions — comes in waves and may ease between episodes. Common in: IBS, gastroenteritis, lactose intolerance, coeliac disease. Distinguish from constant pain (structural/inflammatory). OET: 'Does the pain come in waves, or is it there all the time?'
Example 1
The patient described a cramping feeling in her lower abdomen that came and went throughout the day — the pharmacist asked whether it was related to eating or bowel movements.
Example 2
Colicky abdominal pain that is relieved after opening the bowels is a characteristic feature of irritable bowel syndrome.
📌 Colicky vs constant pain — OET distinction:
COLICKY → comes in waves, may ease between | causes: IBS, bowel spasm, gastroenteritis | CONSTANT → unremitting | causes: inflammation, structural, ischaemia → more serious
WORD
OFTEN FEELS BLOATED / BLOATING
BLOH-ting 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈbləʊtɪŋ/
⚠️ 'Bloating' — stress on BLOH: BLOH-ting (2 syllables). Do NOT say 'bloh-TING'. Bloating = a sensation of fullness, tightness, or swelling in the abdomen — often with visible distension. Patient expressions: 'I feel bloated', 'my stomach feels full and tight', 'I feel gassy'. Common causes: IBS, lactose intolerance, coeliac disease, SIBO, constipation. OET: 'Is it worse after eating? Does it improve after passing wind or opening your bowels?'
Example 1
The patient said she often felt bloated, especially after meals — the pharmacist asked whether any particular foods seemed to trigger it and whether it improved after using the toilet.
Example 2
Bloating that is relieved after passing wind or opening the bowels suggests a functional gut disorder such as IBS rather than a structural cause.
WORD
THE URGE TO GO TO THE TOILET / FAECAL URGENCY
FEE-kul UR-jun-see 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˈfiːkəl ˈɜːdʒənsi/
⚠️ 'Faecal' — stress on FEE: FEE-kul (2 syllables). 'Urgency' — stress on UR: UR-jun-see (3 syllables). Faecal urgency = a sudden, strong, difficult-to-defer urge to open the bowels. Patient expressions: 'I need to rush to the toilet', 'I can't hold on', 'it comes on suddenly'. Key symptom of IBS-D, IBD, and bowel urgency disorders. OET: 'Do you ever feel like you need to rush to the toilet urgently and find it difficult to hold on?'
Example 1
The patient described a sudden urge to go to the toilet that came on without warning — the pharmacist asked how often this happened and whether she had ever not made it in time.
Example 2
Faecal urgency that is waking the patient from sleep, or associated with blood in the stool, should be referred urgently to a GP.
📌 IBS-D vs IBD — OET red flag distinction:
IBS-D: urgency + cramping + no blood + relieved by defaecation + worse with stress | IBD (Crohn's/UC): blood/mucus in stool + nocturnal symptoms + weight loss + fever → URGENT GP referral
WORD
SEPSIS
SEP-sis 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈsɛpsɪs/
⚠️ Stress on SEP (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'sep-SIS'. Sepsis = a life-threatening emergency — the body's overwhelming dysregulated response to infection, causing organ damage. The Sepsis Six (hospital protocol): (1) give oxygen, (2) take blood cultures, (3) IV antibiotics, (4) IV fluids, (5) check lactate, (6) monitor urine output. RED FLAGS: fever or hypothermia + tachycardia + rapid breathing + confusion + low BP + mottled/discoloured skin. Immediate emergency services required.
Example 1
The patient's deteriorating condition — high fever, confusion, and rapid breathing — prompted the pharmacist to call emergency services immediately, as she suspected sepsis.
Example 2
Sepsis can develop rapidly from an apparently minor infection — if a patient looks very unwell and has multiple red flag signs, do not delay in calling for emergency help.
📌 Sepsis red flags — OET must know:
fever (>38°C) OR hypothermia (<36°C) | tachycardia (HR >90) | rapid breathing (RR >20) | confusion/altered consciousness | low BP | mottled/pale/blue skin → CALL 111 / EMERGENCY
WORD
SEPTICAEMIA
sep-tih-SEE-mee-uh 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌsɛptɪˈsiːmiə/
⚠️ Stress on SEE (3rd syllable): sep-tih-SEE-mee-uh. Do NOT say 'sep-TIH-see-mee-uh' or 'sep-tih-see-MEE-uh'. Septicaemia = bacteria actively multiplying in the bloodstream — also called 'blood poisoning'. Older clinical term — now largely subsumed under 'sepsis' in modern usage. Still encountered in patient records, OET listening passages, and media. Causes systemic illness: high fever, chills, rigors, rapid deterioration. A medical emergency.
Example 1
The patient had developed septicaemia following a dental procedure and was admitted for intravenous antibiotics and close monitoring.
Example 2
Septicaemia — blood poisoning — can develop very quickly from an untreated infection, which is why it's important to seek help early if you feel suddenly very unwell.
📌 Sepsis terminology progression — OET clarity:
BACTERAEMIA → bacteria present in blood (may be transient, asymptomatic) | SEPTICAEMIA → bacteria multiplying in blood, causing illness | SEPSIS → body's dysregulated response → organ damage | SEPTIC SHOCK → sepsis + severe BP drop → life-threatening
WORD
BACTERAEMIA
bak-tuh-REE-mee-uh 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌbæktəˈriːmiə/
⚠️ Stress on REE (3rd syllable): bak-tuh-REE-mee-uh. Do NOT say 'bak-TER-ee-mee-uh' or 'BAK-tuh-ree-mee-uh'. Bacteraemia = the presence of bacteria in the bloodstream — not necessarily causing symptoms. Transient bacteraemia occurs after dental procedures, bowel investigations, or catheterisation. In most healthy people, the immune system clears it rapidly. However, in patients with prosthetic heart valves or certain cardiac conditions, prophylactic antibiotics are given to prevent infective endocarditis.
Example 1
Transient bacteraemia can occur after invasive dental procedures — patients with prosthetic heart valves are given prophylactic antibiotics beforehand to prevent infective endocarditis.
Example 2
Bacteraemia differs from septicaemia in that bacteria are present in the blood but not necessarily multiplying to cause systemic illness.

FEELING GASSY / MY STOMACH FEELS REALLY GASSY
FEE-ling GA-see 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈfiːlɪŋ ˈɡæsi/
⚠️ 'Gassy' = informal for flatulence or bloating. Clinical: flatulence, abdominal distension. OET rephrase: 'So you're experiencing a lot of bloating or wind?' Common with IBS, lactose intolerance, metformin. Explore: timing (after meals?), associated bowel changes, duration.
Example 1
The patient said her stomach felt really gassy — the pharmacist asked whether it was worse after eating and whether she had noticed any change in her bowel habits.

Feeling gassy is a common side effect when starting metformin — taking it with food and starting on a low dose usually helps.

gassy → flatulence/bloating | runny tummy → diarrhoea | tummy pain → abdominal pain | off my food → reduced appetite

Clinical: 'variant presentation', 'genetic variant', 'variant strain'. MBA: 'product variant'. OET listening: may describe an unusual or atypical form of a condition.

The project manager outlined the key deliverables for the quarter — a market analysis, a stakeholder report, and a revised budget forecast.

COMPROMISED
KOM-pruh-myzd 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈkɒmprəmaɪzd/
⚠️ Stress on KOM (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'kom-PRUH-myzd'. 'Compromised' = weakened, damaged, or put at risk. Clinical uses: 'compromised immune system' (immunocompromised), 'compromised airway' (obstructed), 'compromised skin integrity'. OET: patients with compromised immunity should avoid live vaccines and take extra infection precautions.
Example 1
Patients with a compromised immune system — such as those on immunosuppressants or chemotherapy — are at significantly higher risk of infection.

The patient's skin integrity was compromised by the long-term steroid use — thin, fragile skin that broke easily.

avoid live vaccines | extra infection precautions | report any signs of infection immediately | regular monitoring | sick day rules apply
WORD
KNOWN UNKNOWNS (RISK MANAGEMENT)
nohn un-NOHNZ 3 syllables
British (RP): /nəʊn ʌnˈnəʊnz/
⚠️ 'Known unknowns' = risks or knowledge gaps we are AWARE of but don't yet have answers to. Contrast: 'unknown unknowns' = risks we don't even know exist yet. A key concept in risk management and strategic planning. Made famous by Donald Rumsfeld (2002). MBA context: identify known unknowns in a risk register and plan contingencies.
Example 1
The project risk register identified several known unknowns — including regulatory changes and supply disruptions — that required contingency planning.
Example 2
A good risk management strategy addresses both known unknowns (anticipated gaps) and prepares for unknown unknowns through scenario planning.
📌 Rumsfeld matrix — MBA risk framework:
known knowns → facts we know | known unknowns → gaps we're aware of | unknown unknowns → risks we haven't anticipated | unknown knowns → things we know but ignore
WORD

roo-TEEN dih-SKRESH-un 5 syllables
British (RP): /ruːˈtiːn dɪˈskrɛʃən/
⚠️ 'Discretion' — stress on SKRESH: dih-SKRESH-un (3 syllables). Do NOT say 'dis-KREE-shun' or 'DIS-kresh-un'. Routine discretion = the everyday exercise of professional judgement to make context-appropriate decisions. Clinical: 'use your professional discretion', 'handled with discretion' (= confidentially). MBA: 'managers are expected to exercise routine discretion in day-to-day operations'.
Example 1
Pharmacists are expected to exercise routine discretion when counselling patients — adapting communication style to each individual's needs.

The matter was handled with routine discretion — the patient's confidential information was not shared beyond those directly involved in her care.
WORD
SYNKINESIS
sin-kih-NEE-sis 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌsɪŋkɪˈniːsɪs/
⚠️ Stress on NEE (3rd syllable). Do NOT say 'SIN-kih-nee-sis' or 'sin-KIH-nee-sis'. Synkinesis = an involuntary movement accompanying a voluntary one — occurs when nerves regenerate incorrectly after damage. Most commonly seen after Bell's palsy recovery: eye closes when smiling, mouth moves when blinking. Distressing complication — patients may need physiotherapy or Botox for management.
Example 1
The patient had developed synkinesis after Bell's palsy — her eye would close involuntarily whenever she smiled, which she found distressing.
Example 2
Synkinesis occurs when facial nerve fibres regenerate along incorrect pathways — leading to unwanted muscle movements that can persist long after the original palsy resolves.
WORD
DEHYDRATED / DEHYDRATION
dee-hy-DRAY-shun 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌdiːhaɪˈdreɪʃən/
⚠️ Stress on DRAY (3rd syllable). Do NOT say 'DEE-hy-dray-shun' or 'dee-HY-dray-shun'. Dehydration = insufficient fluid in the body — caused by inadequate intake or excessive loss (vomiting, diarrhoea, sweating, fever). Signs: dry mouth, dark urine, dizziness, reduced skin turgor, sunken eyes. OET: assess hydration in any patient with vomiting/diarrhoea/fever — sick day rules apply.
Example 1
The patient appeared dehydrated — her mouth was dry, her urine was dark, and she felt dizzy on standing — the pharmacist advised increased fluid intake and urgent GP review.
Example 2
Dehydration can worsen kidney function and increase the risk of medication toxicity — particularly with lithium, metformin, and NSAIDs.
📌 Dehydration signs — OET assessment:
dry mouth | dark/concentrated urine | dizziness on standing (postural hypotension) | reduced skin turgor | sunken eyes | confusion (severe) | rapid heart rate
WORD

mal-NYOO-trish-un 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌmælnjuːˈtrɪʃən/
⚠️ 'Malnutrition' — stress on NYOO (2nd syllable of 'nutrition'): mal-NYOO-trish-un. Do NOT say 'mal-noo-TRI-shun' or 'MAL-nyoo-trish-un'. Malnutrition = a deficiency (or excess) of nutrients impairing health. Undernutrition signs: weight loss, muscle wasting, fatigue, poor wound healing, oedema. Common in: elderly, cancer patients, restricted diets, prolonged illness.
Example 1
The elderly patient appeared malnourished — significant weight loss and muscle wasting were visible — the pharmacist recommended GP referral and discussed nutritional supplement options.

Malnutrition in hospitalised patients delays wound healing and increases infection risk — nutritional screening should be performed on admission.
📌 Malnutrition signs — OET cluster:
visible weight loss | muscle wasting (sarcopenia) | fatigue | poor wound healing | peripheral oedema | brittle hair/nails | vitamin/mineral deficiencies
WORD
IV FLUIDS (INTRAVENOUS FLUIDS)
in-truh-VEE-nus FLOO-idz 6 syllables
British (RP): /ˌɪntrəˈviːnəs ˈfluːɪdz/
⚠️ 'Intravenous' — stress on VEE: in-truh-VEE-nus (4 syllables). Do NOT say 'IN-truh-vee-nus' (wrong stress). 'IV' = eye-VEE. IV fluids = administered directly into a vein. Common types: normal saline (0.9% NaCl), dextrose (5%), Hartmann's solution (balanced crystalloid). Used for: dehydration, shock, electrolyte imbalance, drug delivery. Patient/carer term: 'drip'.

Example 2
IV fluids can help restore hydration quickly when oral intake is not possible — the patient was monitored closely for signs of fluid overload.
📌 Common IV fluid types — OET/clinical reference:
normal saline (0.9% NaCl) → volume replacement | dextrose 5% → hydration + energy | Hartmann's → balanced electrolyte replacement | colloids (e.g. albumin) → plasma expansion

preh-DOM-ih-nunt-lee 5 syllables

A predominantly liquid diet = mainly soups, smoothies, juices — unable to tolerate solid food. Clinically relevant in: dysphagia (swallowing difficulty), severe nausea, post-surgical recovery, oesophageal conditions. OET: 'Can you tell me what you've been able to eat and drink?' CARBONATED DRINKS / FIZZY DRINKS
KAR-buh-nay-tid DRINKS 5 syllables

⚠️ 'Carbonated' — stress on KAR (1st syllable): KAR-buh-nay-tid (4 syllables). Do NOT say 'car-BON-ay-tid'. 'Fizzy drinks' = common British patient term.
