DECOMPOSE (MEDICATION / DRUG STABILITY)
dee-kum-POHZ 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˌdiːkəmˈpəʊz/
⚠️ Stress on POHZ (3rd syllable). Do NOT say 'DEE-kom-pohz' or 'dee-KOM-pohz'. In pharmacy: 'the medication decomposes if exposed to heat, light, or moisture' = it breaks down chemically and loses potency or becomes toxic. OET counselling: always advise on correct storage to prevent decomposition — refrigerate if required, keep in original packaging, away from light/heat.
Example 1
The pharmacist explained that the medication could decompose if stored in a warm or humid environment — she advised keeping it in a cool, dry place away from direct sunlight.
Example 2
Some medications — such as aspirin — decompose in the presence of moisture, which is why they should be kept in an airtight container.
WORD
DISPUTE (NOUN VS VERB — STRESS SHIFT)
noun: DIS-pyoot | verb: dih-SPYOOT 2 syllables each
British (RP): noun /ˈdɪspjuːt/ | verb /dɪˈspjuːt/
⚠️ STRESS SHIFT: noun = DIS-pyoot (stress on 1st syllable) | verb = dih-SPYOOT (stress on 2nd syllable). This is the standard British noun/verb stress shift pattern — also seen in: PER-mit (n) vs per-MIT (v), RE-cord (n) vs re-CORD (v). In clinical settings: 'to dispute a diagnosis' = to challenge or question it. MBA: 'a commercial dispute', 'dispute resolution'.
Example 1
The patient wished to dispute the diagnosis — she felt her symptoms had not been fully investigated — the pharmacist suggested she request a second opinion.
Example 2
The commercial dispute between the two companies was referred to an independent arbitrator for resolution.
📌 Noun/verb stress shift — British RP rule:
DIS-pute (n) / dis-PUTE (v) | PER-mit (n) / per-MIT (v) | RE-cord (n) / re-CORD (v) | PRO-test (n) / pro-TEST (v) | IN-crease (n) / in-CREASE (v)
WORD
MINIMISE
MIN-ih-myz 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈmɪnɪmaɪz/
⚠️ Stress on MIN (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'min-IH-myz' or 'MIN-ih-myze' with exaggerated final z. 'Minimise' = to reduce to the smallest possible amount OR to play down the significance of something. OET communication WARNING: 'minimising' a patient's concerns (e.g. 'it's nothing to worry about') is a red flag in OET communication scoring — always validate concerns before offering reassurance.
Example 1
The pharmacist was careful not to minimise the patient's concerns — she acknowledged them fully before explaining the management plan.
Example 2
Taking the medication with food can help to minimise nausea and stomach upset during the first few weeks of treatment.
📌 OET communication — never minimise patient concerns:
❌ 'It's nothing to worry about' | ❌ 'Lots of people have this' (without validation) | ✅ 'I can understand why that worries you' | ✅ 'Your concern is completely valid — let me explain what we can do'
WORD
IMPLICATIONS
im-plih-KAY-shunz 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌɪmplɪˈkeɪʃənz/
⚠️ Stress on KAY (3rd syllable). Do NOT say 'IM-plih-kay-shunz' or 'im-PLI-kay-shunz'. Implications = the possible consequences or effects of something — 'the implications of this diagnosis', 'the clinical implications of non-adherence', 'financial implications'. OET: 'What are the implications for your daily life?' MBA: 'strategic implications', 'implications for the business model'.
Example 1
The pharmacist explained the implications of stopping the medication suddenly — including withdrawal symptoms and loss of disease control.
Example 2
The board discussed the financial implications of the proposed merger before making a final decision.
WORD
INTEGRATED PROJECT
IN-tih-gray-tid PROJ-ekt 6 syllables
British (RP): /ˈɪntɪɡreɪtɪd ˈprɒdʒɛkt/
⚠️ 'Integrated' — stress on IN: IN-tih-gray-tid (4 syllables). 'Project' — stress on PROJ: PROJ-ekt (2 syllables). An integrated project = one that combines multiple components, departments, or workstreams into a unified whole. Requires cross-functional collaboration. Contrast with siloed projects (each team works independently). Key MBA concept.
Example 1
The MBA cohort delivered an integrated project combining financial analysis, marketing strategy, and operational planning into a single proposal.
Example 2
An integrated project approach ensures that all workstreams align with the overall strategic objective rather than operating in isolation.
WORD
HANDOVER SYSTEM
HAN-doh-vur SIS-tum 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˈhændəʊvər ˈsɪstəm/
⚠️ 'Handover' — stress on HAN: HAN-doh-vur (3 syllables). 'System' — stress on SIS: SIS-tum (2 syllables). A handover system = a structured process for transferring responsibility and information from one person or team to another. Critical in healthcare — poor handovers cause medication errors and patient harm. Pharmacists play a key role in medication reconciliation during handovers.
Example 1
The hospital implemented a new electronic handover system to ensure critical patient information — including medication changes — was accurately communicated between shifts.
Example 2
A structured handover system using tools like SBAR (Situation, Background, Assessment, Recommendation) reduces the risk of important information being lost between shifts.
📌 SBAR — clinical handover framework:
S = Situation (what's happening) | B = Background (relevant history) | A = Assessment (your clinical judgement) | R = Recommendation (what you think should happen next)
WORD
RESOURCE LEVELLING
REE-sors LEV-ul-ing 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˈriːsɔːs ˈlɛvəlɪŋ/
⚠️ 'Levelling' — stress on LEV: LEV-ul-ing (3 syllables). 'Resource' — stress on REE: REE-sors (2 syllables — British pronunciation). Do NOT say 'reh-SORS' (American). Resource levelling = a project management technique that adjusts the schedule to resolve resource conflicts — ensuring no person or resource is over- or under-allocated. May extend project timeline. Key MBA/PMP concept.
Example 1
The project manager used resource levelling to redistribute the team's workload — ensuring no one was overloaded while keeping the project on schedule.
Example 2
Resource levelling is particularly important in healthcare settings where staff availability is limited and patient demand is variable.
WORD
INTERMITTENTLY
in-tur-MIT-unt-lee 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˌɪntəˈmɪtəntli/
⚠️ Stress on MIT (3rd syllable). Do NOT say 'IN-tur-mit-unt-lee' or 'in-tur-mit-UNT-lee'. Intermittently = occurring at irregular intervals — stopping and starting, not continuous. Clinical: 'pain occurs intermittently', 'patient takes medication intermittently'. Contrast: constantly (all the time) | consistently (reliably regular) | irregularly (unpredictably).
Example 1
The patient said the pain occurred intermittently — coming on for about 20 minutes at a time before disappearing completely, then returning later.
Example 2
Taking medication intermittently rather than as prescribed can lead to subtherapeutic levels and reduced effectiveness.
📌 Frequency adverbs — OET clinical distinction:
constantly → all the time, unceasing | intermittently → stops and starts, irregular intervals | consistently → reliably, regularly | irregularly → no set pattern, unpredictably
WORD
IRREGULARLY
ih-REG-yoo-lur-lee 5 syllables
British (RP): /ɪˈreɡjʊləli/
⚠️ Stress on REG (2nd syllable). Do NOT say 'IR-reg-yoo-lur-lee' or 'ih-reg-YOO-lur-lee'. Irregularly = without a regular pattern — unpredictably, erratically. Clinical: 'the patient takes her medication irregularly' = missing doses unpredictably. OET: relevant in adherence counselling — explore WHY irregular (forgetfulness, side effects, cost, beliefs) before advising.
Example 1
The patient admitted she had been taking her medication irregularly — sometimes twice a day, sometimes not at all — the pharmacist discussed the importance of consistent dosing.
Example 2
An irregular heartbeat — or heart beating irregularly — should always be investigated by a doctor, even if it causes no symptoms.
WORD
CONSTANTLY VS CONSISTENTLY
KON-stunt-lee | kun-SIS-tunt-lee 4 / 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈkɒnstəntli/ | /kənˈsɪstəntli/
⚠️ 'Constantly' — stress on KON. 'Consistently' — stress on SIS: kun-SIS-tunt-lee (4 syllables). These are frequently confused. CONSTANTLY = all the time, without stopping (implies continuous, unceasing presence). CONSISTENTLY = regularly and reliably, following a pattern (implies dependability and regularity). Clinical: 'constantly in pain' ≠ 'consistently takes medication every morning'. MBA: 'consistently delivering results' = reliably, not 'working constantly'.
Example 1
The pharmacist explained: 'The goal is not to constantly think about your medication, but to consistently take it at the same time each day.'
Example 2
She described being constantly in pain — it never let up — which the pharmacist distinguished from pain that occurs consistently at a particular time of day.
📌 Constantly vs Consistently — quick rule:
CONSTANTLY → unceasing, all the time ('the pain is constant') | CONSISTENTLY → reliably regular ('take it consistently every morning') | ❌ 'consistently in pain' sounds odd — use 'constantly' | ❌ 'constantly takes medication' implies obsessive — use 'consistently'
WORD
ACID REFLUX / GORD (GASTRO-OESOPHAGEAL REFLUX DISEASE)
gas-troh-ih-sof-uh-JEE-ul reh-FLUKS dih-ZEEZ oesophageal = 6 syllables
British (RP): /ˌɡæstrəʊ ɪˌsɒfəˈdʒiːəl ˈriːflʌks dɪˈziːz/
⚠️ 'Oesophageal' — stress on JEE: ih-sof-uh-JEE-ul (5 syllables). Do NOT say 'ee-SO-fuh-jee-ul' or 'oh-sof-uh-GEE-ul'. 'Acid reflux' = patient term. 'GORD' = clinical diagnosis (British/NZ/AUS); 'GERD' = American. Acid reflux = stomach acid flowing back into the oesophagus → heartburn, regurgitation, sour taste. Common triggers: large meals, fatty/spicy food, alcohol, caffeine, carbonated drinks, lying down after eating, obesity. OET: 'acid reflux' in speaking; GORD in writing.
Example 1
The patient complained of a burning sensation in her chest after meals — the pharmacist explained this was likely acid reflux and advised her to avoid large meals, carbonated drinks, and lying down within two hours of eating.
Example 2
GORD is managed with lifestyle changes alongside medication — PPIs such as omeprazole are first-line, with H2 receptor blockers as an alternative for milder symptoms.
📌 GORD lifestyle advice — OET counselling checklist:
avoid triggers: fatty/spicy food, caffeine, alcohol, carbonated drinks | eat smaller meals | don't lie down within 2 hours of eating | elevate head of bed | lose weight if overweight | stop smoking
WORD
FULL BLOOD COUNT (FBC) — NOT 'BLOOD COUNT'
ful blud KOWNT 3 syllables
British (RP): /fʊl blʌd kaʊnt/
⚠️ Always say 'FULL blood count' — NOT 'blood count' alone (not standard British/NZ clinical usage). FBC = a standard blood test measuring: red blood cells (RBC), white blood cells (WBC), haemoglobin (Hb), haematocrit, and platelets. Used to detect: anaemia, infection, clotting disorders, haematological malignancies. British/NZ/AUS: FBC | American: CBC (complete blood count). OET: use FBC, not CBC, in British/NZ contexts.
Example 1
The GP arranged a full blood count to investigate the patient's fatigue and pallor — the pharmacist explained it would check for anaemia and signs of infection.
Example 2
A full blood count is one of the most commonly ordered blood tests — it gives a broad overview of the body's blood cell health.
📌 FBC components — OET clinical reference:
Hb (haemoglobin) → anaemia | WBC → infection/inflammation/malignancy | Platelets → clotting | MCV → type of anaemia (low = iron deficiency; high = B12/folate deficiency) | Neutrophils → bacterial infection | Lymphocytes → viral infection
WORD
H2 RECEPTOR BLOCKERS (H2 ANTAGONISTS)
aych-TWO reh-SEP-tur BLOK-urz 6 syllables
British (RP): /eɪtʃ tuː rɪˈsɛptər ˈblɒkərz/
⚠️ 'Receptor' — stress on SEP: reh-SEP-tur (3 syllables). Do NOT say 'REE-sep-tur' or 'reh-sep-TOR'. H2 receptor blockers = medications that reduce stomach acid by blocking histamine H2 receptors on gastric parietal cells. Examples: famotidine, cimetidine (ranitidine withdrawn 2020). Less potent than PPIs but faster-acting. OET KEY DISTINCTION: H2 blockers → faster onset, shorter duration | PPIs → slower onset, longer duration, more potent.
Example 1
The pharmacist explained that H2 receptor blockers reduce stomach acid production by blocking histamine — they work more quickly than PPIs but for a shorter period.
Example 2
H2 receptor blockers such as famotidine can be used for mild to moderate acid reflux — for more severe GORD, a PPI like omeprazole is usually preferred.
📌 Acid suppression drugs — OET comparison:
H2 blockers (famotidine, cimetidine) → faster onset, shorter duration, milder GORD | PPIs (omeprazole, lansoprazole, pantoprazole) → slower onset (take before food), longer duration, more potent → first-line for GORD | Antacids (Gaviscon, Rennie) → immediate relief, short duration → symptom relief only
WORD
BARIUM SWALLOW
BAIR-ee-um SWOL-oh 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˈbɛəriəm ˈswɒləʊ/
⚠️ 'Barium' — stress on BAIR: BAIR-ee-um (3 syllables). 'Swallow' — stress on SWOL: SWOL-oh (2 syllables). Do NOT say 'bar-EE-um' or 'SWOL-ow' (swallow has a schwa ending: SWOL-oh). Barium swallow = patient drinks barium contrast liquid → X-rays visualise oesophagus, stomach, upper GI tract. Used for: dysphagia, GORD, hiatus hernia, strictures, motility disorders. OET patient counselling: fast beforehand, stools may appear white for 1–2 days (normal).
Example 1
The patient was scheduled for a barium swallow to investigate her difficulty swallowing — the pharmacist explained she would need to fast beforehand and that her stools might appear white for a day or two after.
Example 2
A barium swallow can reveal structural abnormalities such as a hiatus hernia or oesophageal stricture — it provides a dynamic picture of swallowing function.
📌 Upper GI investigations — OET reference:
barium swallow → oesophagus/upper GI structure + motility | barium meal → stomach + duodenum | barium enema → large bowel | endoscopy (OGD) → direct visualisation of oesophagus, stomach, duodenum | colonoscopy → large bowel direct view
WORD
BURPING / BELCHING / ERUCTATION
BUR-ping | BEL-ching | ih-ruk-TAY-shun 2 / 2 / 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈbɜːpɪŋ/ | /ˈbɛltʃɪŋ/ | /ɪˌrʌkˈteɪʃən/
⚠️ 'Eructation' — stress on TAY: ih-ruk-TAY-shun (4 syllables). Do NOT say 'ee-ruk-TAY-shun' or 'ih-RUK-tay-shun'. Register ladder: burping (patient/informal) → belching (semi-clinical) → eructation (formal/written). Use 'burping' or 'belching' when talking to patients. Write 'eructation' in clinical notes only. Excessive burping/belching = symptom of acid reflux, aerophagia (swallowing air), H. pylori, gastroparesis, functional dyspepsia.
Example 1
The patient mentioned she had been burping frequently after meals — the pharmacist asked whether she also experienced heartburn or a sour taste in her mouth, as this could suggest acid reflux.
Example 2
Excessive belching, particularly when associated with bloating and early satiety, may indicate delayed gastric emptying — GP referral is recommended.
📌 Register ladder — use the right word for the audience:
Patient/speaking: burping | Semi-clinical/OET speaking: belching | Formal/written notes: eructation | Similarly: farting → passing wind → flatulence/flatus | being sick → vomiting → emesis
WORD
COLITIS
koh-LY-tis 3 syllables
British (RP): /kəˈlaɪtɪs/
⚠️ Stress on LY (2nd syllable). Do NOT say 'KOH-lih-tis' or 'kol-IH-tis'. Colitis = inflammation of the colon. Types: ulcerative colitis (UC — chronic IBD, bloody diarrhoea, managed long-term), infectious colitis (C. difficile, salmonella — acute, often self-limiting), ischaemic colitis (poor blood supply), microscopic colitis. OET: UC is the most clinically relevant for pharmacy — requires consistent medication even in remission. Key drugs: mesalazine, azathioprine, biologics.
Example 1
The patient had been diagnosed with ulcerative colitis — the pharmacist counselled her on taking mesalazine consistently, even during symptom-free periods, to maintain remission.
Example 2
Colitis caused by Clostridioides difficile (C. diff) typically occurs after antibiotic use — the pharmacist asked whether the patient had taken antibiotics recently and advised her to see her GP urgently.
📌 Colitis types — OET distinction:
Ulcerative colitis (UC) → chronic IBD, bloody diarrhoea + mucus, starts in rectum, spreads proximally | Crohn's → can affect any part of GI tract, skip lesions, transmural | Infectious colitis → acute, C. diff / Salmonella / Campylobacter | Ischaemic colitis → elderly, vascular disease
WORD
TEXTURE OF FOOD / FOOD TEXTURE MODIFICATION
TEKS-chur uv FOOD 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈtɛkstʃər əv fuːd/
⚠️ 'Texture' — stress on TEKS: TEKS-chur (2 syllables). Do NOT say 'TEK-shur' (dropping the /t/) or 'tex-CHUR'. Food texture modification = altering food consistency to make swallowing safer — used in dysphagia management. Classified by IDDSI (International Dysphagia Diet Standardisation Initiative): Level 0 (thin liquid) → Level 7 (regular). Patient expressions: 'I can't manage solid food', 'lumpy foods make me choke'. OET: refer to speech and language therapist (SLT).
Example 1
The patient said she was struggling with the texture of solid food — lumpy foods made her cough and choke — the pharmacist recommended an urgent referral to a speech and language therapist.
Example 2
Food texture modification is a key part of dysphagia management — the IDDSI framework provides a standardised way to classify appropriate food and drink consistencies.
📌 IDDSI food texture levels — OET/dysphagia reference:
Level 0: thin liquid | Level 1: slightly thick | Level 2: mildly thick | Level 3: liquidised | Level 4: puréed | Level 5: minced & moist | Level 6: soft & bite-sized | Level 7: regular
WORD
BELL'S PALSY
BELZ PAWL-zee 3 syllables
British (RP): /bɛlz ˈpɔːlzi/
⚠️ 'Palsy' — stress on PAWL: PAWL-zee (2 syllables). Do NOT say 'PAL-see' or 'PAWL-see'. Bell's palsy = sudden-onset unilateral facial weakness from inflammation of the facial nerve (CN VII) — idiopathic, associated with HSV. Key features: drooping on one side, inability to close eye, drooling, loss of taste. OET treatment: high-dose prednisolone within 72 hours + eye protection (drops, patch). Most recover fully within 3–6 months. Complication: synkinesis (see separate card).
Example 1
The patient had been diagnosed with Bell's palsy — the pharmacist counselled her on completing the full prednisolone course and using eye drops and a patch to protect her cornea as she could not fully close her eye.
Example 2
Bell's palsy treatment is most effective when prednisolone is started within 72 hours of symptom onset — the pharmacist emphasised the importance of not stopping the tablets early.
📌 Bell's palsy — OET counselling checklist:
prednisolone: start within 72 hours, complete full course | eye protection: lubricating drops (day) + ointment (night) + eye patch | tape eye shut at night if needed | avoid rubbing eye | most recover fully 3–6 months | red flag: if not improving by 3 weeks → GP review
WORD
ACHALASIA
ak-uh-LAY-zhuh 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˌækəˈleɪʒə/
⚠️ Stress on LAY (3rd syllable). Do NOT say 'ak-HAL-uh-see-uh' or 'ah-kuh-LAY-see-uh'. Achalasia = a rare oesophageal motility disorder — the lower oesophageal sphincter fails to relax and peristalsis is absent. Key OET distinction from GORD: achalasia causes dysphagia for BOTH solids AND liquids early on (GORD causes heartburn; mechanical strictures cause solids-first dysphagia). Symptoms: progressive dysphagia, regurgitation of undigested food, chest pain, weight loss. Diagnosed by barium swallow + manometry.
Example 1
The patient described progressive difficulty swallowing — initially solids, now liquids too — the pharmacist recognised this pattern and recommended urgent GP referral for investigation.
Example 2
Achalasia is distinguished from other swallowing disorders by the combination of dysphagia to both solids and liquids, regurgitation of undigested food, and the absence of heartburn.
📌 Dysphagia differential — OET red flag distinction:
Solids only first, then liquids → mechanical obstruction (stricture, cancer) — URGENT referral | Solids AND liquids from start → motility disorder (achalasia) | Solids AND liquids + neurological signs → neurological cause (stroke, MND) | All of above → URGENT GP referral
WORD
THIS STUDY WAS CONDUCTED USING AGGREGATED POPULATION DATA
kon-DUK-tid | ag-REG-ay-tid conducted = 3 | aggregated = 4
British (RP): /kənˈdʌktɪd/ | /æˈɡreɡeɪtɪd/
⚠️ 'Conducted' — stress on DUK: kon-DUK-tid. 'Aggregated' — stress on REG: ag-REG-ay-tid. CORRECT: 'This study was conducted using...' ✅ | 'This study was carried out using...' ✅ | 'This study is made by using...' ❌ (wrong tense + unnatural phrasing). 'Aggregated population data' = data combined from a large group — used in epidemiology and public health research. Contrast with individual-level data. Use 'conducted' in formal academic writing — it is the most precise and widely accepted verb.
Example 1
This study was conducted using aggregated population data from the New Zealand Health Survey, providing a broad overview of medication adherence trends.
Example 2
While this study was conducted using aggregated population data, future research should examine individual-level variation to better understand disparities across subgroups.
📌 Academic methodology verbs — ranked by formality:
✅ was conducted (most formal, preferred) | ✅ was carried out | ✅ was undertaken | ⚠️ was performed (more clinical/lab context) | ❌ was made | ❌ is made by using
WORD
PUBLISHED ARTICLE / ACADEMIC PAPER / RESEARCH PAPER
PUB-lisht AR-tih-kul | ak-uh-DEM-ik PAY-pur 5 / 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˈpʌblɪʃt ˈɑːtɪkəl/ | /ˌækəˈdɛmɪk ˈpeɪpər/
⚠️ 'Academic' — stress on DEM: ak-uh-DEM-ik (4 syllables). Do NOT say 'AK-uh-dem-ik'. KEY DISTINCTIONS: published article/paper = peer-reviewed, formally published in a journal (출판된 학술 논문) | academic paper = scholarly writing, may or may not be peer-reviewed (학술 논문) | research paper = reports original research findings (연구 논문). OET writing: cite as 'According to a published research paper...' or 'As referenced in a published article...'
Example 1
According to a published research paper, patients who receive pharmacist-led counselling show significantly improved adherence to long-term medication regimens.
Example 2
As a reference, I have attached a published academic paper outlining the current evidence base for this treatment approach.
📌 Academic citation phrases — OET writing:
'According to a research paper by [author]...' | 'As referenced in a published article...' | 'A recent study conducted by... found that...' | 'As a reference, I have attached...' | 'For your information, [background detail]...'
WORD
RIGOUR / CREDIBILITY (RESEARCH QUALITY)
RIG-ur | kred-ih-BIL-ih-tee 2 / 5 syllables
British (RP): /ˈrɪɡər/ | /ˌkrɛdɪˈbɪlɪti/
⚠️ 'Rigour' — stress on RIG. British spelling: rigour ✅ | American: rigor. Do NOT confuse with 'rigor mortis' (post-mortem stiffening) — same pronunciation, different context. 'Credibility' — stress on BIL: kred-ih-BIL-ih-tee (5 syllables). RIGOUR = thoroughness and precision of methodology. CREDIBILITY = trustworthiness of findings. In qualitative research: credibility replaces 'validity' as the key quality criterion.
Example 1
The study demonstrated strong methodological rigour — the sample size was appropriate, methods were transparent, and limitations were clearly acknowledged.
Example 2
The credibility of the qualitative findings was strengthened by member-checking — participants confirmed that the researcher's interpretations accurately reflected their experiences.
📌 Research quality criteria — qualitative vs quantitative:
Quantitative: validity | reliability | generalisability | objectivity || Qualitative: credibility | transferability | dependability | confirmability | reflexivity
WORD
REFLEXIVITY STATEMENT (연구자 성찰성 진술)
reh-flek-SIV-ih-tee 5 syllables
British (RP): /rɪˌflɛkˈsɪvɪti/
⚠️ 'Reflexivity' — stress on SIV: reh-flek-SIV-ih-tee (5 syllables). Do NOT say 'reh-FLEK-sih-vih-tee'. A reflexivity statement = a declaration by the researcher of their own background, positionality, and potential biases — and how these may have influenced the research (연구자 성찰성 진술). Essential in qualitative research. Strengthens credibility and transparency. Usually placed in the Methods section. Distinct from 'reflection' — reflexivity is ongoing, systematic, and embedded in the research process.
Example 1
The researcher included a reflexivity statement acknowledging her experience as a practising pharmacist — she recognised this may have influenced her interpretation of the patient interview data.
Example 2
A strong reflexivity statement does not eliminate bias — it makes the researcher's positionality transparent so readers can assess how it may have shaped the findings.
📌 Reflexivity statement — what to include:
Who you are (role, background) | What assumptions you bring | How your position may influence data collection/interpretation | What steps you took to minimise bias | Why transparency matters for this study
WORD
COLLECTIVE BENEFIT / DISAGGREGATION / RECIPROCITY
kuh-LEK-tiv BEN-ih-fit | dis-ag-reh-GAY-shun | reh-SIP-roh-sih-tee 5 / 5 / 5 syllables
British (RP): /kəˈlɛktɪv ˈbɛnɪfɪt/ | /dɪsˌæɡrɪˈɡeɪʃən/ | /rɪˈsɪprəsɪti/
⚠️ Three key research ethics / Indigenous data sovereignty principles. COLLECTIVE BENEFIT (집단적 이익) — stress on LEK and BEN: research must benefit the community studied, not just the researcher. DISAGGREGATION — stress on GAY: dis-ag-reh-GAY-shun — breaking down combined data into subgroups to reveal disparities (e.g. by ethnicity). RECIPROCITY — stress on SIP: reh-SIP-roh-sih-tee — mutual exchange; researchers must give back to communities. All three are part of the CARE Principles for Indigenous Data Governance.
Example 1
The research team upheld the principles of collective benefit and reciprocity — findings were shared back with the community in accessible formats to inform local health decisions.
Example 2
Disaggregation of the data by ethnicity revealed significant disparities that were hidden in the aggregated figures — this underscored the importance of not relying solely on population-level data.
📌 CARE Principles — Indigenous Data Governance:
C = Collective Benefit (집단적 이익) | A = Authority to Control | R = Responsibility | E = Ethics
WORD
DESCRIPTION / DISAGGREGATION (AS A PAIR)
dih-SKRIP-shun | dis-ag-reh-GAY-shun 3 / 5 syllables
British (RP): /dɪˈskrɪpʃən/ | /dɪsˌæɡrɪˈɡeɪʃən/
⚠️ 'Description' — stress on SKRIP (2nd syllable). 'Disaggregation' — stress on GAY (4th syllable). DESCRIPTION = a factual account or summary — 단순 기술, 작성 (describing what is observed without interpretation). DISAGGREGATION = breaking aggregated data into specific subgroups to reveal hidden patterns or disparities. These often appear together: first describe the overall data, then disaggregate to reveal subgroup differences.
Example 1
A simple description of the overall data showed similar outcomes — however, disaggregation by ethnicity revealed significant disparities in medication access.
Example 2
The report moved from description to disaggregation — first summarising the population-level findings, then breaking them down by age group and socioeconomic status.
WORD
DESPITE / IN SPITE OF — CONCESSIVE PREPOSITIONS
dih-SPYT | in SPYT uv 2 / 4 syllables
British (RP): /dɪˈspaɪt/ | /ɪn ˈspaɪt əv/
⚠️ 'Despite' — stress on SPYT: dih-SPYT (2 syllables). 'In spite of' — stress on SPYT. Both are followed by a NOUN or NOUN PHRASE (not a clause). COMMON ERROR: 'Despite it is raining' ❌ | 'Despite the rain' ✅ | 'Despite the fact that it is raining' ✅. OET writing: 'Despite her reluctance, she agreed to the referral' ✅. 'In spite of this' = nevertheless — used to introduce a contrasting point. DESPITE is slightly more formal; IN SPITE OF is equally correct but slightly more emphatic.
Example 1
Despite the language barrier, the patient understood the key counselling points clearly after the pharmacist used simple diagrams and plain language.
Example 2
In spite of the difficulties she faced as a new immigrant, she demonstrated strong adherence to her medication regimen throughout the treatment period.
📌 Concessive connectors — OET writing variety:
Despite + noun/gerund | In spite of + noun/gerund | Although + clause | Even though + clause | Nevertheless (sentence connector) | However (sentence connector) | Notwithstanding + noun (formal)
WORD
ACCOUNTABILITY
uh-kown-tuh-BIL-ih-tee 6 syllables
British (RP): /əˌkaʊntəˈbɪlɪti/
⚠️ Stress on BIL (4th syllable): uh-kown-tuh-BIL-ih-tee. Do NOT say 'uh-KOWN-tuh-bil-ih-tee' or 'a-count-uh-BIL-ih-tee'. Accountability = the obligation to accept responsibility and answer to others for outcomes — distinct from responsibility (the duty to act beforehand). Clinical: 'professional accountability' — pharmacists are accountable to patients, employer, and regulatory body (Pharmacy Council NZ). MBA: 'corporate accountability', 'accountability framework'.
Example 1
Professional accountability requires pharmacists to document counselling conversations accurately — this provides a clear record if the patient's care is ever reviewed.
Example 2
The governance framework introduced clear accountability structures — each workstream had a named owner responsible for reporting outcomes to the board.
📌 Responsibility vs Accountability — key distinction:
RESPONSIBILITY = the duty to act / perform a task (before or during) | ACCOUNTABILITY = answering for the outcome (after the fact) | Example: the pharmacist is RESPONSIBLE for counselling | the pharmacist is ACCOUNTABLE if counselling was inadequate
WORD
TWOFOLD / THREEFOLD / FOURFOLD
TOO-fold | THREE-fold | FOR-fold 2 syllables each
British (RP): /ˈtuːfəʊld/ | /ˈθriːfəʊld/ | /ˈfɔːfəʊld/
⚠️ All stress on the number (1st syllable): TOO-fold, THREE-fold, FOR-fold. Do NOT say 'too-FOLD'. Twofold = twice as great (×2) = 100% increase. Threefold = three times (×3) = 200% increase. Fourfold = four times (×4). Used in research and MBA to describe relative magnitudes. Contrast: 'twofold increase' (×2) vs 'two percent increase' (very small). OET/MBA: more formal and precise than 'doubled' or 'tripled'.
Example 1
Patients who received pharmacist counselling showed a twofold improvement in adherence rates compared to those who received standard care alone.
Example 2
The intervention resulted in a threefold reduction in hospital readmissions over the 12-month follow-up period.
📌 Multiplier vocabulary — research/MBA:
twofold (×2) | threefold (×3) | fourfold (×4) | fivefold (×5) | manifold (many times) | marginal (very small) | substantial (large but unspecified)
WORD
POINTING TOWARD / SIDE BY SIDE
POYNT-ing tuh-WORD | SYD by SYD 4 / 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈpɔɪntɪŋ təˈwɔːd/ | /saɪd baɪ saɪd/
⚠️ 'Toward' (British: towards) — stress on WORD: tuh-WORD. 'Side by side' — each 'side' rhymes with 'ride'. POINTING TOWARD = indicating or suggesting (hedging language — softer than 'proves'). Appropriate for preliminary or correlational findings. SIDE BY SIDE = placed next to each other for direct comparison ('a side-by-side analysis'). Both used in research writing and MBA presentations.
Example 1
The emerging data is pointing toward a significant association between non-adherence and readmission — though longitudinal research is needed to confirm this.
Example 2
The consultant presented a side-by-side comparison of the two merger scenarios — highlighting differences in projected ROI, integration risk, and time to value.
📌 Academic hedging language — strength of evidence:
Strong: demonstrates | shows | proves | establishes || Moderate: suggests | indicates | supports || Weak/preliminary: points toward | is consistent with | raises the possibility that | may suggest
WORD
COMPLEMENTARITY
kom-pleh-men-TAIR-ih-tee 6 syllables
British (RP): /ˌkɒmplɪmenˈtærɪti/
⚠️ Stress on TAIR (4th syllable). Do NOT say 'kom-PLEH-men-tair-ih-tee' or 'kom-pleh-MEN-tair-ih-tee'. Distinct from 'complementary' (adjective, 5 syllables: kom-pleh-MEN-tuh-ree). Complementarity = the quality of two things enhancing or completing each other. Research: 'complementarity of methods'. MBA: 'complementarity of team skills'. OET writing: 'the complementarity of pharmacist and GP roles'.
Example 1
The study highlighted the complementarity of pharmacist-led counselling and GP medical management — each role enhanced the effectiveness of the other.
Example 2
The complementarity of qualitative and quantitative methods allowed the researchers to develop a richer and more nuanced understanding of patient adherence behaviour.
📌 Complementarity vs Complementary — don't confuse:
complementary (adj, 5 syl) → kom-pleh-MEN-tuh-ree = things that complement each other | complementarity (noun, 6 syl) → kom-pleh-men-TAIR-ih-tee = the state or quality of being complementary | ≠ complimentary (= free of charge / expressing praise)
WORD
CARRY OUT
KA-ree owt 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈkæri aʊt/
⚠️ 'Carry' — stress on KA: KA-ree. Phrasal verb meaning to perform, execute, or complete a task. OET/academic register note: 'carry out' is natural and correct in OET speaking and professional letters. In formal academic writing, prefer 'conduct' or 'undertake' for slightly higher register. All three are acceptable — choose based on context. Examples: 'carry out a biopsy', 'carry out an assessment', 'carry out a medication review'.
Example 1
The pharmacist carried out a full medication review to identify potential interactions before the patient's scheduled surgery.
Example 2
The study was carried out over a 12-month period, involving 200 participants from three community pharmacies.
📌 Register ladder — 'carry out' vs alternatives:
carry out → natural, correct, slightly informal | conduct → formal, academic preferred | undertake → formal, implies commitment | perform → clinical/procedural context | execute → business/project management
WORD
DIALLED INTO / B2–C1 LEVEL / PHRASING AND GRAMMAR SLIPS
DYLD IN-too | BEE-TWO to SEE-WUN Phrases — no single stress pattern
British (RP): /daɪəld ˈɪntuː/
⚠️ British spelling: 'dialled' (double-L) ✅ | American: 'dialed'. 'Dialled into' = calibrated to, adjusted for — informal but widely used in language teaching. 'B2–C1' = upper-intermediate to advanced on the CEFR scale. 'Phrasing slips' = slightly unnatural word choices. 'Grammar slips' = minor grammatical errors. OET candidates typically perform at B2–C1 — understanding this helps with self-assessment. Note: OET Grade B ≈ CEFR B2+.
Example 1
The tutor provided a rewrite dialled into B2–C1 level — natural enough to be credible, but with subtle phrasing and grammar slips reflecting the student's current proficiency.
Example 2
Awareness of your own recurring grammar slips — such as missing articles or incorrect prepositions — is the first step toward eliminating them under exam conditions.
📌 CEFR levels — OET equivalence:
A1–A2 = Beginner | B1 = Intermediate | B2 = Upper-intermediate (≈ OET Grade C+) | C1 = Advanced (≈ OET Grade B) | C2 = Proficiency (≈ OET Grade A) | OET target: Grade B = C1 level
WORD
VALIDATING (PATIENT CONCERNS / RESEARCH)
VAL-ih-day-ting 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈvælɪdeɪtɪŋ/
⚠️ Stress on VAL (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'val-IH-day-ting' or 'val-ih-DAY-ting'. Two key uses: (1) OET communication: validating a patient's concerns = acknowledging their feelings as real and understandable BEFORE giving information — a core OET marking criterion. Skipping validation = automatic penalty. (2) Research: validating a tool/questionnaire = confirming it measures what it claims to measure. NEVER move straight to information-giving without validating first.
Example 1
The pharmacist began by validating the patient's concerns — 'I completely understand why this feels frightening' — before moving on to explain the medication.
Example 2
Validating the questionnaire involved testing it with a sample population and comparing the results against an established gold-standard measure.
📌 OET validation — do this BEFORE anything else:
(1) Validate: 'I can understand why you're worried about that' | (2) Normalise: 'Many patients feel the same way' | (3) THEN give information | ❌ Never: 'Don't worry, it's fine' (dismissive) | ❌ Never: jump straight into facts without acknowledging the emotion
WORD
INTEGRATIVE
IN-teh-gruh-tiv 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈɪntɪɡrətɪv/
⚠️ Stress on IN (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'in-TEH-gruh-tiv' or 'in-teh-GRAY-tiv'. Integrative = combining or unifying different elements into a coherent whole. Three key contexts: (1) Research: 'integrative review' = synthesises both quantitative and qualitative studies. (2) MBA: 'integrative thinking' = holding opposing ideas and finding creative resolution (Roger Martin). (3) Clinical: 'integrative medicine' = combining conventional and complementary approaches.
Example 1
The team conducted an integrative review of the literature — synthesising findings from randomised controlled trials and qualitative patient experience studies to inform the new guidelines.
Example 2
Integrative thinking allowed the project team to reconcile the competing demands of cost reduction and quality improvement — finding a solution that achieved both.
📌 Integrative vs related terms:
integrative → combining into a whole (integrative review, integrative thinking) | integrated → already combined (integrated care, integrated project) | integral → essential, fundamental ('an integral part of') | integrity → moral uprightness / structural completeness
WORD
PRONE TO (SIDE EFFECTS / COMPLICATIONS)
PROHN too 2 syllables
British (RP): /prəʊn tuː/
⚠️ Stress on PROHN (1st word). 'Prone' rhymes with 'phone', 'bone', 'tone'. Do NOT say 'PRON' (short vowel). 'Prone to X' = having a characteristic tendency or vulnerability toward something undesirable. Clinical OET: 'prone to infections', 'prone to falls', 'prone to side effects'. Distinct from 'likely to' (neutral probability) — 'prone to' implies an inherent predisposition. Use 'prone to' + noun or gerund: 'prone to bleeding' ✅ | 'prone to bleed' ❌.
Example 1
Patients taking long-term corticosteroids are prone to infections — the pharmacist advised her to seek medical attention promptly if she developed any signs of illness.
Example 2
Elderly patients are particularly prone to falls when starting antihypertensive medication — the pharmacist advised rising slowly from a seated position and using support if needed.
📌 'Prone to' — clinical uses for OET:
prone to infections (immunosuppressed) | prone to falls (elderly + antihypertensives) | prone to sunburn (amiodarone, doxycycline) | prone to bleeding (anticoagulants) | prone to hypoglycaemia (insulin, sulphonylureas) | prone to constipation (opioids, iron)
WORD
PEER REVIEW (학술 논문 심사 / 동료 평가)
PEER reh-VYOO 3 syllables
British (RP): /pɪər rɪˈvjuː/
⚠️ 'Review' — stress on VYOO (2nd syllable): reh-VYOO. Do NOT say 'REE-vyoo'. Peer review = evaluation of submitted academic work by independent experts in the same field before publication — ensures quality, validity, and rigour. 'Peer-reviewed' = adjective: 'a peer-reviewed journal', 'a peer-reviewed study'. OET/MBA: citing peer-reviewed evidence carries far more credibility than anecdotal or unpublished data. KEY DISTINCTION: peer review ≠ peer comparison (see separate card).
Example 1
The pharmacist cited a peer-reviewed study published in The Lancet — peer-reviewed evidence carries significantly more weight than anecdotal reports or unpublished data.
Example 2
All articles in this journal undergo rigorous peer review — submissions are evaluated by at least two independent experts before a publication decision is made.
📌 Peer review vs Peer comparison — do NOT confuse:
PEER REVIEW (학술 심사) → academic evaluation before publication | credibility marker in research | 'peer-reviewed journal' ✅ || PEER COMPARISON / BENCHMARKING (재무 비율 비교) → financial performance vs industry peers | used in MBA finance, valuation, credit analysis
WORD
PEER COMPARISON / BENCHMARKING (재무 비율 비교)
BENCH-mar-king | PEER kum-PA-rih-sun benchmarking = 3 | peer comparison = 5
British (RP): /ˈbɛntʃmɑːkɪŋ/ | /pɪər kəmˈpærɪsən/
⚠️ 'Benchmarking' — stress on BENCH (1st syllable). 'Comparison' — stress on PA: kum-PA-rih-sun (4 syllables). Peer comparison = comparing a company's financial ratios or KPIs against similar companies in the same industry (재무 비율 비교). Benchmarking = broader process of measuring performance against a standard or best-in-class. MBA finance: peer comparison is central to comparable company analysis ('comps') — used in valuation, credit analysis, and strategic review. Example metrics: CCC, ROE, EBITDA margins, debt ratios.
Example 1
The analyst conducted a peer comparison of the company's CCC against similar franchise operators — benchmarking revealed inventory turnover was significantly slower than the industry average.
Example 2
Benchmarking against industry peers identified two areas of underperformance — the board used these findings to set measurable improvement targets for the following financial year.
📌 Benchmarking vs Peer Comparison — MBA distinction:
PEER COMPARISON → same industry, similar companies, financial ratios (e.g. CCC, ROE, P/E) | BENCHMARKING → broader, can compare against best-in-class or internal targets | Both used in: valuation (comps) | credit analysis | strategic performance review | KPI setting
WORD
STARK ILLUSTRATION
STARK il-us-TRAY-shun 5 syllables
British (RP): /stɑːk ˌɪləˈstreɪʃən/
⚠️ 'Stark' — rhymes with 'dark', 'park', 'mark'. 'Illustration' — stress on TRAY: il-us-TRAY-shun (4 syllables). 'A stark illustration' = a striking, clear, and often confronting example that makes a point unmistakably obvious. MBA/academic writing: use to introduce a powerful, dramatic example. Reserve for genuinely striking data points — overuse weakens the impact. Related: 'a stark reminder', 'a stark contrast', 'starkly different'.
Example 1
The 40% drop in foot traffic during the first lockdown is a stark illustration of the vulnerability of location-dependent franchise models to external shocks.
Example 2
The gap between franchisee attrition rates and new store openings provides a stark illustration of the underlying health of the franchise network.
📌 'Stark' collocations — MBA writing:
a stark illustration of | a stark contrast between | a stark reminder that | starkly different | the stark reality is that | stands in stark contrast to
WORD
DELIBERATELY
deh-LIB-ur-ut-lee 5 syllables
British (RP): /dɪˈlɪbərətli/
⚠️ Stress on LIB (2nd syllable). Do NOT say 'DEL-ih-bur-ut-lee' or 'deh-lib-ur-AT-lee'. 'Deliberately' = done consciously, intentionally, and with full awareness. Distinct from: 'purposefully' (with determination and direction) | 'intentionally' (simply 'on purpose') | 'strategically' (with a specific goal in mind). MBA: 'deliberately structured', 'deliberately priced'. OET: 'the patient deliberately avoided taking the medication' — implies a conscious, informed choice.
Example 1
The franchise model was deliberately structured to shift operational risk to franchisees while retaining royalty income at the corporate level.
Example 2
The pricing strategy was deliberately set below market rate during the launch phase to accelerate market penetration before moving to a premium positioning.
📌 Deliberately vs related adverbs — MBA distinction:
deliberately → consciously, intentionally (focus: it was a choice) | purposefully → with determination and direction (focus: moving toward a goal) | strategically → with a specific long-term objective (focus: the plan) | intentionally → simply 'on purpose' (neutral, less emphasis)
WORD
INCREMENTAL STORE / NEAR-PURE ROYALTY INCOME / MINIMAL CENTRAL OVERHEADS
in-KREE-men-tul | ROY-ul-tee | OH-vur-hedz incremental = 4 | royalty = 3 | overheads = 3
British (RP): /ɪnˈkriːməntəl/ | /ˈrɔɪəlti/ | /ˈəʊvəhɛdz/
⚠️ 'Incremental' — stress on KREE: in-KREE-men-tul (4 syllables). 'Royalty' — stress on ROY: ROY-ul-tee (3 syllables). 'Overheads' — stress on OH: OH-vur-hedz. Core franchise financial concept: each additional (incremental) store generates royalty revenue (typically % of sales) with almost no additional cost to the franchisor — because the franchisee bears all operating costs. This is the engine of margin expansion at scale. 'Near-pure' = almost entirely profit, very few associated costs.
Example 1
Each incremental store adds near-pure royalty income with minimal additional central overheads — this scalability drives the superior margin profile of the franchise model.
Example 2
At scale, incremental royalty income from new stores flows almost directly to EBITDA — central overhead costs are largely fixed and do not grow proportionally with the store count.
📌 Franchise operating leverage — MBA model key insight:
Royalty income = % of franchisee sales (e.g. 5–8%) | Franchisee bears: rent, staff, COGS, equipment | Franchisor bears: brand, systems, support (largely fixed) | Result: each new store → near-pure margin contribution → high operating leverage → margin expansion at scale
WORD
SYMMETRICAL / ASYMMETRICAL
sim-ET-rih-kul | ay-sim-ET-rih-kul 4 / 5 syllables
British (RP): /sɪˈmɛtrɪkəl/ | /eɪsɪˈmɛtrɪkəl/
⚠️ Both: stress on ET (2nd syllable of 'metric'). Do NOT say 'SIM-eh-trih-kul' or 'SIM-et-RIK-ul'. Symmetrical = equal and balanced on both sides. Asymmetrical = unequal, one side larger or different. MBA/finance: 'symmetrical risk' (upside = downside), 'information asymmetry' (one party has more information than the other — a market failure concept). 'Asymmetric payoff' = downside is limited but upside is large — often the desired investment profile.
Example 1
The risk profile was not symmetrical — the downside was capped at the initial outlay, while the upside was theoretically unlimited.
Example 2
Information asymmetry between the franchisor and prospective franchisee creates a risk that the franchisee may not fully understand the obligations they are entering into.
📌 Symmetry vocabulary — MBA finance/strategy:
symmetrical risk → equal upside and downside | asymmetric payoff → limited downside, large upside (desirable) | information asymmetry → one party knows more than the other | symmetric information → both parties equally informed (theoretical market efficiency)
WORD
CONVERSELY
KON-vurs-lee 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈkɒnvɜːsli/
⚠️ Stress on KON (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'kon-VURS-lee'. 'Conversely' = introducing a direct logical reversal of the previous statement. Used as a sentence adverb — followed by a comma. Distinct from: 'however' (general contrast, softer) | 'on the other hand' (balanced comparison of two options) | 'in contrast' (highlighting a difference). 'Conversely' specifically signals the opposite is true — the strongest reversal connector.
Example 1
Company-owned stores require significant capital expenditure and carry full operational risk; conversely, franchise stores generate royalty income with negligible incremental cost to the franchisor.
Example 2
Higher store density increases brand visibility and customer convenience; conversely, it can lead to cannibalisation of sales between nearby locations.
📌 Contrast connectors — MBA writing register:
conversely → direct logical reversal (strongest) | however → general contrast (most versatile) | on the other hand → balanced two-sided comparison | in contrast → highlights a specific difference | nevertheless → contrast despite a concession | that said → softer contrast, often spoken
WORD
TRAJECTORY
truh-JEK-tuh-ree 4 syllables
British (RP): /trəˈdʒɛktəri/
⚠️ Stress on JEK (2nd syllable). Do NOT say 'TRAJ-ek-tor-ee' or 'truh-jek-TOR-ee'. 'Trajectory' = the path or course something follows over time — originally physics (path of a projectile), now widely used in MBA and clinical contexts. MBA: 'growth trajectory', 'earnings trajectory', 'store opening trajectory'. OET: 'the trajectory of the patient's recovery', 'disease trajectory'. Implies directional movement over time — upward, downward, or flattening.
Example 1
The company's store opening trajectory — averaging 12 new locations per year — suggests royalty income will continue to expand significantly over the forecast period.
Example 2
The patient's recovery trajectory was encouraging — steady improvement over the first two weeks suggested the antibiotic course was effective.
📌 'Trajectory' collocations — MBA & OET:
MBA: growth trajectory | earnings trajectory | store opening trajectory | share price trajectory | OET: recovery trajectory | disease trajectory | treatment trajectory | deteriorating trajectory (red flag) | upward / downward / flattening trajectory
WORD
PROXIES / PROXY (FINANCIAL PROXIES)
PROK-seez 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈprɒksɪz/
⚠️ Stress on PROK (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'prok-SEEZ'. Proxy = an indirect measure used as a substitute for something that cannot be measured directly. MBA/finance: 'store count as a proxy for market penetration', 'same-store sales as a proxy for brand health'. Always acknowledge the limitation — a proxy is an approximation. Distinct from: indicators (broader signals) | measures (direct quantifications) | benchmarks (comparison standards).
Example 1
Store count and same-store sales growth were used as proxies for overall franchise network health, given the limited availability of individual franchisee financial data.
Example 2
EBITDA is often used as a proxy for operating cash flow, though it excludes changes in working capital and capital expenditure.
📌 Proxies vs Indicators vs Measures — MBA distinction:
PROXY → indirect substitute for something unmeasurable | INDICATOR → a signal suggesting direction or trend | MEASURE → a direct quantification of something specific | BENCHMARK → a comparison standard or reference point
WORD
INDICATORS (LEADING VS LAGGING)
IN-dih-kay-turz 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈɪndɪkeɪtəz/
⚠️ Stress on IN (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'in-DIH-kay-turz'. Indicators = measures used to assess performance, health, or progress. KEY DISTINCTION: leading indicators = predict future performance (e.g. new store pipeline, customer satisfaction); lagging indicators = confirm past performance (e.g. revenue, net profit). KPIs = key performance indicators. OET: clinical indicators of deterioration (rising temperature, falling blood pressure, confusion).
Example 1
New franchise agreements signed is a leading indicator of future royalty income, while reported EBITDA is a lagging indicator of historical performance.
Example 2
Clinical indicators of deterioration — including a rising heart rate, falling blood pressure, and increasing confusion — prompted the team to escalate the patient's care.
📌 Leading vs Lagging indicators:
LEADING (predictive): new orders | customer satisfaction | staff engagement | store pipeline | R&D spend || LAGGING (confirmatory): revenue | profit | market share | readmission rates | EBITDA
WORD
MEASURES (FINANCIAL / CLINICAL)
MEH-zhurz 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈmɛʒəz/
⚠️ Stress on MEH (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'meh-ZHURZ'. 'Measures' = specific quantifiable variables used to assess performance or outcomes. Distinct from proxies (indirect substitutes) and indicators (signals). MBA: 'financial measures such as EBITDA, CCC, and ROCE'. OET: 'clinical measures such as blood pressure, HbA1c, and eGFR'. Also a verb: 'this metric measures operational efficiency'. Also means 'actions taken': 'precautionary measures'.
Example 1
The three key financial measures used in this analysis are CCC, ROCE, and EBITDA margin — each captures a different dimension of performance.
Example 2
Blood pressure and HbA1c are two of the most important clinical measures for monitoring cardiovascular and diabetic risk in pharmacy practice.
WORD
PROJECTED TO WORSEN / OPERATING CASH FLOW / SHORTFALL
proh-JEK-tid | OP-ur-ay-ting KASH floh | SHORT-fawl projected = 3 | operating = 4 | shortfall = 2
British (RP): /prəˈdʒɛktɪd/ | /ˈɒpəreɪtɪŋ kæʃ fləʊ/ | /ˈʃɔːtfɔːl/
⚠️ 'Projected' — stress on JEK. 'Operating' — stress on OP. 'Shortfall' — stress on SHORT. 'Projected to worsen' = forecast to deteriorate based on modelling. 'Operating cash flow' = cash from core business operations (before investment/financing). Negative operating cash flow = cash outflow — a red flag. 'Shortfall' = gap between actual/forecast and target/required. IMPORTANT phrasing: 'projected to worsen to approximately negative NZ$600,000' ✅
Example 1
Scenario A's aggressive expansion is projected to worsen the operating cash flow to approximately negative NZ$600,000 — driven by capital requirements outpacing royalty income.
Example 2
The projected shortfall of NZ$600,000 would require drawing on existing credit facilities or seeking additional equity funding.
📌 Financial forecast language — MBA writing:
is projected to → formal forecast | is expected to → slightly less certain | is estimated to → based on approximation | is forecast to → based on modelling | 'worsen to' → deteriorate toward a specific figure | 'improve to' → recover toward a target
WORD
VERDICT
VUR-dikt 2 syllables
British (RP): /ˈvɜːdɪkt/
⚠️ Stress on VUR (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'vur-DIKT'. Originally a legal term — now used in MBA and business writing to introduce a final, decisive recommendation. More punchy and authoritative than 'conclusion'. Best used in executive summaries and board presentations where a clear recommendation is expected. Structure: analysis → options → verdict. OET: rarely used clinically but may appear in listening passages.
Example 1
The verdict is clear: Scenario B's measured expansion approach preserves cash flow flexibility while delivering meaningful royalty income growth.
Example 2
After weighing the financial projections and risk profiles of both scenarios, the verdict favours Scenario B on the grounds of sustainability and lower execution risk.
📌 Report conclusion language — MBA register:
Punchy: The verdict | The recommendation | In summary || Formal: In conclusion | On balance | Taking all factors into consideration || Hedged: On balance, the evidence suggests | The analysis points toward | The preferred option appears to be
WORD
MATERIALLY
muh-TEER-ee-ul-ee 5 syllables
British (RP): /məˈtɪəriəli/
⚠️ Stress on TEER (2nd syllable). Do NOT say 'muh-TEER-ee-AL-ee' or 'MAT-er-ee-ul-ee'. 'Materially' = to a significant or meaningful extent — enough to make a real difference. In accounting and law: 'material' = significant enough to influence a decision. Stronger than 'somewhat' or 'slightly', less absolute than 'fundamentally' or 'entirely'. MBA: 'materially worse', 'materially impact earnings'. OET: 'materially impair quality of life'.
Example 1
Scenario A's cash flow position is materially worse than Scenario B's — the NZ$600,000 shortfall represents a meaningful risk to short-term financial stability.
Example 2
A delay of six months in the store rollout programme would not materially affect the five-year revenue projections.
📌 Degree adverbs — MBA writing strength scale:
marginally → very small change | slightly → small | somewhat → moderate | materially → significant, decision-relevant | substantially → large | fundamentally → core, structural | entirely → complete
WORD
RESTING ON (SCENARIO B IS RESTING ON...)
REST-ing on 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈrɛstɪŋ ɒn/
⚠️ 'Resting on' = depending on, relying on, contingent upon — used when a scenario or argument depends on specific assumptions holding true. MBA analytical writing: identifying what a scenario 'rests on' exposes its key assumptions and vulnerabilities. If those assumptions fail, the scenario fails. This is a critical step in sensitivity analysis. 'Scenario B is resting on X' = 'Scenario B's viability is contingent on X being true.'
Example 1
Scenario B is resting on the assumption that new stores will generate positive cash flow within 18 months — if this extends to 24 months, the projections change materially.
Example 2
The entire bull case is resting on the assumption that royalty rates remain stable — any renegotiation with franchisees would significantly alter the financial model.
📌 Assumption language — MBA sensitivity analysis:
resting on | contingent on | predicated on | premised on | dependent on the assumption that | subject to the condition that | this holds only if...
WORD
CORDIAL
KOR-dee-ul 3 syllables
British (RP): /ˈkɔːdiəl/
⚠️ Stress on KOR (1st syllable). Do NOT say 'kor-DEE-ul'. 'Cordial' = warm, friendly, and polite — creating a pleasant, comfortable atmosphere. OET: a cordial manner builds rapport and trust with patients. Distinct from: formal (correct but not warm) | casual (too relaxed for clinical settings) | friendly (slightly less professional). The OET Speaking sub-test rewards candidates who create a genuinely cordial interaction.
Example 1
The pharmacist maintained a cordial and professional manner throughout — greeting the patient warmly, using her name, and checking understanding at each stage.
Example 2
A cordial opening establishes the tone for the entire consultation — 'Hello Mrs Kim, lovely to see you again. How have you been getting on?'
📌 OET rapport-building — opening moves:
greet by name | warm, cordial tone | acknowledge waiting time | open question to start: 'How can I help you today?' | avoid jumping straight to clinical questions
WORD
GUMS / GINGIVA / GUM DISEASE
GUMZ | JIN-jih-vuh | jin-jih-VY-tis 1 / 3 / 4 syllables
British (RP): /ɡʌmz/ | /ˈdʒɪndʒɪvə/ | /ˌdʒɪndʒɪˈvaɪtɪs/
⚠️ 'Gingiva' — stress on JIN: JIN-jih-vuh (3 syllables). 'Gingivitis' — stress on VY: jin-jih-VY-tis (4 syllables). Patient term: gums. Clinical: gingiva / gingival tissue. Gum disease stages: gingivitis (early, reversible — red, swollen, bleeding gums) → periodontitis (advanced — bone loss, tooth mobility). Pharmacist relevance: drug-induced gingival overgrowth — phenytoin, ciclosporin, amlodipine. OET: 'my gums bleed when I brush' = possible gingivitis → refer to dentist.
Example 1
The patient mentioned her gums bled every time she brushed — the pharmacist asked how long this had been happening and recommended she see a dentist, as this can be an early sign of gum disease.
Example 2
Amlodipine can cause gingival overgrowth in some patients — the pharmacist advised her to maintain excellent oral hygiene and attend regular dental check-ups.
📌 Drug-induced gingival overgrowth — OET must know:
phenytoin (anti-epileptic) | ciclosporin (immunosuppressant) | amlodipine (calcium channel blocker) → all can cause gingival overgrowth | counsel: excellent oral hygiene + regular dental review
WORD
ROOT CAUSE (ANALYSIS)
ROOT KAWZ 2 syllables
British (RP): /ruːt kɔːz/
⚠️ 'Root' rhymes with 'boot', 'loot', 'suit'. 'Cause' = KAWZ (rhymes with 'paws'). Root cause = the fundamental underlying reason for a problem — not a surface symptom. MBA: root cause analysis (RCA) uses tools like '5 Whys' and the fishbone/Ishikawa diagram. OET: 'We need to find the root cause of your recurring infections rather than just treating each episode' — distinguishes symptomatic treatment from treating the underlying cause.
Example 1
The pharmacist explained that the recurring mouth ulcers might be a symptom — she recommended seeing a GP to investigate whether a nutritional deficiency was the root cause.
Example 2
Root cause analysis of the dispensing error revealed a systemic labelling issue rather than individual negligence — the entire process was subsequently redesigned.
📌 Root cause analysis tools — MBA/quality improvement:
5 Whys → ask 'why?' five times to drill to root cause | Fishbone diagram (Ishikawa) → map causes across categories: People, Process, Equipment, Environment, Materials, Management | Pareto analysis → 80/20 rule — find the 20% of causes driving 80% of problems
WORD
IMPOLITE / DISCREET VS DISCRETE
im-puh-LYT | dis-KREET impolite = 3 | discreet = 2
British (RP): /ˌɪmpəˈlaɪt/ | /dɪˈskriːt/
⚠️ 'Impolite' — stress on LYT (3rd syllable). 'Discreet' — stress on KREET. CRITICAL SPELLING: discreet (careful, private) ≠ discrete (separate, distinct) — same pronunciation, completely different meanings. 'Be more discreet' = handle more privately and sensitively. OET: impolite communication is penalised — includes interrupting, dismissing concerns, condescending tone, discussing sensitive medication loudly. Always move to a private space for sensitive counselling.
Example 1
The pharmacist was careful not to sound impolite when asking about adherence — she framed it as 'How have you been getting on with the tablets?' rather than 'Have you been taking them properly?'
Example 2
The pharmacist made a note to be more discreet when discussing sensitive medications — moving to a consultation room rather than speaking across the open counter.
📌 discreet vs discrete — never confuse these:
discreet → careful, private, tactful ('handle it discreetly') | discrete → separate, individually distinct ('three discrete components') | both pronounced: dis-KREET
WORD
GET THE HANG OF IT (IDIOM)
get thuh HANG uv IT 5 syllables
British (RP): /ɡɛt ðə hæŋ əv ɪt/
⚠️ 'Get the hang of it' = informal idiom meaning to learn how to do something and become comfortable with it. OET: patients may say 'I can't get the hang of this inhaler' — recognise this as a technique problem and offer to demonstrate. Use it yourself to normalise learning: 'Most patients find it tricky at first, but you'll get the hang of it with a bit of practice.' Builds rapport and reduces patient anxiety about new techniques.
Example 1
The patient said she couldn't get the hang of the inhaler — the pharmacist offered to demonstrate step by step and asked her to practise in the pharmacy.
Example 2
'Don't worry — most people find the pen injector tricky at first. You'll get the hang of it within a few days, especially once it becomes part of your daily routine.'
📌 Related idioms for learning/adapting — OET patient language:
get the hang of it → learn a technique | get used to it → adapt over time | figure it out → work out how to do it | get the knack of it → master a skill | trial and error → learning by trying
WORD
CONVERSE / BE ABLE TO CONVERSE WITH
verb: kun-VURS | noun: KON-vurs 2 syllables
British (RP): verb /kənˈvɜːs/ | noun /ˈkɒnvɜːs/
⚠️ STRESS SHIFT: verb = kun-VURS (stress on 2nd syllable) | noun = KON-vurs (stress on 1st). These are completely different words: converse (verb) = to have a conversation ✅ | the converse (noun) = the opposite ✅. Do NOT confuse. OET: 'I was able to converse with the patient using plain language.' More formal than 'talk' or 'chat'. 'Be able to converse' = have the capacity to communicate effectively.
Example 1
The pharmacist was able to converse with the patient in clear, simple language — avoiding jargon and checking understanding at each step.
Example 2
Despite the language barrier, the pharmacist was able to converse with the patient using visual aids and short, simple sentences.
📌 Noun/verb stress shift — converse and others:
CON-verse (n) = the opposite | con-VERSE (v) = to talk | similarly: CON-tent (n) vs con-TENT (adj) | PRO-test (n) vs pro-TEST (v) | IN-crease (n) vs in-CREASE (v)
WORD
ACCORDING TO YOUR SYMPTOMS / BASED ON WHAT YOU'VE DESCRIBED
AK-kor-ding too | BAYST on wot yoov dih-SKRYBD Phrases — variable
British (RP): /əˈkɔːdɪŋ tuː/ | /beɪst ɒn wɒt juːv dɪˈskraɪbd/
⚠️ OET speaking register: 'Based on what you've described' ✅ (natural, conversational, empathetic) | 'From what you're telling me' ✅ | 'According to your symptoms' ⚠️ (slightly textbook/formal — acceptable but less natural in OET speaking). Always follow with a clinical statement, then a referral or action: 'Based on what you've described, this sounds like it could be acid reflux — I'd recommend seeing your GP for a formal assessment.'
Example 1
Based on what you've described — the burning sensation after meals and the sour taste — this sounds consistent with acid reflux; I'd recommend seeing your GP.
Example 2
From what you're telling me, it sounds like the symptoms started after you began the new medication — let me check if this is a known side effect.
📌 OET clinical linking phrases — naturalness ranking:
✅ Most natural: 'Based on what you've described...' | 'From what you're telling me...' | 'It sounds like...' | ⚠️ More formal: 'According to your symptoms...' | 'In light of what you've said...' | ❌ Avoid: 'Your symptoms indicate that...'
WORD
IMPROV ACTOR / IMPROV MINDSET (OET SPEAKING)
IM-prov AK-tur 4 syllables
British (RP): /ˈɪmprɒv ˈæktər/
⚠️ 'Improv' = short for improvisation — IM-prov (stress on IM). An improv actor responds spontaneously to unexpected input without a script. Core improv principle: 'Yes, and...' — accept what's offered and build on it, rather than blocking or freezing. OET Speaking application: when a patient says something unexpected, don't freeze or restart — accept it and respond naturally. Bay's coaching focus: push through errors without stopping. Stopping to self-correct is the enemy of fluency.
Example 1
Approaching the OET roleplay with an improv mindset — accepting whatever the patient says and building on it naturally — prevents the awkward pauses that come from trying to self-correct mid-sentence.
Example 2
'Yes, and...' is the improv actor's golden rule — in OET, this means: acknowledge what the patient says, then move forward. Never block. Never freeze. Just respond.
📌 Improv principles applied to OET Speaking:
'Yes, and...' → accept + build | Don't block → never 'no, that's wrong' | Push through errors → fluency > accuracy in OET speaking | Listen actively → your next move depends on what the patient just said | Stay in character → you are the pharmacist, always
